The Red Page
HEAVEN.
Fish (fly-replete, in depth of June,
Dawdling away their watery noon)
Fonder deep wisdom, dark or clear,
Each secret fishy hope or fear.
Fish say, they have their Stream and Pond,
But is there anything Beyond$
This life cannot be All, they swear,
For how unpleasant if it were!
One may not doubt that, somehow, Good
Shall come of Water and of Mud;
And, sure, the reverent eye must see
A purpose in Liquidity.
We darkly know, by Faith we cry,
The future is not Wholly Dry.
Mud unto mud! —Death eddies near —
Not here the appointed End, not here!
But somewhere, beyond Space and Time,
Is wetter water, slimier slime!
And there (they trust) there swimmeth One
Who swam ere rivers were begun,
Immense, of fishy form and mind,
Squamous, omnipotent, and kind;
And under that Almighty Fin
The littlest fish may enter in.
Oh! never fly conceals a hook,
Fish say, in the Eternal Brook,
But more than mundane weeds are there,
And mud celestially fair;
For caterpillars drift around.
And Paradisal grubs are found;
Unfading moths, immortal flies,
And the worm that never dies.
And in that Heaven of their wish,
There shall be no more land, say fish.
—Rupert Brooke, in New Numbers.
Bill, the Bounder
Acute observers like Price Collier and
other recent writers about Germany have
noted that the Prussian has so compara-
tively lately reached nationhood that in
many respects the race in its boorish de-
meanor and brutal, undisciplined methods
shows all the signs of bumptious youth.
The Prussian is bumptious because he
is not at all sure that he is the equal of
other races. He has to assert himself be-
cause he, alone among modern nations, con-
siders it necessary to assure the world that
he is properly civilised. It is just that
sneaking fear that his manners are not
quite gentlemanly that makes him an over-
bearing bounder. The German has to for-
mulate his conception of a gentleman: we
do not need to.
It is this inherent defect in the German
character, the inability to recognise that,
bluntly, honesty is the best policy, that
Germany and Good Faith, by E. H. C.
Olipliant, a study of the History of the
Prussian Royal Family (Critchley Parker,
Melb., 3s. 6d.) trenchantly exposes. Oli-
phant shows that Germany’s conduct in the
events leading up to the present war, her
treachery and faithlessness, are precisely on
a par with her behavior towards other
nations during 300 years. She is merely
carrying out, with the blunders inevitable
in the weak disciple of a great man, the
brutal, dishonest methods of Bismarck.
Justly, the author notes that the charac-
teristics of the Hohenzollerns are not the
characteristics of the people of Germany as
a whole, though in many cases they are the
characteristics of the Prussian people. Yet
even the brutal and offensive Prussian, hated
by the other races of the German Empire,
has not got all the faults of treachery and
faithfulness that mark the Hohenzollern
family.
-It is not against the German people that
the British Empire has any reason to feel
animosity: it is against the rulers of Ger-
many, against its reptile press, against its
untruthful historians, against its hatred-
working University professors, against its
brutal military class, against its offensive
officialdom, and against its treacherous Im-
perial Family.
Jt
The Hohenzollerns are a family of ban-
dits. They first emerged in history as feudal
lords who lived by brigandage in the Suabian
Alps. But in this they merely resembled
their contemporaries. “What distinguishes
them from the others is that they have
never dropped their old characteristics.” It
is a pretty record that history writes of
this family. There was the first to obtain
independence (in East Prussia), Frederick
William, the “Great Elector,” the creator
of the Hohenzollern absolutism, an unscrupu-
lous' and faithless statesman and bully, im-
pressing on all the doctrine that might is
right, the founder of the army. There was
Frederick William 1., the “archtype ofßran-
dernburg bully,” a “low-minded, vicious,
vulgar swaggerer.” He was the king that
had at work all over Europe recruiting offi-
cers and official kidnappers of tall men for
his famous regiment of tall grenadiers. And
not only men. He started a breeding farm
for high grenadiers. “Wherever a stalwart
peasant woman, married or unmarried, or a
farmer’s big-boned wife or daughter, was
found, she was seized and transported to
Potsdam, and mated with a giant grena-
dier.” And there were others, each possess-
ing the treacherous rapacity of the bully that
now, in its latest member, has hurled itself,
in blind meglomania, upon the fair facade
of civilisation. Reading the long record of
treachery to ally and neutral, one wonders
how other nations could have put faith in
Prussia again. Yet, not two months ago,
there were believers in Prussian honesty
even in the British Cabinet. Yet the lying
that Bismarck found necessary to bring
about the Franco-P-russian war had been
naively admitted by that shameless old
trickster.
As a liar Bismarck has never had a
superior; but it is well to remember that his
methods are those still in vogue in Prussia,
that they are regarded with general approval,
and that the highest aim of every Prussian
statesman is to effect Bismarckian coups in
the Bismarckian way; and yet there were
yesterday, probably are to-day, and assuredly
will be to-morrow, English statesmen and
English newspaper writers who do not recog-
nise the distinction between truth and honor
and honesty on the one hand and Prussian
morality on the other!
The brutality of Prussian military
methods, which have shocked the civilised
world within the last month, might have
been foreseen. The author reminds us of
the Kaiser’s bloodthirsty instructions to the
German troops prior to their despatch to
take part in the joint expedition against
Pekin.
“No quarter will be given, no prisoners
taken. All who fall into your hands shall
be at your mercy. Just as the Huns 1000
years ago, under the leadership of Attila,
gained the reputation for sternness in Virtue
of which they still live, so may the name of
Germany become known in such a way in
China that no Chinaman hereafter may dare
so much as to look askance at a German.”
How well his soldiers carried out his in-
structions in defenceless China has been well
attested by independent observers. Now
Belgium can add her sad testimony of the
brutality of the mailed fist.
Jt
William’s colossal vanity, strikingly shown
by his dismissal of Bismarck, has led him
into a long record of diplomatic failures.
He has tried to bring about hostile coali-
tions of the. Powers of Europe against Eng-
land, the United States and Japan (not
jointly, but separately—each in turn), and
has not succeeded in effecting a single one
of them ; he has sought to bring France and
England to blows, and has succeeded in
bringing about an entente between them;
he has sought to shatter the entente, and
has only united it more firmly; his greatest
object of all has been to incite Russia and
England to war, and he has had the morti-
fication of seeing them hand in hand; he
has endeavored to foment ill-feeling between
England and the United States, and sees
them more friendly than they have ever
been before; he has thirsted to defy the
Monroe doctrine, and has had to acknow-
ledge his complete acceptance of it; he has
flattered and cajoled the American people
to the utmost, without achieving any very
definite gain ; he has given plentiful assur-
ances of friendliness to the people of Eng-
land, and those assurances have not been
believed (for very good reasons).
The author’s trenchant summing up:—
The Hohenzollerns occupy a place by
themselves among the royal houses of
Europe. They are not disgraced by the
callous cruelty of the Romanoffs, the farm-
yard morals of the Bourbons, or the religious
bigotry in which these dispute pre-eminence
with the unlearning Hapsburgs; but in un-
faithfulness, in low cunning, and in the
arts of the sharper they stand alone and
unapproachable. In ingratitude they are
an excellent second to the Hapsburgs; in
cold-blooded treachery not even the Bour-
bons are their equals. If, of all these
families, the Bourbons have been on the
whole the basest and most degraded, the
Hohenzollerns have assuredly been the un-
loveliest and the trickiest; and, unfortu-
nately for the character of the German
people, they are asked to admire their im-
perial line for those very qualities which
make them hateful.
August
The responsibility for the small entry —26
—for the August sonnet competition, must
be referred to Bill Hohenzollern. Despite
our suggestion that the war should
not be allowed to intrude into this pacificist
series, three of the entrants could not help
themselves. Two excellent war sonnets may,
however, find place in another part of this
publication. Good sonnets were submitted
by W. Calm, Julianna, Emo, Mary A. K.
Robb and Charles F. Pettinger. In the
humorous class P. Phrozeout and Mick Much
reach publication:—
Six sonnets qualified for the final heat. A
complicated allegory spoiled a sonnet by L.
J. Villiers, despite a fine conclusion. And
if Lilia Gormhuille McKay had kept to the
level of her sextette !
Upon the long, green hills the gorse is gold,
With shining mists the mountain-spurs are
hung;
A-dotvn the valleys silver showerets pass
To gleaming sands, where sea-tvinds still
blow cold;
And through the night each gleeful star has
flung
Its petalled likeness on the meadow grass.
A. Gore-Jones spoils a fine sonnet by a line
of prose:—
... .Upon the busy rivers glide
The cane punts in a swiftly darkening tide,
Bearing the staple for some sugar mill.
Two names well known in these competi-
tions dispute the prize: S. Elliott Napier and
Nina Murdoch. Napier’s sonnet is literary,
using the vaguer general term instead of
the more vivid particular name. Nina Mur-
doch (77 Ben Boyd-road, Neutral Bay, Syd-
ney), though brazenly adapting for Aus-
tralian use a hackneyed line of Tennyson,
wins by her brilliant Australian color.
The spendthrift wattles wave each yellow
tress,
And lavish largesse on the wanton bees,
Who, golden-thighed with all their fragrant
fees,
Yield to the nectar'd blooms their bold caress.
The mating birds, decked in their nuptial
dress,
Keep tryst amid the secret, shady trees,
And upward to the light and to the breeze
The green-lanced armies of the icheatfield
press.
For this is now the August fairies' hour,
When stooping o'er her cradle, one by one,
They on the new-born Spring their gifts
bestow;
The gift of bursting shoot and budding
flow'r;
The gift of humid rain and gracious sun;
The golden gift of youth and all its glow!
S. Elliott Napier.
I’m glad I didn’t giv’ ther struggle best
W’en ole July fair socked it onter me,
Fer, ’struth, it’s good ter be erlive en see
Young August dance on sour ole Winter’s chest.
Th’ pairin’ birds er busy wiv their nest,
’En ropes er flowers erwait th’ busy bee.
Th’ ole gohanner’s crawlin’ frum ’is tree,
’E’s fine ez wire frum ’is long winter rest.
On sunny Sundays ain’t it jes’ th’ thing
Ter lay out on yer back in long green grass,
'En listen ter yer nippers larkin’ gay.
W’ile all eround yer matin’ birdies sing,
’En fer up in ther blue th’ cloudlets pass.
Oh! the glory uv ’n’ Austral August day.
P. PHEOZBOUT.
’Ere’s Augus’ runnin’—dicker) Time can’t go!
The flamin’ year a pair uv heels is show’n’ ;
The frost’s a flock uv cockatoos that’s flown
From broken paddicks w’ere the w’ite ’u’d show.
Yer oughter screw the crops git up en’ grow!
It don’t seem no time since the wheat wuz sown,
En’ now the headland’s all wif capeweed blown —
The strippin’ ull be roun’ in arf a mo!
Now springin’ milchers en’ the pelt-bare sheep
Uv gut a rosy picnic in the grass,
En’ wattles look like sunbeams in a heap
Ur golden fleeces fur the wind to class.
Miss Spring uz took the deal; ’er game is deep—
Jist ’ear ole Winter growlin’ out: “I pass!”
Mick Much.
Syd. 0. comes nearer to perfection:—
The earth is like a honey-laden bee
That, drunk with joy, flies chanting ’mid
the flowers
Far sparkling through the limpid, azure
bowers
And golden highways of infinity.
While Life, that swells with spring-tide like
the sea,
And surges joyous, shouting of its flowers,
Sweeps through the land to break in foamy
showers
Of blossom, dripping pearls from every tree.
The gums are tipped with glory of new leaves,
Scarlet and bronze; and swaying in soft
airs
They break the sunlight into jewelled rain.
The birds flit, liquid-tongued, in happy pairs,
And joy a flowery mia-mia lightly weaves,
To dwell by sapphire hills and emerald plain.
SYD. C.
AUGUST.

Shaw believes fervently in moving-pic-
tures. They are so nicely immoral. “The
cinematograph begins educating people
when the projection lantern begins clicking,
and does not stop until it leaves off. It is
educating you far more effectively when you
think it is only amusing you than when it
is avowedly instructing you in the habits of
lobsters Probably at present the best
work the cinema does is the exhibition to
masses of poor children of the habits, dress,
manners and surroundings of people who
can afford to live decently... .Much of tlie
clumsiness and ugliness of our habits is
simple ignorance; we have never seen any-
thing better The cinematograph, by
familiarising us with elegance, grace,
beauty and the rest of those immoral virtues
which are so much’-more , important than the
moral ones, could easily make our ugliness
look ridiculous. The moral virtues can take
care of themselves only too well; it is our
deficiency in the immoral ones that is keep-
ing us back in the march of civilisation.”
By NINA MURDOCH.
Who can have robbed the winter of its sting
And tipped the stately bottle-brush with red,
And set the peeivee calling overhead?
Who is it speeds on dancing feet to bring
The scented brown borohia, and to fling
The sarsaparilla on the bracken bed,
And seeing that the blue wren fain would
wed
Splashes a deeper purple on his wing f .
Oh! it is August, singing by the creek
And flitting to and fro upon the heath,
With busy fingers and bewitching ways
Of darting here and there at hide and seek
To please her babe, the Spring, who under-
neath
A leafy shelter with a wild flower plays.
Brown Drama
Two dramas by Rabindranath Tagore,
Chitra, and The King of the Dark Chamber
(Macmillan, 2s. 6d. and 4s. 6d. respectively),
introduce us to the strange stage world of
India. It is a world of half-lights, subdued
tints, a world of suggestion and not of
drama. Action is rare in this placid un-
folding of narrative; the characters dream
instead of doing. Yet it has a haunting
appeal in its poetry, its quiet beauty, its
silence, its childish outlook, its slurred and
wavering technique. .
There are but four characters in Chitra,
and two of these are gods. This "‘dramatic
poem” has been performed without scenery
in India, the actors being surrounded by the
audience. There are, too, no stage direc-
lions. Chitra, the king’s daughter, being
his only child, has been brought up as a
boy. But she falls in love with the great
warrior, Arjuna, who is living as a hermit
in the forest. She finds that, dressed as a
man, she does not touch Arjuna’s heart;
and asks the god to give her “the power of
the weak and the weapon of the unarmed
hand.” “Give me but one brief day of per-
fect beauty, and I will answer for the days
that follow.” Her prayer is granted, not
for one day but for a whole year. Chitra,
dressed as a woman, and in her new perfect
loveliness, enraptures the man. They lovei
But Chitra, dowered with her false robe of
loveliness, begins to hate her bodily perfec-
tion.
“Heaven,” she “came so close to
my hand that I forgot for a moment that it
had not reached me. But when I woke in
the morning from my dream I found that
my body had become my own rival. It is
my hateful task to deck her every day, to
send her to my beloved and see her caressed
by him. O god, take back thy boon!”
The god refuses. The mortals play their
romance out. Chitra asks: “If by some
magic I could shake myself free from this
voluptuous softness, and fling it from my
body like borrowed clothes, would you be
able to stand it? If I stand up straight
and strong with the strength of a daring
heart spurning the wiles and arts of twin-
ing weakness... .shall I then appeal to
man’s eye? No, no, you could not endure
it. It is better that I should keep spread
about me all the dainty playthings of fugi-
tive youth, and wait for you in patience.
When it pleases you to return, I will smil-
ingly pour out for you the wine of pleasure
in the cup of this beauteous body. When
you are tired and satiated with this wine,
you can go to work or play; and when I
grow old I will accept humbly and grate-
fully whatever corner is left for me.” The
eastern woman —that is, strangely enough,
sister to the suffragette.
At last Chitra comes clothed in her male
attire. “I have many flaws and blemishes.
I am a traveller in the great world-path,
my garments are dirty, and my feet are
bleeding with thorns... .The gift that I
proudly bring you is the heart of a woman.
Ilere have all pains and joys gathered, the
hopes and fears and shames of a daughter
of the dust; here love springs up struggling
toward immortal life. Herein lies an imper-
fection which yet is noble and grand.” And
her lover merely says, “Beloved, my life is
full.” No dramatic suspense, no “curtain,”
merely a charming conceit poetically and
placidly told. „
Frankly, this critic doesn’t know what
The King of the Dark Chamber is all about.
It is such a very brown drama that it is
nearly black. There is a king who has never
been seen by his subjects, and not even by his
queen except in a room that is always kept
in pitch darkness. Thus the queen does not
even know what her husband looks like; she
has never seen him by daylight. Not only
does nobody see the king, but he remains
invisible throughout the drama even to the
audience. His voice is all that is heard, com-
ing from the blackness of the dark chamber.
The wife believes in her instinct: she declares
she could recognise him; but when the king
makes the test she puts her money and her
jewels on the wrong horse. There is a crowd
of lesser kings who come to the conclusion
that since the king is invisible there is no
king. They plot to set up a pretender, but
in the end are defeated by the invisible king.
The one glimpse the queen gets of the king
shows him to her as “black like the ever-
lasting night.” Her love for him is thus
destroyed. She appeals to him to hold her
back, to hold her by the hair, if necessary,
even to strike her. His unresisting silence
makes her wild. No wonder! She leaves
him; there is a series of battles among the
minor kings, whom in the end the unseen
king puts to flight, but what it is all about
the author does not explain ; then the queen
returns to the dark chamber and humbles
herself before the unseen king.
That Is what happens: the moral of the
drama remains unexplained, unindicated.
Possibly The King of the Dark Chamber is
a very good brown drama ; but in Australia
it would not be half as popular as The
Silver King.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Europe—1914.
The courtesies of Grace, the call of Art,
The mongery of Commerce —all of these
He fashioned in a sofa-dream of ease,
Drawing-room and Gallery and Mart;
Yet, in his heart,
Was laired, he knew so well, the Bloody
Thing
That, growling there, crouched ready for the
spring.
The scented parleys of polite Romance,
For all its tinsel-gaud and ribbon-play,
Its flower-petailed strewings of the way,
Its lilied dolling-up of circumstance....
What idle chance
■ Had this to glut the hollow guts of It,
That licked Its jowl, dry-frothed with
famine-spit?
Swishing Its brush in sullen murder-mood;
Beneath Its hide, like frisk of wind on water,
The muscles ran a ripple-stave of slaughter;
It sniffed upon the air the tang of blood,
The reek of food,
Then with a roar, reverberating till
The very stars were trembled, leapt to kill.
It leapt, with all the gloat of murder-heat
And brutal hanker urging to the leap;
Swift as Its motive thought, with rapine
sweep
It smote live flesh to huddles of dead meat,
Nor stayed to eat.
But, drunken with the shambles’ wannish
smoke,
Felt bloodier lust at every murder-stroke.
With reddened tusks and blood-bedabbled
jowl,
It crunched through bone and ripped with
bloody claws
Hot quivering hunks of flesh; Its sloppy
jaws
Gaped but to kill or belch a murder-howl
Or savage growl
Along the foetid airs of carrion breath,
Fanned by miasmic winnowings of Death.
And day by day and week by crimson week.
It stayed not in Its carnage, save to gain
Fresh impulse from Its dreams ; the writhe
of pain.
The wounded moan, the burst veins’ dripping
leak.
The dreadful shriek
When Life and Death were grappling in the
throes
. . . .All these made merry music in Its doze.
* * * *
And he —he viewed it all in stupid daze,
A sickly fullness goitred at his_ throat.
Stifling his speech with gorge of acrid bloat;
Then, wiping from his eyes the mattery haze
That-filmed his gaze,
Wailed to the skies, “Lord God of Battles,
hear!
Is this the End and is the End not near?”.
Then sank down, shuddering, like a child in
fear!
MAORILAND.
BERNARD INGLEBY.
The Last Drunk.
The fragments of the broken world
Shot out like spluttered grease,
And I was quite amazed to find
Myself a solid piece.
The earth had shattered like a shell;
And, through vast spaces flung,
I drifted in an ether sea
With myriad star-lights hung,
Until I met another shape
That spoke in cheery tones;
Its face was as the face of Smith;
Its voice the voice of Jones.
Words that to ever hear again
I had' not dared to think
Resounded bell-like in my ears!
Just “Comeanaveadrink !”
Then arm-in-arm we sank through space,
And rose again, till flight
Had borne us where the Jewelled Gates
Gleamed brightly in the night.
Quoth I: “Oh, Smith-Jones, let us shun
The place where plain souls are;
Now we have come to Heaven, I
Prefer the private bar!
Emboldened by our thirsts we flung
The gaudy portals wide;
And where the agate lanterns hung,
Stepped fearlessly inside.
With wonder and delight we saw
The angel of the place,
The star-shine glimmered in her hair;
I seemed to know her face.
She filled two crystal goblets up
With Heaven’s longest beers;
And Jones and I together stood
And drank for years and years.
There loomed one terror in my heart,
And that I told to Smith.
“When all the cash we have is gone
What shall we purchase with?”
Smith-Jones’s answer was a laugh;
As reaching out afar
lie drew from Space as from a bank
A bright, new, shining star!
And there we drank, as ages passed,
Of liquors rich and rare,
Expending all the stars until
The dizzy skies seemed bare.
Still one remained, and, as I reached
To snatch it from its place
Flung from the golden bar of Heaven
I hurtled into Space.
That Smith and Jones were forced to pay
To mend the broken light
Seems but a little price for such
A rich and gorgeous night!
N.S.W.

conditions. Owners report that these cars run smoothly on rough roads, and
never falter at the steepest hills. Throughout the longest journey the
engine runs silently and satisfactorily and never over-heats.
It will run very slowly or very quickly as required, responding to the
opening of the controlling throttle in a way that will satisfy the most
critical.
The Delaunay-Belleville has four-cylinders cast in pairs, with enclosed
valves. Bosch dual high-tension magneto ignition. Delaunay-Belleville

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 17, 1914.
SHORT HOURS, SHORT WAGES AND
SUNDRIES.
The curse comes home to roost first in New
South Wales, because that State is more
addicted to imports, foreign loans and
Freetrade than any of its neighbors. The
Holman Government has postponed the
delivery of its financial statement with the
plain, simple, unabashed explanation that,
by reason of the stoppage of the lOU, it
doesn’t know where it stands, or lies, or
falls down. This great gold-producing coun-
try has loafed into such a state of depend-
ence upon gold-loans from countries which
don’t produce gold that, when the golden
land can’t borrow its own sovereigns back
at high interest, the bottom begins imme-
diately to fall out of things. The war
hasn’t yet added a single Conner to our taxa-
tion, yet in New South Wales the men
employed on public works are being put on
half-time. Very soon they may be glad to
get two days’ work per week, which will be
one-third time. In a number of private
enterprises the half-time system, or the
system of paying half the wages in cash
and half in certificates and deferred
promises, is being considered. What kind
of a show the Holman Government will
make with its financial statement a couple
of months hence, Heaven only knows. Even
if the Germans are then taking the back
track to Berlin, finances may be much worse
than they are to-day, for victory is only a
shade less expensive than defeat, and the
ruin grows worse with every day of war.
A little while ago there was no drought on
the horizon as there is to-day. The country
was turning out more produce than it ever
did before, and selling it at high prices.
The markets of the world were open to it.
The Cohen of the world was not un-
friendly. The New South Wales revenue
was far larger than it had been at any
previous time. Yet the Holman-McGowen
combination produced the biggest deficit
that its State ever heard of. How it will
pan out now is a mystery, but unless it is
a very different gang from the gang it used
to be it will pan out something terrible.
Even before the war and the loan stop-
page came to pass, and the drought
threatened, there were certain signs of a
breakdown in New South Wales. One sign
was the decrease in the profits (so-called)
made out of manufactures. The number
of employees increased, and the amount of
work done increased, but in one respect
there was a falling-off. This table explains
the situation:—
PER EMPLOYEE.
Total Raw Wages Balance
output. materials. paid. added.
£ s. d. £ s. d. £ s. d. £ ,s. d.
1909.. 468 9 7 308 2 5 83 11 9 76 15 5
1910.. 497 11 11 326 11 8 90 16 4 80 3 11
1911.. 500 6 3 321 8 4 96 7 1 82 10 10
1912.. 529 5 6 333 0 2 104 8 10 91 16 6
1913.. 545 4 6 347 13 11 109 8 2 88 5 1
The figures in the last column are com-
monly uescribed as “manufacturers’ profit,”
but in reality they have to cover many
things besides. Out of them comes rent,
taxes, -interest, exchange, bad debts, coal,
gas, water supply, repairs, depreciation,
new buildings, new machinery, insurance,
and a multitude of sundries. As the use
of machinery increases the margin required
to keep things going increases. Between
3912 and 19±8 the average wage of the
workman rose considerably, but the balance
available to keep the workman’s loom or
other tools of trade in order, or to buy
improved appliances, dropped considerably.
Plainly the policy of shoving up wages,
without any corresponding increase in the
tariff —and the tariff is the only thing which
prevents the high-wage Australian being
swamped out of existence by low-wage
people outside—was near the breaking-
point before the European troubles
appeared.
Australia is supposed to float loans
abroad for public works purposes only. In
reality it pawns posterity to German and
other foreign financiers to pay for foreign
boots and clothes and iron-ware and sun-
dries, so that its people may be out of work,
and its population may not increase, and its
capacity for defence may be kept as low as
possible. Loans are floated abroad because,
if this country sent out enough gold to cover
the difference between its scanty exports on
one side and its imports and its foreign
interest bill on the other, there would, in-
side two years, be hardly a copper left in
the community. Supposing that Australia
exported sufficient goods to cover its im-
ports and to cover the interest on its exist-
ing foreign liabilities, it could pay for all
its public works out of local loans (in
which case the interest would remain in
the country), or, which would be far better,
it could pay for them out of internal taxa-
tion. There are two ways, and only two,
in which this reform can be arrived at.
One way is to bring about an enormous,
incredible, unprecedented increase of exports
of raw material; but, unfortunately, with
a drought in view, and with many of the
wool and ore markets closed, exports are
likely to fall off rather than increase. The
other way is to cut down imports ruthlessly
by a stupendous increase of the tariff, and
import artisans instead of goods, and force
a feckless, casual, loan-mongering commu-
nity to depend on its own toil and its own
resources.
* * * *
This implies, as a side issue, that the
Commonwealth must take over the State
debts, and establish a very substantial sink-
ing fund for their repayment. The' Com-
monwealth has no power to prevent people
of the Holman class pawning posterity to
the financier at Berlin or elsewhere. But
it lias power to take over the lOU when it
is floated, and to levy a tax for repayment
purposes which will convince the fool people
who support Holman and his kind that
there is no ultimate profit in the policy of
putting the child up the spout, so that the
old bootmaking narent may be out of work
while the community wears German shoes.
* * * *
As already mentioned, a considerable sec-
tion of the working community is being put
on half-time. It is expected to sit down in
an ill-clad condition while the wool rots. It
is also expected to struggle along as best
it can on foot, because the ores that should
be made into steel rails lie dead in the
ground, and steel rails can’t be procured
from Germany. Its bullock is selling at
a great reduction because the hide doesn’t
find the old nlentiful market in Europe, and
at the same time its boots are liable to
reach famine price because the European
artisan is too busy making war to make the
usual boots out of our own supply of hides.
Even the Foreign-trade journal is short of
German paper on which to print its full and
frank and complete opinion of Germany.
In short, the Foreign-trade policy of export-
ing raw materials and importing manufac-
tured goo(ls and loans is breaking down at
the first teal trial. The trial promises to
be short, so the breakage may not be very
serious, but if another Thirty Years’ War
came to pass, wonderful events would hap-
pen. Apart from the fact that Australia
has sent a handful of troops abroad this
country is as far removed from the trouble
as if it were located in Jupiter. Its
resom ces are unimpaired and its safety isn’t
threatened. It has merely proved itself
some lund of a lunatic. Nobody can sym-
pathise 7 ery much with the New South
Wales workman who is put on half-time.
Despite thousands of warnings he has been,
and still is, Australia’s most consistent fol-
lower of the Import-and-Burst policy which
is at the bottom of the trouble.
* * * *
It is commonly alleged that the war will
give a great impetus to Australian industry,
and that affairs will, from this time onward,
wear quite a new complexion. But, unless
the tariff is radically changed, nothing of the
sort is likely to happen. In fact, things
are likely to be worse instead of better.
Inside -five years, as shown above, the
average wage of the New South Wales fac-
tory employee (including men, women,
girls, flappers, boys and office-boys) lias
risen from £B3 11s. 9d. per annum to £lO9
Bs. 2d. There has been no addition to the
tariff to counterbalance this very remark-
able rise; and every year, as the
import returns and the foreign loan figures
show, the struggle of the local manufacturer
to hold his own against the foreigner has
become more acute. When the European
calamity is’ ended there will probably be more
low-priced dabor available in Belgium and
Germany and Austria than has been seen
thereabouts for a generation. The ruined in-
dustries will have to be built up afresh, and
cheap widows and children will take the dead
men’s places at the looms. With the local
trades-unionist, who is very often also a
Foreign-trader, still pushing up his demands
for wages, the Australian manufacturer isn’t
likely to spend much money on extending his
works, no matter how short the community
may be, for the moment, in the matter of
clothes and boots and steel rails. Unless the
tariff and the whole financial system is radi-
cally amended he is merely invited to be a
cat’s-paw and a stopgap until our worst
enemies are ready to resume business at the
old address. Then he is to be a bad debt.
The situation doesn’t appeal to him. Still
less does it appeal to the bank which finances
him.
[James Edmond, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Patriots of York-street.
The S. M. Herald is perpetually being
almost persuaded to grow into a good Pro-
tectionist. Then, just when it is apparently
going to take the leap, it sets its ears back
and props on the brink. As one instance of
this tendency to stumble on the threshold
and not go the whole ho°', it came out last
Saturday with a few words about trade and
patriotism, the closing sentence being as
follows: —
Our own people should in future be patriotic enough
to give the preference to Australian or British goods,
and look for Australian or British trade marks, not
for German ones.
And it may be added that our own people
should in future be patriotic enough to avoid
breaking into houses, forging cheques,
assaulting policemen and throwing bottles in
the street to the detriment of George V.’s
subjects, especially as George Y. wants his
subjects just now ; and to kill one of tliem
with a bottle is playing into the hands of
Austria. But in case they should lack the
necessary patriotism the law provides stren-
uous means of compulsion in the shape of a
gaol. The Bulletin doesn’t propose gaol
for the user of German goods, but: a heavy
fine might meet the casej and the handiest
place at which to collect the fine is at the
Customs House. Unless the tariff is boosted
up, the patriotic person who deals in high-
wage Australian goods, made under the
shadow of the Union John and with the
royal blessing and approval, will simply be
undersold and ruined by the unpatriotic one
who sells (possibly under a false description)
cheap-labor German goods. (Germany is
going to be a very cheap-labor country for a
long while now.) Let anyone go along
York-street, Sydney, and look at the names
of the patriots whom the guileless Herald
proposes should be trusted on low-tariff prin-
ciples to push Australian rather than Ger-
man goods when the war is over. Does
Schmidt look promising, or Sciiwabe, or
Schrader, or Blumentiial, or Gottlieb
FreyV Or does the paper pin its faith to
Hermann Gottschalkv
[James Edmond, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
4.
The Need for a Bard.
The war is badly in want of its stipendiary
poet—a man who will follow the struggle in
a baggage waggon or a balloon, and burst
into song when anything stupendous is done.
Tennyson rushed into ' metre over the
charge of the Six Hundred at Balaclava,
and though his rhymes were rotten —for in-
stance, “hundred” and “sundered” don’t go
together—he made that little scuffle famous.
It wasn’t very much of a scuffle. The Rus-
sians were already beaten, and were retreat-
ing. They were badly outnumbered. The
Six Hundred had two victorious armies
behind them to give support, and both
armies lent a hand at the rescue of the dis-
ordered outfit. There was a rush upon the
rear of the ruined Russians, and a rush
back. Two-thirds of the attacking force was
wiped out, but episodes of that sort happen
in every war. By accident, however, there
was a thunderous bard around, and he
wrote a ringing rhyme, and tried to make
“hundred” rhyme with “sundered.” The
present war is such a huge affair that in-
dividual acts of heroism are liable to be
lost. When the Russians and the Germans
first met, the Russian commander evidently
regarded time as the great essential. By
going straight at the enemy he would lose
men; by going round the enemy he might
lose a day.. Two cavalry regiments were
blotted out in the sprint on the German guns,
but a third one broke through and cleared
the way. Neither Tennyson, Byron,
Shelley, Bret llarte, Shaicspeare,
Roderic Quinn or Campbell was there to
write up the magnificent suicide, so the
episode only got a few lines of dull prose
in the cable intelligence. The story of the
French Zouaves who plugged into the battle
at Meaux, 8000 strong, and came back with
only 1000 survivors, ought to have been put
into immortal print by David McKee
Wright, or Mrs. Forrest, or Whitney, or
Dennis, but the battered remnant couldn’t
find a singer of songs in the vicinity. When
the British army did its retreat in Bel-
gium, holding back an almost overwhelming
enemy, it would have been a gain to
literature if Homer or Milton or “Bel-
lerive” or Grant Llervey or God had been
on hand to write up the subject. As it
was, Thomas Atkins plodded along
methodically in the mud, and nobody built
a song-and-dance about him. The lack of
the right poet at the- right place and the
right moment has spoiled many a war. A
recent Irish person raised a very humble
scrap at Cremona to more dignity than it
deserved by writing second-rate verses about
it. The battle of Mohacz looms more largely
in history than Leipzig or Chalons because
someone penned or blotted third-rate melody
concerning it. The moral is that every army
should be accompanied by its stipendiary
poet, and its deputy poet, and its assistant
poet, and its apprentice poet, and its hon-
orary poet, and its sub-poet, and perhaps
even by its office-boy poet. Then its deeds
would live in story, and in the end the
public would get tired of them and put the
whole lot into the ash-barrel.
A Sydney Need.
The destruction of Darlinghurst Gaol,
Sydney, was begun the other day, and it was
decided by the authorities to throw the build-
ing open for a week, and give the public an
opportunity for a last, long bask in its
dreadful atmosphere. The “right-thinking”
idea appeared to be that a severely-limited
number of well-dressed sightseers would
transpire; that they would gloat in a de-
tached and refined spirit over the “drop” and
the rest of the prison paraphernalia, _ and
then go quietly away in an uplifted condition
of mind. What actually occurred was a
shock to “Pro Bono Publico” end a cause
of anguish to “Sturdy .Liberal.” There
was an unprecedented rush on the doomed
resort. Thousands fought their way in, and
the longer it stayed open the bigger the
crowds grew. The S. M. Herald wrote a
leader headed “Refinement,” pointing out the
community’s melancholy lack of that quality.
The Minister who had been resnonsible for
the idea publicly heaped ashes upon his
head. And all because the show hal turned
out a howling success instead of a frost.
The moral which this paper deduces from it
is that Sydney is starved in the matter of
historical museums —in the sense of resorts
where the drama of the past, as opposed to
the literature of the past, is featured. It
was a queer commentary on something or
other- —either on the illogicality of the paper
or on the foolishness of its correspondents —
that in the very issue in which the protests
against the alleged morbid rush on Darling-
hurst were thickest there should have ap-
peared, without a word of editorial
rebuke, a contribution urging the estab-
lishment in Sydney of a place along
the lines of the Musbe Carnavalet.
The Musfee Carnavalet is really an
unoccupied Darlinghurst Gaol —only more
so. There one is chown model guillotinefe,
books bound in the skins of dead malefactors,
handkerchiefs stained in the life’s blood of
murdered kings and queens, hair taken from
executed people after their death, and other
exhibits reminiscent of the dreadful past. No
one, either in Australia or elsewhere, has
ever thought of protesting against the lack of
national "refinement” which causes a demand
for a Carnavalet museum. Possibly because,
to be consistent,' the objector would have to
range the whole civilised world with his pro-
testations. In London, thousands go, unre-
buked, every year to the Tower, and peer into
the dungeons, and gaze on the spot where so
many were beheaded in the past. Further
thousands eagerly inspect the Catacombs at
Rome, and the Bridge of Sighs at Venice,,
and the mouldering corpse of St. Charles
Borromeo, in Milan. Cathedral, and the
“oubliettes” of Chilian Castle, and the par-
ticular section of the floor of Canterbury
Cathedral where St. • Thomas a’Beckett
was murdered, and countless other places
which are odorous of bygone horrors. These
institutions supply what may be called the
melodrama of history. The demand for it is
perfectly natural and in every-way legitimate,
human nature being so constituted that when
it is young it reads Grimm’s Fairy Tales,
and when it is old it. makes a success of
“The Face at the Window.” The Werz
Gallery, in Brussels, is. more marrow-freezing
than all the empty gaols in creation ; yet
civilisation would not readily do away with
it. As a matter of fact, one of Sydney’s
great needs is a place which will mirror
the unpleasantnesses as well as the beauties
of the past—society’s defeats in addition to
its triumphs. The Holman Government has
been busy of late resuming fine old foreshore
dwellings like Vaucluse, Greycliff and Car-
rara for different purposes. The next one
.to be taken over might well be transformed
into the Australian equivalent of a Carna-
valet Museum. Judging by Darlinghurst
form, it would be as popular as a good surf
beach.

A Place Within the Meaning of the
Act.
If the wages paid to Judges and their asso-
ciates, solicitors, law clerks, barristers, tip-
staves, court criers and sundries, while pon-
dering the question of what is a “place”
within the meaning of the Victorian Gaming
Act, were lumped together they would pro-
vide a sum that would go a long way towards
paying for the trans-Australian railway.
The conundrum has again been before the
Full Court for the manyth time. Sol Green,
Melbourne’s eminent bookmaker, was fined
for having used a “place” for betting, con-
trary to the provisions of the blessed statute;
the “place” being a private box at the
G.P.O. Green asked Judge Wasley to
upset the beak’s decision, but Wasley re-
fused, so Solomon, in all his glory, appealed
to the Full Court. The Court viewed the
“place” (a receptacle sin. x 3in. x 4in.) from
all points of the compass, and after listening
to the remarks of several learned gentlemen,
and scratching its own learned horsehair, it
decided that a “place” must be somewhere
where someone can do something objection-
able. Examining Green and examining the
box, the Court reckoned that the idea of
Solomon misbehaving hirhself in the box
was not feasible, for Solomon couldn’t get
into the box, though part of him might do
so. The Bulletin would like to remind the
Court that, about eight years ago, it settled
this vexed question in a leading article, and
if his joint and several Honor had read that
article he might have been saved a lot of
trouble. Perhaps his Honor was too mean
to pay 6d. for the paper. This was the gist
of the definition :
A place is anywhere beneath Heaven or above •
Hell, or any other spot whatsoever.
It includes the welkin, and Purgatory, and Wal- ■
halla and Niflbeim and the Happy Hunting Grounds ,
and the Jasper Sea.
It embraces the ocean right down to the bottom i
of the ocean.
It covers the past, the present and the future...
Also it covers superstition and tradition and legend, and!
life and death, and the hope and fear of a hereafter,,
and the beginning of things and the end of things,
and all things or scarcity S-of things between the-
beginning and the end.
It includes everything not otherwise specified.
Inside the bounds of its definition are live animals-
and dead animals and comatose animals which the-
veterinary surgeon has doubts about.
The term is sufficiently wide to account for bal-
loons, aeroplanes, birds, divers, drowned ships, and all’
properties known to exist, or not known to exist, or-
iikely to exist, or never likely to exist.
The highest cloud and the lowest level of the-
deepest wild cat are in the category.
A “place” takes in Adam and the Last Man. A 150,.,
it includes ruins, mummies, re-incarnations, dreams,,
visions, prophecies, hieroglyphics and base imag-
inings.
And if there happens to be any darned thing which l
these specifications don’t cover, that also is a “place”
within the meaning of the Act, and anybody who pro-
fesses that it isn’t shall be hanged.
If some member would rush through a BilF
to the above effect, the Law wouldn’t require
to worry so much about Sol Green. Wh<v
is Sol Green, anyhow?


Aeroplane v. Battleship.
Deab Bulletin,- —The idea is beginning to
get about that all that is required to suc-
cessfully defend Australia and Maoriland
is a few aeroplanes manned by dare-devil
airmen who would be willing to take their
lives in their wings by going out and drop-
ping explosives on a hostile fleet. This
notion should be jumped on heavily with
all the two boots of Gon. The man in the
street is prone to sadly over-estimate
the lighting abilities of the air machine,
and imagines that the easiest way to wipe
out battle fleets is to climb skyward and
drop dynamite on to the decks of the enemy.
This is all so much piffle. Evidently
the general idea is that the decks of fighting
craft are made of celluloid, tin or tissue
paper. The fact is that it is just
about as futile to hit a modern battleship
on the deck as it is to hit Jack Johnson on
the head. Modern, high-angle gun fire
doesn’t land slap up against the side of a
ship. It really falls from the clouds, and
the farther it has been hurled the straighter
it drops. Take a shell from a 13in. gun
with an effective range of about 12 miles.
The gun that delivers it noses up towards
Heaven, and at the highest point in its
flight the shell will reach an altitude of
well over 20,000 ft. So when that shell
arrives it is coming down from a great
height in an awful hurry, and there are
many more, chances of it landing on the
ship’s deck, if it hits its mark, than striking
the vessel on the side. Because of this fact
the deck of the modern lighting ship is
about the most heavily protected part of

the vessel. The really vulnerable portion
of a battleship is the part below the water-
line, and that’s where the submarine or
floating mine gets in its work. For
anything dropped from above to be really
effective it would need to come from a great
height, say, between 6000 and 10,000 feet, and
it would have to be strong, and consequently
heavy, in order to give it penetrative power.
1-low many bombs weighing 8001 b. apiece
can the modern aeroplane take up skyward,
and how many men woulu be necessary to
drop junks of loaded ironmongery of that
description? Also, what chance would there
be of hitting anything from a distance of
even GOOOft.j! I am not decrying the value
of the aeroplane in its proper place; but
the air machine is not a fighting craft and
is never likely to be one. It is a valuable
adjunct to the army and the fleet as a
scout, because of its great speed and the
fact that it can go anywhere without need-
ing rails or a road. But the way to defend
this continent is by building up an efficient
fleet along the lines of the present small
Australian Navy—producing a _ 27-knot
fighting force, backed up by sufficient sub-
marines and an adequate man-army and
transport on land. Just now Admiral
Jellicoe holds the North Sea and the
Atlantic because he commands the greatest
fleet backed by the best gunners that have
ever sailed the sea, and the way to defend
Australasia is to build up such an Aus-
tralian fleet that its admiral will be able
to hold the Pacific safe. —Yours, etc.,
Moana.
“10-2-4”; According to Sydney Sun
(11/9/T4) :
A New York report from German sources states
that the Kaiser, who is at the front, drove in a
motor car to a point on rising ground ar.d there slept
the sleep of the weary, undisturbed by the thunder
of the cannon.
He looked worn and haggard. When he awoke he
mounted a horse and chatted dispiritedly for a few
moments with his staff. Then he moved off to a spot
from which he could watch the field operations
through his binoculars. For two hours he did not
speak a word, and then he dismounted and went to
the German rear at top-speed in the automobile which
had been brought up for him. r „ ar(1 „
The Emperor is surrounded by some of his Guards,
who do not leave him night or day.
I put it frankly, as liar to liar, to the im-
postor who sent that message: Is there a
word of it that couldn’t have been deduced
without the necessity of going within IU,UUU
miles of the war, by any person outside an
asylum? Now, had something like the fol-
lowing come along, I should have regained
it as worth while, if only by reason ot its
unexpectedness: —•
The Kaiser had not been near the front till yester-
day. He had been too busy fishing to spare the time.
However, at 9.80 a.m. he drove slowly up in a milk-
man’s cart, which is the customary vehicle for
monarchs in war-time. He looked thoroughly cheer-
ful as was but natural in the circumstances, the
corpses of his subjects being plainly visible on all
sides. After a few joking words with his stall he
grew bored or something, and turned his back on
proceedings. Most of the staff was in a valley. Ihe
rest of it was in drains and under bridges and cul-
verts. The Kaiser used a newspaper, folded into a
spiral, for the purpose of examining the surrounding
scenery. Most War Lords would have used binoculars
—but not William. He proceeded, ultimately, in the
milk-cart above mentioned in the direction ot the
enemy’s machine guns. When last seen the enemy
had opened fire on him, and the milk-horse had been
shot dead. He has dispensed with his Guards. House-
hold regiments are, of course, only used by monarchs
in peace-time. No monarch requires Guards during
a war.
I venture to say that something on the lines
of the above would have produced a marked
sensation. The other only evokes rage.
When I read such stuff I feel that I have
been treated as a member of a vast nursery,
and I blaspheme for the loss of my penny.
It is a serious thing for papers to make
people blaspheme daily over their squandered
coppers.
“F.C.” :—-
The Melb. Age didn’t do Joe Cook half so much
harm in Victoria as the action of the Landlords in
monkeying with Premier Peacock’s Price of Goods
Bill, designed to correct the vagaries of exploiters,
cornerers and such like during the war. Rightly or
wrongly, their attitude was taken as a fair measure
of the Fusion point of view in such things, and
Cook got the resulting kick. Liberalism has, of
course, always contended that it is impossible to
regulate the prices of goods, since the law of supply
and demand attends to that. War, however, knows
nothing of .that law —or any other, for that matter.
War is a matter of getting down to first principles,
and the strongest makes his own laws and imposes
them wherever he can. When the Vic. Landlords were
invited to test this patent theory of theirs they fell
back on another excuse, that there was no need for
any such drastic course; it was a “restriction of
trade” and an “interference with private enterprise,”
and so forth. But the electors remembered the con-
dition imposed—in the interest of private enterprise
—by these same men on the State’s coal industry at
AVonthaggi ; viz., that no coal must be sold to the
public individually. They were told that some of
the wheat then being shipped away was going in-
directly to Germany, and doubtless they recollected
that some of their Tory legislators bore suspiciously
German names — Saciise, Sternberg, etc. So when
the Obstructives met on Tuesday things had hap-
pened, and they withdrew all their amendments, and
passed the Bill without argument, likewise its co-
ordinate Foodstuffs and Commodities Bill. It was
a fearsome backdown for the House of Rich Land-
lords.
[F. C. Jones,' Elgar-road, Burwood, Vic.]

The Bulletin desires to mention _ that it
didn’t publish any Melbourne theatrical pic-
tures last week, because the postal arrange-
ments went wrong as usual.
Gye’s illustrations turned up about the
middle of the day on Wednesday, whereas
they should have arrived early on Tuesday.
The reason of this delay no man knows.
The matter has been inquired into very
often, for it has happened very often, and
the only result has been a memo saying that
the complaint was received and that it would
be considered more or less. Presumably
the postal service is rotten. Presumably it
is exceedingly rotten under Cook and more
or less rotten under Fisher. It seems to
be especially rotten under Cook. William
Kelly may have something to do with the
trouble. Without any desire to be unpleas-
ant, The Bulletin expresses a hope that
the person who is responsible may be chained
up in the dog-lcennel of the Hereafter for a
million years, along with his first wife’s
step-mother.
[James Edmond, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
It may be that the downfall of the German
Empire, the dismemberment of Austria, the
vanishment of the Hoiienzollerns from
among the reigning families of Europe, the
reconstruction of Poland, the restoration of
Alsace and Lorraine to France, and the
recovery of Trieste by Italy will all hinge in
the end upon these few days of stress when
the Belgians held Liege against impossible
odds, and threw the German time-table out
of gear. Ever since then the Teuton has
been about a fortnight late, and that meant
that all his enemies were two weeks ahead.
To quote only one instance, the Germans
should have been at Paris before the Prus-
sians were at Lemberg. It must have made
a difference. Instead of the. Austrian tail
being stiffened and exalted by good men from
France, the German tail was depressed by
the information that its one ally was practi-
cally dead. And Paris doubtless took an
extra absinthe on the strength of it, and felt
that it could knock out a million Germans.
It was apparently by the smallest of margins
that the Allies turned the tide at last, and
started ihe Kaiser’s legions on the back
track for home. If William’s train liadn t
been late there might have been no such
margin.
An ultra-reactionary Tic. Assembly-man
mentioned the other day that Australian
bran and pollard were being sold for export
at a lower price than locally, and demanded
that their export be absolutely prohibited.
Simultaneously the head of a firm of gram
merchants informed Alfred Deakin, as
chairman of the Federal Food Supply Com-
mission, that there was very little surplus
wheat in the Commonwealth, and its export
should be entirely stopped pending a reli-
able estimate of the coming season s crop.
Neither of these persons seems to have
grasped that we don’t export goods tor
amusement, but to pay our debts; and that
these debts have to be paid, if not m wheat,
wool, etc., then some other way. It looks
as though, between the war and the drought,
the Laborites will have a tough .financial
and industrial row to hoe. All tilings con-
sidered. the sooner the promised Protective
tariff is passed the lighter the dreadful im-
plement will be to handle.
[F. C. JoneSj Elgar-road, Burwood, \ic.]
The news that Maoriland s Government pro
poses to introduce some reasonable control
over public works expenditure may be re-
garded as evidence that, for the present at
any rate, the old game played by Seddon and
Ward, and diligently copied by the unimagi-
native Massey, is up. A Board of thiee
members, it is suggested, will act as a sort
of advisory committee, or controlling author-
ity, or referee, or something of the sort; and,
if' Premier Massey gets the right sort ot
men, the innovation should, for a time at any
rate, be productive of good. All over Maon-
land are miles and miles of loan roads and
hordes of loan bridges that have fallen into
decay through the neglect and parsimony of
local authorities, which spend most of then
time composing letters to the Ministry beg-
ging for a remittance out of loans to meet
the cost of repairs and upkeep of the works
which should be maintained out of lates.
This was all very well while the boom was
on, but as a programme it could manifestly
not last an hour after the bursting of the
bubble. The bubble has burst.

“Clifton”: One striking effect of the war
is seen in the Australian Note Issue. The
circulation at the end of last Julj
was £9,712,000. At the end of Aug-
ust it was £11,812,762—an increase of
more than f 2 ,100,000. When peope
feel their stomachs sinking at tnougnts
of battle and bloodshed, and commence to
withdraw their mite from the banks, and to
deposit it in a jam-tin in the backyard l or an
old stocking under the bed, the banks aie
practically compelled to push paper currency
so as to protect their stocks of gold.. Hit
despised ten-shilling note, which previously
passed an apologetic existence with a pro-
geny of only 50,000, jumped m a month to
99 000 In fact, several bank-clerks have
overcome the habit of indulging in a convul-
sive shudder at the mention of the article.
The gold reserve now stands at £6A45 <.L,
the balance— approximately £5, 000,000-
having been loaned mainly to the states at
current rates of interest. If the reserve
stood at only 25 per cent which is the
statutory requirement,, another £3.000,000
would be available for investment. Even it
the reserve were fixed at the extra-safe mai-
gin of 40 per cent., the total sum that could
be invested would be more than £7,000,000.
The present high reserve (a shade less than
54 per cent.) cannot long be maintained.
Both Federal and State Governments are
faced with the prospect of heavy deficits,
and the bailiff will be on the national door-
step, and the only practicable way of meet-
ing the bill, pending the adjustment of taxa
tion, will be to borrow from the Notes Re-
serve Fund. Apart from the interest wliu
it earns and the brokerage, commission ami
other charges which it lias saved to the tetat*
Treasurers who have raised loans from, it,
the Australian Note Issue seems a fail
handy thing to have about the premises in
these times of trouble.

The March of Ivan.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Are you coming, Ivan, coming?—Ah, the
ways are long and slow,
In the vast land that we know not —and we
never sought to know.
We are watching through the daybreak, when
the anxious night is done,
For the dots upon the skyline —black against
the rising sun :
We are watching through the morning haze,
and waiting through the night,
For the long, dark, distant columns that pro-
claim the Muscovite !
Are you coming, Ivan, coming? (Oh! the
world is growing grey
With the terror of the future and the mad-
ness of to-day!)
Are you marching, Ivan —forward? (Oh!
the world is dark’ning fast,
For the crimes of greater nations ’gainst the
small ones in the past.)
Yours, in part, to make atonement, so re-
member what you are!
Ivan ! Sing ! —“The Slav is coming ! On
for Itussia and the Czar!”
Ivan’s Song.
“Yes, I’m coming, IVAN, coming— l am
marching out again
On the weary roads of Itussia, past the
forest, marsh and plain ;
Fast the field and past the village, in the
shine and in the rain—
By the cart-rut and the grass-track and the
jolting cattle train.
(And, maybe, some gleam of glory penetrates
my sluggish brain)
1 am marchina out for Russia, and for
Europe and for you —
But, maybe, I’m mainly marching just
because they told me to.
“I have marched to many frontiers, in the
pregnant days gone by,
When they told us where to march to, but
they did not tell us why.
And they showed us who to tight with, and
they told us where to die.
I have seen our grey battalions to their
Heaven —or Hades—-hurled —-
’Twas enough it was for Russia!- —what
cared we about the world?
“Did one moan of Ivan’s mother penetrate
to other lands?
Did one prayer of Ivan’s father —with his
old and knotted hands?
Did one sob from Ivan’s sweetheart, or
one cry from Ivan’s wife?
Or a wail from Ivan’s children, for the loss
of Ivan’s life?
Marching with the Wolf of Hunger —march-
ing with the Bear of Strength !
We have marched for many winters —but the
end is near at length !
“’Tis a long, long march from Plevna, when
the Bear went to his den.
It is far from the Crimea; Oh ! you did not
want us then !
From the shambles of Port Arthur, ’twas a
weary way and slow —
And our track was always dotted with the
black dots on the snow.
By black dots and crimson splashes you may
trace poor Ivan’s track —
And I think that Ivan’s banner should be
red, and white and black.
“Ne’er was Present-blinded tyrant who
learnt wisdom from the Past,
And there’s one forgot the errand that
brought Ivan this way last!
‘To the frontier, and no farther’ seemed our
motto and our vow,
Since we marched from burning Moscow —
but tve're marching farther now!
Neighbor’s burning house —or city ! —they
are easv to forget;
But we lit a light for Europe that shall be
rekindled yet!
“Never song of Ivan’s valor, or of ‘Russia’s
Flag Unfurled !’
Or the Iron Cross of Russia, penetrates the
Outer World.
Ye! who civilise and peddle, ye who hesitate
and lag,
Never heard the Russian March and never
saw the Russian flag !
You have called on us to save you, never
saying why, or how,
But the Samivor is boiling ! and you’ll hear
and see us now.
“From our garrets and our cellars —from
the little all we had —
Where the winter brings the sleigh-bells to
the streets of I'etrograd ;
From our huts and from our hutches —from
wherever we may be —
From our goat’s-flesh and our mare’s milk
and our black unsweetened tea,
From the silence of Siberia, and the strange,
snow-deadened streets —
From the blazing banks of Jordan, where
we dip our winding sheets.
From our black bread and our vodka—ask-
ing naught, and undismayed—
From our never-empty cradles! —we are
coming to your aid.
“Oh, we leave no bands behind us, blaring
tunes of Tyranny,
And we wave no swords above us, yelling
songs of Liberty;
And no blatant voice of ruler, as we tramp
through dry and wet,
Blares: ‘Remember You are Russians!’ —
we’re not likely to forget.
There are some who have forgotten—merely
men, like you and me—
And one object of .our marching is to jog
their memory.
“You shall hear us, you shall see us —save
the dead and deaf and blind—
While the armor of our winter hardens thick
and fast behind.
We are marching, we are coming, and we ere
not on the sea —
You shall see us on the farthest frontier
of our enemy !
And while you fix up your frontiers, and
remember what you are,
We shall march with Peace for Europe!
back to Russia and the Czar.”
N.B.W.
HENRY JARSON.

Swift & Company Ltd., Australasian Agents, 26/30 Clarence St., Sydney
arrange for each man to get the same rate of
wages as he was getting at Burrinjuck—
that is, if the military pay was less, Lane
and Peters would make up the difference.
This rule was to obtain from the day the
men left the works till their return to Aus-
tralia. If, after going to Sydney, any man
should fail to pass the military tests his job
would be kept open for him. However, all
tbat volunteered were accepted, and then
the firm had each one of them insured
for £250, and, to cap a splendid record, pre-
sented the men’s commanding-officer with a
fine charger. To complete the analogy of
Sydney Gas Co. and Lane and Peters, let
me say that neither firm is anxious for pub-
licity. Both are quite satisfied if they never
hear another word about the business:

“Ballot” : Jogging home t’other evening I
read in the evening rag an official letter
from the N.S.W. Attorney-General saying
that it would be quite illegal and absolutely
opposed to the law of the land to attempt
to run a public sweep or lottery as a means
of raising money for the expenses of the
war. Just as I had assimilated this item I
cannoned into a raucous-voiced party who
yelled at me to buy a ticket in the Eight
Hours’ sweep. Seeking refuge in a tram I
turned over the page and found the result,
in big type, of one of the Tatt.’s sweeps on
a big Soring handicap. Getting out I ran
into a friend who nagged at me to take a
ticket in a raffle, first prize £lOO, got up by
actress Grace Palotta, with kind permis-
sion of the lion. Attorney-General and Min-
ister for Justice. Said raffle was in aid of
the war fund. When I reached my liumpy
I found a letter from a female relative
enclosing a book of tickets for another raffle,
conducted by St. Joseph’s Convent, Bondi,
with a request to “try and get rid of a few
i”. the office.” This sweep or lottery was
also by kind permission of the lion. Attorney-
General and Minister for Justice. Now,
are these two hon. Attorneys-General and
Ministers for Justice, or does the hon. soli-
tary individual do business occasionally in a
trance ?
The war, so far as it has gone, can be put in-
few words this week. There may yet be a
revival and a resurrection on the part of the
spike-helmeted legions of Germany, but at
present nothing of the sort seems probable.
The legions were on the run when last heard
of, and were running in disorder. The
French, British and Belgians all had bad
times in the early part of the campaign, but
whatever their losses they got away in good
order, and were ready to fight again next
day. Also, they left very little behind them
in the way of prisoners and guns. The
Russians suffered two defeats at the hands
of the Germans, but the Bear only shook his
ears and put his great feet down and re-
sumed. Possibly the German and Austrian
armies were over-trained, and when the sol-
dier lost sight of his officer in the confusion
of a temporary disaster he found that all the
initiative had been knocked out of him. Too
little training is bad, but too much may be
bad also. Anyhow, the rally of the Fra neo-
British army outside Paris and of the Bel-
gian forces under the guns of Antwerp were
marvellous efforts of recuperation. So far
their opponents have shown no such capacity
for starting business again with the old vigor
at the old address. These beautifully organ-
ised and regulated forces may be something
like the Macedonian phalanx, which was in-
vincible so long as every soldier kept step,
yet went to pieces at a very slight breakage.
Some person—probably a liar —told a story
of how, at certain German manoeuvres, he
saw 14,000 soldiers listening for the enemy,
each one bent forward at exactly the same
angle, and each with his right hand to his
ear. If Joffre and French and Rennen-
kampf leaned forward at a different angle
and listened with the other ear they might
utterly disorganise the rules of the German
game.

“J.P.” : The Rev. J. O’Bryen Hoare, who
passed over at Christchurch (M.L.) re-
cently, earned a crust until 1892 in Fog-
land and Maoriland as a Church of England
parson. In that year, at Christchurch, the
home of patent religions, he founded a brand-
new creed of his own. At first it was
called _ “Our Father’s Church,” then “The
Socialists’ Church,” and at one period of
its existence it was known as “The Cyclists’
Church.” Meetings were held on the road-
side out in the country every Sunday morn-
ing. The congregation and its best girl
hiked out, and then lay around on the grass
while O’Bryen Hoare held forth. The correct
church-going attire for this service was a
cycling sweater, and ditto skirt or pants, ac-
cording to your sex. It wasn’t a bad kind of
church if the girl you took along happened
to be young and pretty. (I think there
was a rule that all girls taken had to be
young and pretty; if there wasn’t there
should have been.) In recent years O’Bryen
Hoare gathered round him most of the
Socialists and Rationalists in the Flat City
and ended up as a Free Thinker. How
freely they think I don’t know.

Had it come at any other time, “The
Rosary” might have been in for a crowded
season, but opening when every mail is
liable to bring a fresh ultimatum, the filling
of Sydney Adelphi is a huge job. However,
the show started well. It couldn’t have
gone better on Saturday night had the new
Pope blessed it, and asked all his dearly
beloveds to turn up in full force, or had the
Moderator of the Scotclibyterian Church or
the Anglican Archbish or the Boss Cali-
thumpian urged their respective flocks to
book seats. And if a collection plate had
gone its rounds at the final curtain there
would have been sufficient to pension the
entire company off for life. Written by
E. E. Rose, who dramatised “The Prisoner of
Zenda,” “The Rosary,” though by no means
new in the general plot, goes about the job
of exposing the villain in an altogether
novel manner, and winds up as this paper
has never seen a bellowdrama wind up, in
the repentance and forgiveness of that gen-
tleman. The story is of a Wall-street stock
broker-owner of a fine country residence,
appropriately named Eden —who is ruined by
the machinations of the snake (no relation
whatever to one of The Bulletin’s well-
known poets), per medium of an unknowing
twin sister of the broker’s wife. Father
Bryan Kelly, a fine old priest, with sufficient
persuasiveness and naturalness of manner to
convert even a Religious Editor, drops in
and talks his way into the good feelings of
everyone. Finally, aided and abetted by a
strong ray of limelight, he has an inspiration
and exposes the villain. This general out-
line may leave the impression that the play
is a dull, almost over-religious drama. But
everything is done in a bright manner, even
by the priest, and there is a large amount of
Yankee humor, and a larger amount that
isn t Yankee. For these and other reasons
it would be a shame to miss the show.
Rather do without a shave and haircut for
e w days and save the cash, than cut out
Ihe Rosary” for economical purposes.

in. the dual role of the twin sisters, plays
with much feeling and emotion, and Miss

A neat exhibition of conjuring and stage
magic opened at the Little Theatre, Sydney,
on Saturday. The magicians, Melides and
lioy Hood, perform their feats of stage illu-
sion capably ; but the majority of the tricks
seem part of the usual outfit of the conven-
tional conjurer. Both Melides, an old gen-
tleman whose French has to be translated
by his younger companion, and Hood do the
usual mystifying things within a tent. The
“Tossing Testros,” jugglers and balancers,
and. subsequently, in a comedy scene,
bluffers, work hard, with pleasing results.
This week Victor Prince comes out in
Sydney with a fresh venture, the Australian
New Comic Opera Co. Its specialty is Aus-
tralian librettos and scores by Australian
authors and composers, and the goods are to
be played by Australian players. He opens
with a patriotic production, “Australia
First,” by D. H. Souter and F. Wynne
Jones, who were legally and morally respon-
sible for “The Grey Kimono” at the Criterion
some three years ago. Wynne Jones
is a man of melodic reputation, Souter is
said to be witty, and Prince was one of the
Firm’s leading comedians until he resigned
to carry out this scheme.

Like the war, the costume drama, “Du
Barry,” is still going on. But, as most people
already know, the drama is mobilised at
Sydney Royal, while the war is all over the
place. Another point of difference is that
it is necessary to pay if you would witness
the drama, whereas the German firework
show is free —sometimes painfully so.

Those who haven’t seep Muriel Starr look
like a chorus lady are advised —indeed ex-
horted —to haste to the Sydney Criterion
before Saturday. On that evening she is
advertised to descend further in the social
scale, becoming the proprietor of “The Yel-
low Ticket,” without which no Russian lady
of the pavement considers herself quite
respectable.
* * * *
Among the curses of modern journalism
are plays like “The Girl in the Taxi,” now
running at Her Majesty’s, Sydney. It im-
poses on the harried writer the irksome job
of mentioning that it is still at the old loca-
tion, in 10, 15 or 20 different ways, accord-
ing to the length of the run. The really
popular musical comedy has driven almost
as many literary persons mad as pantomime
itself.

The big punch at Sydney’s Imperial Salon
de Luxe this week will be delivered on
Saturday afternoon, when, in the presence
of the local nobility and the admiring
peasants of the suburbs, a novel entertain-
ment in aid of the Alexandra Hospital for
Children commences. No charitable cause
has a stronger claim on patriotic Austra-
lians than that which has for its object the
health and happiness of the Commonwealth’s
embryo • citizens.

** * *
In despite of the fact that theatrical busi-
ness in Sydney has fallen away, and that
the salaries of the profesh are threatened
with the pruning knife (perhaps axe, or
pruning dynamite would better express it),
the daily afternoon performances at the
National Amphitheatre (which might have
been expected to be amongst the first to feel
sick in the box-office), continue to draw
steady and profitable trade. Schell’s lions
(they will eat Daniel for the last time to-
morrow), Brent Hayes (the banjoist),
Millie Doris, Gordon and Day, the Cas-
sellis and others are more or less responsible
for the profits.
** * *
The Gilbert and Sullivan co. at Adelaide
Royal improved steadily as it went on, and
the final “Mikado” saw the best work and
the most crowded houses. Ethel Morrison
is an acceptable singer of the massive type
that Gilbert so unsportingly persisted in
ridiculing. Ruth Lincoln and Dorothy Vane
pair well, so do Frank Wilson and
McKeown, while Villiers Arnold is a good
all-rounder and Walenn a true humorist.
The season recalled many things to the
middle-aged and taught much to the young.
“The Forty Thieves” have just arrived.
Adelaide takes its pantomime at any season
of the year when it can be got, and is duly
grateful. The Tivoli has got down to “sum-
mer” prices—a wise move by manager
Crawford, the effect of which is already
visible. The briskest turns of the week are
the Carpatti athletes and James Teddy, a
newcomer to Australia, who claims various
records in standing-jumps. With feet
together, he flits over a series of chair-and-
table hurdles with not only apparent ease but
very obvious grace. It is a striking turn. The
smaller King’s Theatre seems to be always
full. Ed. Blondell put in an hilarious week
in a “sketch.” He is a sound heavyweight
humorist, but the lady partner in the play
has merely good looks without acting skill.
The Two Cruets have now come in, also
Bogues and Olivotti, and Les Warton (who
was never so good as when paired with
Irving Sayles). Douglas Mawson has given
several shows. As a public speaker he will
never shine; still, he has improved consider-
ably, and his great popularity combined
with his really fine pictures to make his even-
ings a success. Wondergraph shows a lot
of army pictures of the moment, and an
ingeniously-faked Trafalgar of the past.
West’s helps out much patriotic film with
the Adams-Tourrier prize song. A local
Carlos-Yemm effort, however, “Strike Hard
and Strike Again,” .goes with a better swing.
And Show Week has brought the usual crop
of concerts.

** * #
A revival of “Monsieur Beaucaire,” by
Julius Knight and co., at Melbourne Royal,
comes too soon after Lewis Waller’s produc-
tion of the same comedy of “quality” man-
nahs on the some stage. The unalloyed
theatricalism of the “Beaucaire” scenes is
an interesting suggestion of the airs that
“quality” gave itself in Bath 150 years ago,
when set puerilities were the whole business
of sassiety life, and people whiled their
foolish hours away in studying the rules of
etiquette and in putting on dog. The story
of how Hoyal Blood asserted its superiority
and Noble Birth curtseyed to the occasion,
is in such perfect harmony with its clothes
and wigs that “Beaucaire” may be called a
picturesque comedy. But a brief revival of
the hollow piece of snobbery suffices for a
long time. Lewis Waller gave Melbourne
all that it wanted to go on with, and Julius
Knight follows at the wrong time.
* * * *
Knight’s way of playing the heroic trifler
is more heavy and less French than the other
man’s, nevertheless he shapes admirably in
the part. Julius empties a full bag of
decorative tricks into the performance, and
cuts a masterful figure at all stages of the
stagey narrative. Miss Irene Browne, as
Lady Mary, is an improved reminder of
Madge Titheradge, in so far that she is
larger and has a more full-blooded style than
the dainty little lady whom she seemingly
imitates. Eardley Turner is mostly a capi-
tal Beau Nash, whilst Frank Harvey is a
passable Duke of Winterset. Martin Lewis
wants vivacity as Beaucaire’s confidential
friend. Lizette Parkes tends to skittishness
in her diction. Leslie Victor is useful in
two characters, and the other people, bar
Captain Badger, are harmless enough. Bad-
ger is an impossible creature, who stands
straddle-legged to fire off his noisy insults
and looks like one of the sham flunkeys who
are engaged by the management to give
pomp and circumstance to a grand opera
season. The Julius Knight period starts
its termination next Saturday with “The
Sign of the Cross.”
** * *
Talking in verse of “Gipsy Love,” at Her
Majesty’s (Melbourne), this paper referred
to the putty-nosed Roumanian nobleman of
the piece as Dagotin, whereas his proper
moniker is Dragotin, according to the pro-
gramme. The error calls for rectification,
although J. Cannot by any other name than
Dragotin would amount to the same thing.
He always amounts to the same thing, but
sometimes he seems worth more than at
others. This time his value is enhanced
by comparison with the other grotesque char-
acter, played by Phil Smith, who would be
utterly wearisome if he were not linked to
the fresh young gaiety of Dorothy Brunton.
.Miss Brunton’s half of the “Kissing” duet
is a joy, albeit the music is not so pretty
as the accompaniments to Miss Glyn’s duets
with Cannot and Dereck Hudson. “Gipsy
Love” _ will be remembered by its melodies
when it is gone. Miss Elsie Spain’s num-
bers are as sweet as the proverbial pie, and
there is a haunting charm about the “Gipsy
Rover” air which Hudson sings to the 20
lovesick maidens, who pursue him as though
he were another Bunthorne, and move their
arms in measured Sandow exercise whilst
they warble the sweet refrain. Heaven
only knows what words they are refraining
as a rule. Neither Hudson nor the chorus
achieves a distinct enunciation of anything
more than “Gipsy Rover,” but it is all very
tuneful and dreamy, and doubtless the other
words can be identified after frequent
hearing.
