* *
No turn at Melbourne Tivoli these times is
more thankfully received than that of
Tom Kelly, the huge-voiced Irishman
who combines an extravagantly Hiber-
nian face and brogue with expensive
American tailorings. Kelly is _ commend-
able in the first place for trying to be
funny in a fashion-plate outfit. In the second
place- his “wheezes” are funny. They don’t
match his green silk socks, but the young-
est of them are humorous. Miss Smithson
has taken a change of ballads from her reper-
toire, and her head notes are as good as gold.
The De Dio lady’s draperies are colored by
much the same lantern effects as in bygone
years, and Carson —of Jerome and Carson —
throws remarkable somersaults on his _ head.
Also A 1 Rover displays eccentric agility in
a turn which is original and crude.
George Murphy, a talkative juggler from
the U. States, is getting friendly receptions
at Melbourne Bijou this week. Most of his
tricks are old acquaintances, but he performs
them very passably, and opens his white-
washed countenance to good effect. The
Jackalew Russian Trio, composed of a sweet
songstress and two furiously gyrating Cos-
sack gents, are worth a word of approval;
but Pearl Livingstone, billed as soubrette
and imitator, merely displays the faint art
that never wins loud favor. The Carpos
Bros, are still balancing nobly for a living.
Miss Dorothy Harris continues to warble
ragtime ditties, heavily charged with ambi-
guity.
“Tiaki”: My music instructor says
(quoting from Steele’s “Prosodia Ration-
alis,” p. 168) that Italian opera gives the
singer a great advantage in that “the number
of vowel and consonant sounds is nearly
equal.” Then why not write opera in
Maori, where every second letter (unless
preceded by the Maori diphthongs “wh”
and “ng”) is a vowel. I think that beautiful
effects could be produced, and have com-
posed an ode as a specimen. Here it is
Ehine tuku mai tou mango,
Tou kaanga kopiro.
Kia ahatia tou kiri pango
Nga kino korero.
Maku e awhi tou kaki—
Ou ngutu mo te kihi.
E koe te-tau-o-te-ate,
Ko tenei taku mihi.
Translated it runs as follows: —
Sweet maid, give me your shark
And also rotten maize.
What of your sable skin
Or what the gossip says?
I will embrace your neck —
Your lips were made to kiss.
Oh thou, “the-loop-o’-the-liver,”
My greeting it is this.
I could, with a very small amount of
musical training, write beautiful opera,
comprising odes, etc., to raw schnapper,
mussells, fat pork, fleas, love, absence of
soap, chivalry, tattooed lips, wood grubs and
all the elements which constitute lyrical
poetry.

AT POVERTY POINT.
“Harrison O.”: “Gipsy Love,” produced
in Melbourne just after the Gilbert and
Sullivan season, confirms my opinion that
Lehar as a writer of orchestra scores is
superior to Sullivan. As an opera “Gipsy
Love” is weaker than the weakest of the
Savoy series, but its orchestration is ahead
of anything in the same line by Sullivan.
In the dance music of the new piece, as in
the Marsovian music of “The Merry
Widow,” Lehar gets a certain wild strain
of melody such as is to be met with towards
the end of Grieg’s “Peer Gynt”; while his
finale to the second act, although concerned
with a comparatively trivial theme, is
superior to that provided by Sullivan even
for so dramatic a story as “The Yeomen
of the Guard.” To my way of hearing, the
difference between the English composer
and the Austrian is, approximately, the
difference between the earlier Verdi, who
was responsible for the barrel-organ melo-
dies of “La Traviata,” and the very differ-
ent Verdi who gave us “Othello” and “Aida.”

The war is no more encouraging to Reper-
tory Theatre endeavor than to any other
form of theatrical entertainment. Gregan
McMahon in Melbourne makes the best of a
bad situation by offering 25 per cent, of the
receipts of his next Repertory season
to patriotic causes. Mac. is staging
“Mrs. Pretty and the Premier,” by Arthur
Adams, on September 26 and 28. On the
first three nights in October he will stage
Galsworthy’s “Joy.”
The Dramatic Authors’ Association
(Eng.) is not giving anything to the cause
of British Imperialism in Australia, seem-
ingly. Jo Smith had arranged an amateur
performance of R. C. Carton’s comedy “Lib-
erty Hall,” to happen at St. Kilda Town
Hall on the 17th inst. —not “ult.” or “prox,”
but “inst.,” as the comic father remarks in
“Gipsy Love.” The “Strolling” Players
Orchestra and Prahran City Band promised
their services, and the gross receipts were
booked for the Patriotic Fund. When all
the printing was out and 1000 tickets had
been sold a mandatory letter from the local
agent for the Authors’ Asso. prohibited the
production. An offer to pay a royalty was
unavailing. The cause of patriotic charity
didn’t matter. So the management hastily
changed the bill to “In Honor Bound” and
“The Nettle,” plus Blascheckian mono-
logues. The performance will eventuate
(d.v.) to-night, the 17th inst.
“Rasper”: Why does the mummer who
follows the original performer in even a
panto, part think it necessary to mimic the
previous person’s mannerisms, regardless of
their good effect or otherwise. -Jessie
Lonnen, as the original “principal boy” in
“The Forty Thieves,” was weak, angular
and quite uninteresting. Her successor in
the part, Winnie Volt, was chosen, on
account, apparently, of her being much
about the same height and slimness as the
other lady, and then she completed the
resemblance by copying the Jessie Lonnen
gait, deportment, smile and air of casual-
ness, instead of trying to put some grace
and ginger into her acting. Talk about the
ghost walking at theatres! The average
understudy, or locum-tenens, is a ghost.
“0.K.”: The libretto of “Gipsy Love,” not
to mention the fact that Jozsi carries his
musical instrument about with him, suggests
that the Gipsy humbug ought to be an actual
fiddler on the stage, even as the late Van
Biene, in “The Broken Melody,” was a fine
’cellist. When he asks the silly Ilona why
she loves him she makes dreamy reference to
the magic of his violin, and practically asks
him to scrape it some more. When the
chorus ladies catch Jozsi alone in the garden
they cry “Give us joy. Play to us ; ” But
he doesn’t play to them; he sings to
them, and they give him vocal sup-
port. Again, when he arrives at the
wine-shop in the second act, the landlord tells
him that his fiddling is what is wanted, but
Jozsi doesn’t fiddle. He travels under false
pretences, as far as the opera goes. He
ought to be Kubelik or iMischa Elman,
instead of a common or garden baritone.
“0.K.” : In issue of B. 27/8/T4 “Mascotte”
commits an arrogant equivocation in saying
“‘Melb’s’ statement (B. 13/8/T4) that
‘Mikado’ opened the existing Princess’s
Theatre in 1885 is incorrect, as that house
started business in December, ISSG.” ‘‘Melb”
was at fault, in his date only. “The Mikado”
did open the existing Princess’s on December
IS, 1886. The performance was a revival of
the opera which had run for 12 weeks at (he
Royal earlier in the year. The first essential
for a correction is absolute correctness, I
take it. Seeing that “Melb” was correct in
naming the show which opened the Prin-
cess’s, the contradiction should have been
confined to the date of the opening. By the
way, Her Majesty’s Theatre in Melbourne
(less of an improved edition of the old Alex-
andra than it now is) was also opened with a
revival of “The Mikado” on Mafficking night
—May 19, 1900. Not the spurious official
Mafeking night, when the drunk had grown
stale, but the Saturday,when the first news
of the relief came to hand and Australia
went mad.
“Mid-Channel”: “Norbell” is not correct
in what he states concerning Charles Ken-
ningham being in most of the original Lon-
don casts of Gilbert and Sullivan opera (B.
10/9/T4). The tenor parts were taken
by Durward Lely in the original productions
of “lolanthe,” “Patience” and “The Mikado.”
At the reproduction of the first two, Robert
Evett was the tenor. Kenningham was in
the reproduction of “The Mikado” at the
Savoy, but the only opera in which he
played an original part was “Utopia Ltd,”
which was the last of the Gilbert-Sullivan
series, and the least successful. He played
the part of Fitzbattleaxe. In the recent
operas, such as “The Gondoliers” and “The
Yeomen of the Guard” Courtice Pounds took
the tenor roles.
“Mid-Channel”: Agree with “C. Ockney”
(B. 10/9/T4) that “Harrison O.” is talking
through his collar when he asserts that “The
chances of this country possessing a Ber-
nard Shaw are remote, bilt there is no rea-
son why it should not disgorge a Pinero.
Shaw has to be born : Pineros and Grundies
can be made.” Pinero is recognised as the
greatest English dramatist, and the only one
to whom the credit is ascribed of being able
to write a play as it should be acted. More-
over, his works typify to the last detail
the master craftsman and the master
dramatist combined, and his “situations”
are the result of skilful building, and
natural, as well as artistic, development.
The quality of his dialogue and epigrams
is unapproachable. He is essentially a
dramatic expert; Shaw is not. Shaw is
but a stage conversationalist, and often-
times so dreary and long-winded that those
who produce his plays have _to start on
the dialogue with an axe, as in “Man and
Superman.” Pinero learnt the art of stage
navigation thoroughly before attempting to
write plays, while Shaw has attempted to
take charge of the ship with only a passen-
ger’s certificate. Pinero has established his
claim to genius in a g_alaxy of masterpieces
(“Mrs. Tanqueray,” “Mrs. Ebbsmith,”
“Iris,” “Letty,” “Mid-Channel,” “Trelawny
of the Wells,” “The Benefit of the Doubt,”
“The Thunderbolt,” “His House in Order,”
“The Amazons,” “The Profligate,” etc.).
Such slabs of lewdness as Shaw’s “Mrs.
Warren’s Profession” do not call for the
birth of a successor.
When the war had been raging just long
enough for Germany to have become very
unpopular, a civic affair, at which an
orchestra was to brighten proceedings, was
held at Ballarat. The orchestra had re-
hearsed a set of pieces called “Sehusucht nach
Wien,” composed by Erik Meyer-Helmund.
As such names wouldn’t have looked well
on the programme some genius got to work,
and the music was produced as “Britannia
Overture—Allitsen.” Next day the Ballarat
Courier said, “The overture simply teemed
with patriotic fervor.” Hoch !
“Norbell”: He was a side-showman o£
variegated experience, and had toured with
everything, from the cigarette-smoking
oyster to the man-eating zoophyte. I sat
beside him on a long and wearisome train
journey, and he told me things. “Me pre-
sent stunt is fleas, performin’ fleas. Easiest
gag in the wide, ’n’ yer can travel yer com-
pany in a matchbox. I’ve got mine ’ere”
(he tapped his watch pocket). “Ye’ve on’y
got ter twist a bit of ’airspring wire roun’
their ’oppin’ appyratters, ’n’ there y’ are!
Then yer puts a dab o’ beeswax on their
tails —just a sensashun, y’ know. It sorter
tickles ’em. When yer wants ’em ter get a
move on yer just breathes on their rudders,
’n’ that warms the wax ’n’ starts ’em goin’.
I’ve got one big bloke runs along a little
tight-rope. Another one climbs a pole, ’n’
I’ve got a team of dog-fleas that drags a little
lorry aroun’ as nach’ral as a brewery wag-
gin. I’ve got a comedian, too. I tethers
’im up to a ’arf-inch nail with a bit o’ fine
sewin’ silk, ’n’ I ses: ‘Ladies ’n’ gen’lemen,
if you’ll give me yer kind attenshun for ’arf
a mo’ I’ll interdooce ter yer notice Bertie,
the clown, the most larfable performin’ flea
in captivity. Bertie was born in Yurong-
street, Woolloomooloo, ’n’ was trained by
Professor Chucklebung, from ’oom I pur-
chased ’im for 50 quid,’ I ses, ’n’ so on. Then
I blows on Bertie, ’n’ ’e begins to ’op like
mad, ’n’ the mugs larf like ’Ell. Yes, it’s
a great slug—better’n Dirty Dora, the girl
that eats mud. J’ever ’ear of ’er? No?
Well, she just let ’er ’air down, V rolled in
the mud a while, ’n’ then they sat ’er in a
tub o’ dirt, ’n’ she’d eat a mixture of choc’-
late ’n’ water —grab it up in ’er ’ands. The
mugs thort it was mud an’ came ev’ry time
at a deener a ’ead. Then there was the
Wild Man uv tli’ Woods ’oo eats mice ’n’
snakes, ’n’ ” But that was my station,
and I got out.

“Job” writes: —
In one of the capital cases disposed of by Justice
Sly in the Criminal Court, Sydney, at the beginning
of September the Judge approved of the jury’s recom-
mendation to mercy, and said he would forward it
to the Executive. In the meantime he pronounced
sentence of death on the prisoner. Some time—it
may be a considerable time—must elapse before the
Executive will deal with the subject, and William
John King will occupy the condemned cell until the
hour of official notification arrives. A thousand to
one may be safely wagered that the prisoner will not
be hanged. If he is to be reprieved from the rope
is it fair to keep him on his plank bed with
manacled hands? It is supposed to be necessary to
tie him up lest he would cheat the gallows by suicide.
He very likely does not know that he will not be
hanged, thus the terrors of death and official mana-
cling are imposed upon him under practically false
pretences. The interval between sentence and com-
mutation has on some occasions stretched out to a
month or six weeks. In one celebrated case (but
that was owing to an appeal on the prisoner’s behalf)
the interval covered nearly a year. If a man is to be
hanged the old style of doing it next day or within
a few days has much to be said in its favor ; if he
is not to be hanged, or probably not to be hanged,
he should not be doomed to the anxieties and mental
torture of the condemned cell. Another thing,
Justice Sly promised to forward the recommenda-
tion of mercy, but he need not have done so. He is
not obliged by law to do so. Some Australian Judges
have refused to pass on such a recommendation
(though his Honor Sly would never do that). The
law should be altered in this respect. The recommen-
dation of a jury should be an integral part of the
verdict. When a Judge is called before the Executive
to advise in the case of a capital sentence he often
tries to confine himself solely to his notes. His
notes and his judgment he tries to make supreme.
Judges have turned sulky before the Executive when
anything like cross-questioning was employed. The
Executive waited in one case for a Judge’s death
before it gave merited release to a prisoner whom his
Horsetail had sentenced years before. That sacrosanct
claim of the Bench must be cut away in the interests
of jurors as well as of prisoners. The tendency of
authority is to stand up for the judicial claim, and
only that now and again Executives have shown back-
bone, more injustices would have been perpetrated
against jurors and prisoners.
No skits on cable news can be more humor-
ous than the truth these times. The news
burlesques itself. Here are some contra-
dictory items clipped from Melbourne jour-
nals (mostly the ’Erald) from day to day
last week: —
The following cable message has been received by
the Minister for External Affairs from the High Com-
missioner’s office: “This crowning victory of Lemberg
means that Russia and Servia have accounted for prac-
tically half the Austro-Hungarian army. Germany
cannot expect help from her ally.”
(PUBLISHED IN “THE TIMES.”)
London, Sunday.
Amsterdam reports large movements of Germans in
Northern Belgium, including Austrian troops.
Austrian troops have moved from Lorraine to fill
gaps in the German ranks.
(PUBLISHED IN “THE TIMES.”)
London, Sunday, 9.55 p.m.
It is stated by Vossische Zeitung, of Berlin, that
the calling of all adults to arms has smashed German
industry.
London, Sunday, 11.20 p.m.
Germany is flooding Italy with commercial travel-
lers in order to retain her trade, which Great Britain
threatens to capture.
London, Monday, 3.20 a.m
It is stated that the Germans are actively bombard-
ing Nancy, the capital of Meurthe-et-Moselle, 220
miles east of Paris by rail and 94 miles west of
Strasburg, the capital of the German imperial terri-
tory of Alsace-Lorraine. The Kaiser, it is added, is
watching the bombardment.
London, Wednesday.
A German aeroplane dropped a bomb in Nancy, but
caused no injury, except that many windows in the
town were shattered.
The poor old Emperor of Austria was. at
latest, in “good health,” after having “died
12 days ago.”

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ceaseless patter
Of hurried feet
Out in the street,
So cheerless and cold.
See them scatter —
The slaves of gold!
When they are old
What will it matter?
We’re careless and gay
In the cafg’s glow—
For the world’s a show
For youth to see;
And we live to-day—
While we're young and free.
Care is the Cat —and Mice are we:
But Old Care’s away!
When youth is 501 d....
All bent and shattered,
Illusions scattered,
Weary feet,
And heart’s as cold
As the rain and sleet....
Out in the street,
When we are old !
N.S.W.

PENEOLDS
WINES
“10-2-4,” his Theory :
Far be it from me to posture as a military expert.
Like most of our kerbstone strategists I have not
been to any war. But arguing from what one knows
of everyday life, it would seem likely that the men
who have been in the forefront of the European
trouble, during the last month, must be losing their
nerve. A battle, under modern conditions, appears,
by all accounts, to be a hundred per cent, more
terrifying than the leisurely little rows which raged
during the second half of the eighteenth and the
beginning of the nineteenth centuries. Where sol-
diers fell in hundreds then, they are now slain in
thousands. And the holocaust is accompanied by a
nerve-racking din made up of screaming shell and
bursting lyddite and the rattle of innumerable mus-
ketry. One of the British war correspondents, accord-
ing to a recent cable message, described the spectacle
of a mere minor engagement as “unthinkably awful,”
and mentioned that he feared to close his eyes lest
A DISTINCTION.
THE BENCH : “You are charged with being drunk. How do you plead?"
THE DRUNK : “ Not guilty, your Worship. When I'm drunk and know I'm drunk
I’m NOT drunk , but when I’m drunk and don’t know Tm drunk lam drunk."
he would see it again. What is the mental effect of it
all on the participants? Strong men often emerge
from a Sunshine disaster, or other big railway smash,
in a condition of nervous wreckage, though the
whole thing was over in a few seconds perhaps, and
though .they were unhurt themselves. The skipper
of the Empress of Ireland became hysterical when he
was landed after the wreck. Many survivors of the
Titanic are said to be unstrung to this day. Eight
years ago I travelled from Colombo to Port Said
with a number of Russians who were returning from
Port Arthur. One, a naval doctor, was melancholy
mad. He had been rescued by the Japs, from a
battleship they had sunk. Most of the others were
more than eccentric. Our ship’s doctor told me they
were suffering from shock, and would take months,
perhaps years, to recover. One sees this tendency in
the most trifling things. A gallant and determined
boxer like Dave Smith is knocked out quickly by a
McGoorty. He can never face McGoorty., with confi-
dence, again. A cross-country rider has a bad fall.
For weeks afterwards his heart is in his mouth at
every fence. If the nerve of notably brave men —
men, that is, whose business it is to be courageous—
can be shattered temporarily by such comparative
trifles as a fall from a horse or a punch in the
jaw, what must be occurring in the case of the very
average i len who make up an army? Unless human
nature is different at “the front” from anywhere else,
the Germans who have been battling through Bel-
gium should be now due for the scrap-heap, in the
shape of a hospital for nervous breakdown subjects.
Speaking as one who knows nothing whatever about
the subject, it seems to me to be an ideal moment
for the allies to hurl at the enemy all the Aus-
tralians, Canadians, Territorials and other troops
who have not had their confidence sapped, and their
nerves shattered, by personal experience of the terrors
and hideousnesses of war. A pugilist of little skill
can utterly rout a champion, provided the latter has
the “Indian Sign” on him. If most of the German
troops now in France have not some affliction of the
sort, after their dreadful experiences, they are some-
thing more than human beings.

Said the German woolbuyer in a Sydney

rikin in a Sydney hotel: “No need to go to

The new far-northern Anglican bishopric
in South Australia is to be called Willochra.
The point was only settled after a long dis-
cussion in the Synod, where it was men-
tioned that in the tiny township already
bearing that name there is “not a single
Anglican.” “The more need for a Bishop.”
(Laughter.) A Voice: “And there is not
even a pub there.” (Renewed laughter.)
Flinders, and Torrens, and Central Aus-
tralia were among the names suggested, but
the native word—which seems to mean
“plenty green-bush”—got a majority of
votes. So far, good ; but the argument that
turned the scale was the lamentable one
that in cadging for English subscriptions a
native title works like a charm. “The name
of ‘Booboorowie’ had a great effect.
’Willochra’ will get £l5, where ‘Flinders’
would only get 10.” (Laughter.) This
world is full of laughter, and some of it is
like the crackling of burnt asses under a
pot.
If the Censor’s office wants to do some
immediate good to the community—as dis-
tinguished from the benefit which it may
be doing the Empire by withholding news of
potential value to the enemy—it will blue-
pencil, or at least drastically sub-edit, those
cable messages which describe the alleged
atrocities done by the Germans upon their
opponents. Many of them are probably
lies. In any case, it does no earthly good
to retail them. The war would not be con-
ducted with a fragment more, or less, vigor
if our papers contained nothing but stories

of how the Germans were cutting off
women’s heads by the score, and impaling
inoffensive children on bayonets and (ances.
The only effect of these improbable yarns
is to be seen in the following Melbourne
newspaper item of last week : —-
When three Germans put in an appearance near
the Stevedores’ Club in the afternoon a melee
occurred. Some of the crowd attacked the three
foreigners, but the rest of the stevedores protected
them, and they escaped with a few bruises and a
great scaring.
Members of the Stevedores’ Club stopped work
earlier than usual this evening, and held a special
meeting, at which they declared that no work would
be carried out by them in compaiw with Germans, or
under German foremen. The instant dismissal of
three German working foremen is to be insisted upon.
There is an Australian of German descent
employed at the place where the writer
usually lunches. He seems a peaceable
youth, and he knows no more about Ger-
many or German ways than a tram-car, and
yet he has had a contused eye pretty well
ever since the war began. He has the in-
flammatory cable items about alleged Ger-
man atrocities to thank for his injuries.
By the simple process of wiping out about
three cablegrams per diem, the Censor will
save the police authorities in places like
Melbourne and Sydney the expenditure of
much time and energy. To understand how
necessary this is, it is only requisite for the
Censor to remember what our guardians of
law and order are. Incidentally, it is worth
while to remember that the British royal
family is almost wholly German.
The M.L. Shipping Co.’s liner Kaipara,
one of the remarkably few victims of Ger-
man raiders on the high seas, would have
been a very valuable prize if the enemy had
been able to retain it. From Maoriland it
carried 28,800 carcases of mutton, 23,500
carcases of lamb, 3740 quarters and 748 bags
of beef, 3233 bales of wool and 1314 casks
of tallow. But as Bull’s navy has a mort-
gage on the sea round all the German ports
the Kaipara was a white elephant to its
captors. This shows what command of the
sea means. A few stray British mercantile
boats may fall into the hands of the Ger-
mans before all the hostile cruisers and
privateers are accounted for, but the enemy
is powerless to make use of its captures.
Britain, on the other hand, has raked in
hundreds of fine German steamers and has
secured the cargoes and the vessels, and, in
addition to robbing the enemy, has feathered
its own nest to the tune of millions.
“Thermometer”: In the midst of atrocities
—real and imaginary—it is distinctly cheer-
ing to read that the Cologne Gazette,
which appears to be as good a snuffler as
the next paper, objects to British prisoners
of war being allowed to play tennis when
“they would be better occupied thinking of
their sins and their country, which has
plunged Germany into war.” Such items
suggest the press volcano that labored and
brought forth a curate and a cup of after-
noon tea.
“Carajo”: “Billy Blue” (B. 3/9/’l4)
says “It is over a century since a prize
court was first held in Sydney,” and pro-
ceeds to cite one that sat in March, 1805.
History tells of many before that date. The
Nostra Senora de Bethlehem, of Spain, was
“condemned as a lawful prize” by a Vice-
Admiralty Court in Sydney on May 1, 1799.
The El Plumier met with a like fate on
December 7, 1799, and the Euphemia, Anna
Josepha and others followed. Prize courts
were almost as common as drunks in Syd-
ney before the dawning of the 19th century.
* * * *
The last act of the Eveleigh Sydney
hold-up case and robbery case consisted of
an application to Justice Sly, of N.S.W., by
the Crown Prosecutor for an order for the
return of two bundles of Federal notes valued
at £5BO. The order was made; but one of
the ladies in the case was enabled to retain
25 half-sovereigns because she wasn’t an
accused person. The case uncovered a point
for all those who object—perhaps on reli-
gious grounds—to handle Commonwealth
notes. Notes can be traced —gold cannot.
But what honest man is going to publicly
object to traceable money?
“The Old Soldier”: The ofncial British
casualty returns are ominous. Consider the
first lot:—- *
Eighteen officers have been killed, 78 wounded and
86 are missing. Of other ranks, 52 have been killed,
312 wounded, and 4672 are listed as missing, of
whom 2682 have been tent to the base as unfit for
active service.
Then they are not missing, because the
authorities know exactly where they are.
Missing men are those who cannot be
accounted for, and should be, in the present
war, generally wounded men who have been
left behind by their own army, and picked
up by the stretcher parties of the pursuing
army. The proportion of officers killed and
wounded is enormous when compared with
that of other ranks. It is 96 to 364, or
more than 26 per cent., when the ordinary
proportion of combatant officers in a British
army is only about 5 per cent. - As for the pro-
portion of missing officers to other ranks, it is
quite negligible, a fraction under 2 per cent.
What does it all mean ? The second list con-
tains the names of 16 officers who are known
to have been killed, 58 wounded and 94 who
are reported missing. So the proportion of
missing officers is apparently increasing—
that is, of those whom the British stretcher
parties cannot pick up, but who have to be
left to the tender mercies of the Germans.
THE BLUSH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ur. Keane, giving an address before a teetotal body
connected with one of the Melbourne churches,
pointed out that drinking habits induce excessive
blushing, and declared that persons of both sexes who
indulged in alcoholic stimulants are much more given
+ o blushing than abstainers are.
So many men have climbed the height
The shocking tale of boose to shout,
To wildly whirl their arms and smite
Old Alcohol, and lay him out,
That ’tis a pleasing change to hear
One learned gent
Who is content
To say a kindly word for beer.
The man who takes his whisky neat
And takes it often is, it seems,
More modest than the one you meet,
Who clings to tea and never dreams
Of trying wine. Now, don’t say “Tush!”
Let science show
The fact it> so,
As proven by the booser's blush.
That healthy, glowing nose you see
Upon the man who swallows gin,
Although it burns aggressively,
Is modesty incarnadine.
It stands for virtue, and appears
To be the bright
And shining light
The Church has lost these many years.
VICTORIA.

There have been some idiotically super-
fluous war cables, but the following, which
appeared in Sydney Sun (9/9/T4), was
perhaps the limit: —-
Baron Vonichenk has established himself in an
imposing suite* of rooms in the leading hotel at
Athens. . ....
He possesses a bottomless purse, and daily distri-
butes Germanised news manufactured by Woolf s
Bureau in Berlin. The Athens newspapers, however,
refuse to publish the information he sends to them.
Not a word as to who Vonichenk is, or how
he puts in his time when there is no war on.
Just a bald picture of a futile nobleman,
writing furiously to the papers from a pub,
and having all his ‘contributions basketed
on sight. The laconic intimation that an
imbecile baron was staying in AJhens would
have been as informative and in every re-
spect as satisfying to the news-seeker.
Another cable in the morning press men-
tioned that a Hamburg butcher had covered
the floor of his shop with Union Jacks.
Also a Sydney butcher, who trades near
The Bulletin office, has an obscene-look-
ing bullock’s tail, freshly torn from its late
owner, hanging in his window, together
with the inscription, “This is how the
Kaiser’s tail will look shortly.” If this
item hasn’t already been cabled to London,
to cheer the heart of Empire, it certainly
will be. It is just the sort of damphoolish-
ness that seems to appeal to those who deal
in cable messages.

By an unlucky coincidence two big sailing
ships were in the vicinity of New Caledonia
early in August when the French Governor
decided to extinguish all the coast lights.
These were the Maoriland-owned barque
Titan fa and the Russian barque Aland.
Neither master was aware that the lights
were out, not knowing even of the war, and
both ships sailed slap into the butt end of
the island. They are still there. The
Titania was skippered by F. Holm, a sturdy
old mariner who has sailed backwards and
forwards across the Tasman Sea almost ever
since there was any Tasman Sea. (Some
say before, but that is doubtful.) This
accident is the first that he has ever met
with, and his language about the cause of it
would melt a clothes-prop.
Teutonian brag is the main factor in the
Australian “set” against the German mem-
bers of the Port Phillip Stevedores’ Associa-
tion at Port Melbourne. These men are
mostly old residents, long-time naturalised,
and their mates would have rubbed along
amicably enough with them if they had dis-
cussed the situation in a moderately partisan
spirit. They might have talked _ German
facts and figures and got an attentive hear-
ing. The Australians and Anglo-Australians
were open to receive instruction as to Ger-
man militarism and its natural bearings on
the trouble in France, where the British
army is. But all they got from the ex-
subjects of the Kaiser was the old brag
that is born in them. The Deutschland
Uhcr Alles sentiment oozed from those sons
of arrogance even when they didn’t skite
about der invinsibul Sherman Army and
vot vas going to habben to der Bridish.
Hence the fighting. Being surrounded by
the enemy, the Sheripans didn’t have to
look far for trouble. One man, who was
taken to Melbourne Hospital, suffering
from a gashed brow, is the naturalised
father of a full-grown Australian-born
family, yet he was always making himself
darned unpleasant concerning his soldier-
governed Farderland. German patriotism
is of the rooster sex. Fatherland
crows and struts and is intolerant of other
roosters, whereas Motherland patriotism is
content to cluck and raise chickens.
“Quetelet” : —-
The incompetence of the quidnuncs ! A Melbourne
paper last week took the utmost pains to gather por-
traits of 25 cardinals, and gave an assurance that
the new Pope must be among them. Yet Benedict
was not included when it seemed a blank impossi-
bility to miss the shot. On the day Assyrian won
the Cup a Melbourne morning paper gave a com-
plete list of all the possible starters —barring
Assyrian. General U. S. Grant was entered by his
uncle at West Point Military Academy as Ulysses
Simpson. His real name was Hiram Ulysses. When
the error . was discovered the family said, “Let the
U.S. remain. It may be lucky.” Richelieu founded
the French Academy, on the ample basis of
40 members, to include all the best French
writers. Yet the best have been left out, so that
there is the common sarcasm of the Forty-First
Fauteuil for France’s most eminent author.
“Leges”: Solicitor-General J. W. Davis,
of the United States, has just visited the
Privy Council in London, and is mightily
impressed by its simplicity. He had
thought the U.S. Courts
could give points to all
in that way, but finds
they have much to learn.
A number of the Aus-
tralian Supreme Court
judges have visited the
London Appeal Court,
and several of them
have given testimony
similar to Davis’s, but
the fact remains that
Australia is far behind
even the United States
in simplicity of proce-
dure. The judges of
the highest British
Court wear neither wigs
nor gowns ! Are such
trappings only to im-
pose on the vulgar?
Idowever, the upholstery
is a minor point, com-
pared with the common
sense. Cases which take
days in Australia are
treated in an hour or
two by the London Ap-
peal Court. There are
no set orations, with
judges merely looking
on and blinking, mak-
ing an occasional note,
and possibly doing an
occasional nap. The
proceedings are entirely
conversational. No man
rises and sweeps his
gown behind him and
holds out his right hand
at a horizontal angle,
and starts to hold forth
for a week. He simply
says what he has got to
say. If he starts to fill
up time by saying what
he hasn’t got to say he
is pulled up short.

George Reid’s unfortunate personal
appearance has found a champion in the
Melbourne Argus. The Melbourne paper
complains that “the picture of the High
Commissioner now added to the Common-
wealth gallery is absurdly like a caricature,
which is a great pity.” Also the Argus
is indignant because “the historical picture
of the opening of the first Commonwealth
Parliament” represents George as an un-
comfortable, squeezed-in person
with a libellously red nose. But
this last complaint ignores the
fact that the picture in question
is not accepted as the real his-
torical record of the event at
Melb. Exhibition building.
Charles Nuttall’s work in sepia,
prepared from photographs for
photogravure reproduction, _is
the historical picture, which,
with few exceptions, accurately
presents things as they were.
George Reid here has his proper
place in the third row of
Federal Parliamentarians facing
the dais. The likeness is a
piece of'flattery rather than a
caricature. Nuttall contrived to
reduce George’s stummick to
non - aggressive proportions
whilst preserving the character-
istic pose of the figure. His
“admirers,” of whom the Argus speaks with
sympathy, can gaze upon that faithful like-
ness of the man’s face and garments, and
rejoice to think that posterity will get an
inadequate idea of his vest measurement.
Detective Whittle, of the Adelaide force,
has been able to get damages from an
obscure weekly sporting paper which “went
for” him with a clumsy and thick-headed
half-brick. “Weary Whittle’s wits want
whittling” was one remark objected to.
Judge Gordon said much of the article might
pass as “a silly attempt to be funny” ; but
distinct charges of “nourishing spite” and
“being a coward in the performance of his
duty” were worth £3O damages. A bright
spot about the hearing of the case was that
someone apparently played an unkind trick
on the Register’s reporter, and caused him
to print the well-known detective’s name as
“John Hop Whittle.”
“The Link”: The theosopliical idea of
the origin and descent of man is marvel-
lously set forth in a book called “Man,
Whence, llow, Whither?” This is the joint
production of xlnnie Besant and C. IV.
Leadbeater, and therefore represents the
very core of theosophic truth. In their
quest for man’s origin the authors have
gone back to life on the moon, when the
men and women of the. future were “monkey
creatures” ; and among those ancestral apes
they have identified themselves, as well as
“Alcyone,” their “coming Christ,” and his
brother. Teaching a general principle by
concrete examples, Mrs. Besant and her
lieutenant proceed to trace their own re-
spective careers in many lives, to
illustrate, the blessed doctrine of re-
incarnation. In the narrative, in
order to prevent confusion, Mrs.
Besant is throughout called “Her-
akles” and Mr. Leadbeater “Sirius.”

liuman” stage, ller-
akles becomes, 12
lives later, a labor-

such garbage, but
having compensa-
tion in the shape
of “a whole pack
of brothers as hus-
bands.” One of
these husbands' is
identified as the
late Charles Brad-
laugli. It is not
mrnrisihg to learn
that, while she was
a woman in those
early days, she had
“v er y numerous
babies.” In a later
“i n c a r n a tion,”
when she was

War rages and politicians howl and curse,
but Knibbs goes on, impassively adding and
dividing and subtracting, and issuing the
results to the public. Among the latest in-
formation which he has unloaded on a
careless community is the news that last
year 516 males and 131 females committed
suicide. They mostly used firearms, but
a good number preferred poison. Women,
vain to the last, favor poison. They regard
it as less messy, and hence productive of a

year 1861 mothers, ranging in age from 17
to 45, earned the bonus by the agespey of
twins. Triplets were rather less frequent,
as is their habit the world over. The most
admirable character of the lot was a lady
of 41, who, in 22 years of married bliss, had
increased the population by 20. Last year
she made what was regarded, in her neigh-
borhood, as her supreme effort, and weighed
in with triplets.
SONG OF THE GRINDSTONE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A grindstone will be placed on board. .. .Officers
will see that all swords and bayonets are sharpened.
—Extract from military order.
There isn’t a cloud in the sky ;
There isn’t a smudge on the sea ;
But somebody’s going to die
Along of that baynit and me ;
So keep it pressed flat on the stone,
And pour on some water and turn ;
Will he choke? Will he drop with a moan?
Will he get it ahead or astern?
It's peaceful enough here on deck;
The fellows are all half-asleep.
(The Sergeant says: “Thrust at his neck—
It knocks a man all of a heap !”)
You’re getting an edge on it now 1
Let’s feel it,. Bill! Cut yourself? Gee:
Say, it makes a bloke feel for the cow !
Say , but ivon’t it be Ilell if it’s ME !
N.S.W.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
“J.0’8.”: The Rev. Herbert P. Hare, of
Mount Morgan (Q.), when war was de-
clared, volunteered to go to the front (the
front hasn’t got any address; it rushes all
over the place like a bald lunatic with his
coat-tail on fire) with the first contingent in
the capacity of chaplain. There was com-
petition, however, for chaplain posts, and,
concluding that he might not find an opening
in that quarter till too late to meet Wilhelm,
he withdrew his application and became a
Tommy. Long ago lie was a private in a
British regiment, so he knows something of
Tommying.
LANGD ON BONYTHON, Adelaide ( S. A.).
Iu some respects the career of Joseph
Earle Hermann, the meteoric Sydney
financier who heard the bottom fall out of*
his glory the other day. recalls Ernest Terah
Hooley, of England. They were both very
sudden men, and apparently they were
equally incapable of keeping their accounts
in an intelligible shape. Ilooley’s books
looked as if they had been written up by a
cannibal or a whirlwind, and though he did
his record breakage many years ago no one
has really got the hang of his affairs to this
day.
The home of an old Sydney identity and
money spinner, John Ilardie, south of the
School of Arts, I’itt-street. was demolished
last week. The old gentleman carried on a
bakery on the George-street end of the block
—with a business that was established in
the late ’4o’s by George Wilkie, who also
ran the London Tavern adjoining, after
Mi’. Edward Samuel, he who opened the
lirst “private” bar. John Ilardie was an
alderman, and was Mayor of Sydney in
1884.
ALLIED AGAINST HIM.
WAR CORRESPONDENT: "Well, there seems to
be no difference of of inion among them
about me, at any rate
Now and then the old man gets a show
as well as the young one. General Ban,
who broke the centre of the German line in
the great battle outside Paris, while French
was busy bursting up the Teutons further
west, is a one-handed man. He lost a hand
fighting against the Germans in the war of
44 years ago, and he has been waiting for
satisfaction ever since.
Died at the age of 66, Ilenniker Heaton,
one of Australia’s small crop' of baronets.
He was born at Rochester (England), but
came to Australia early. Here he married
a daughter of the Sydney Evening News,
and developed into an heroic advertisement
canvasser, and made money, and inherited
money, and shoved things along marvellously.
By reason of a genius for the limelight he
became commissioner for almost everything
under the sun, and got a gold casket pre-
sented to him in London, and a silver casket
in Canterbury, and was banqueted and cabled
about to an amazing extent. With his Aus-
tralian money lie went back to England, and
became a Tory M.P., which job he held for
25 years. The peerage that he yearned for
never came. Ilenniker never made an enemy
if he could help it. nor did he ever miss a
chance to pour kindly grease over a possible
friend.
“Jeaines” writes :—-
The S. M. Herald, writing of Pertab Singh—
whom it called Singhj—said:—
He has gained himself a welcome in any gathering
of Englishmen in India. He is one whom Lord
Roberts saw' fit to shake by the hand and address as
“old friend.”
As though it were a patronising act on the part of
Englishmen to welcome him and a condescension on
Roberts’s part to grasp his hand. The fact is
Pertab Singh has always been more a sportsman than
a warrior, and as much a courtier as either. He was
best known till 20 years ago, or less, as a champion
polo-playjr. Also, he shared with the then Maharajah
of Cooch Behar the intimate friendship of Edward
VII. Fashionable Englishmen of a few generations
back did not merely welcome these two potentates;
they almost grovelled to them. Some reasons, were
that they were in a position to provide better polo,
shooting and so forth than any other men in India ;
and that not to come to India recommended to them
was equivalent to being an admitted nonentity,
socially, in London. A weakness of Lord Roberts —
which, by the way, neither Kitchener nor French has
ever shared—is a vast respect for people who are
popular at Court. His staffs have alwaj's been more
noted for birth than brains. Pertab Singh, as a
man whom British royalties have always delighted
to honor, would have been sure of Roberts’s friend-
ship had he ne>»r seen a shot fired in anger.
By “Solomon Malaprop” :
A late cablegram states that the ancient Emperor
of Austria is feeling very well. There is no reason to
disbelieve the statement. St. Peter is also feeling
very well, likewise Paul and Silas and Jeremiah and
Moses and other people. The old Austrian potentate
was up against ah impossible set of circumstances lor
66 years, and he did about as good work as any man
could do when he was appointed to keep the peace
among 18 nations that hated each other like the very
Devil. Anybody who got to Heaven after a life of
that sort would feel very well.
Signed journalism breaks out in curious
places. The other day a fraud who was
driving a motor-car in a Sydney street col-
lided with and bruised a lady. His name
was demanded, and presumably lie racked
his brain for one that would suit. Then he
evidently remembered a signature he saw in
The Bulletin —that of a man whose worst
vice is the writing of political verse—and
lie gave that. Last Monday the writer was
disturbed in the centre of his best poem by
the arrival of the bill.
“Jeaines” on some prominent warriors
alive and dead
The Duke of Westminster, who appears to have
earned the first V.C. of the war, and who followed it
up by a venturesome effort in a motor-car, is the
despair of British wowserdom. 'the genuine British
wowser leaps, as though stung, every time the Duke's
name is mentioned. He resembles Max Beerbohm’s
Lord George Hell, who, when virtuous women raised
their skirts in horror as he passed, was wont to stop
and through his eyeglass lightly appraise their ankles,
lhe Duke is not yet 40, and has just been separated
from his wife. He is one of the best horsemen in
England, but being tall and heavy, has had to forego
steeplechasing of late years. He helped to finance the
polo team,before last that went to New York, and
announced that he would eat his hat or his boots, or
something, if they didn’t bring back the ashes. They
got well beaten, bat I never. heard that he ate any-
thing unusual. The De Crespigny who was killed
with the Queen’s Bays comes of a famous fighting
family. He is a son of the amazing old baronet
(Sir Claude) whose life has been one. prolonged
uproar. All the Dc Crespignys are in the army,
barring one who is a sailor, and whom Sydneyites will
remember as the rider of several steeplechasers at
Randwick 12 or 15 years ago. The Brooke who was
killed at the same time as Captain de Crespigny is a
son of Rajah Brooke, of Sarawak, and was in the 16th
Lancers. The original Brooke settled down on Sara-
wak and made himself a kind of king. The present
man is his nephew. Prince Ernest of Saxe-Meningen—
also dead —was elderly and poor, and the husband of
a lady with neither money nor birth to render her
acceptable, but he was one of the most popular Ger-
man princelings in Europe. His morganatic marriage,
20 years ago, nearly caused his relatives of the
Kaiser’s side of his family to burst with annoyance.
And then, his mother having died, his father pro-
ceeded to marry morganatically also. By the way,
the assumption of the local papers that the British
cavalry regiments are officered by “aristocrats” is
absurd. The S. M. Herald said on Saturday, for
example:—■
The British aristocracy has in the past weeks
answered all the criticisms directed against it.
With the exception of a few regiments such as the
9th Lancers and the 10th Hussars, the officers of the
cavalry arm are mostly well-to-do bourgeois. The
4th Dragoon Guards, for example, which seems to
have done notably well during the retreat from
Mons, used to be known as the Trade Union. Three-
quarters of its officers drew their incomes from busi-
nesses of one sort or another.
OBIT—SEPTEMBER 5.
And in death they were not divided.
[Done by FRANK JESSUP, 5 Cary-street, Leich-
hardt, Sydney.]
It is mentioned by cable that, during one
of the battles when .Toffre and French were
turning the tide, the weary Kaiser sat on
his horse for two hours in dead silence
watching the whirl of events. Never before
since his accession has he been silent for
two hours in his waking moments. All that
time he neither issued an ultimatum, or
composed an opera, or founded a new civi-
lisation, or re-organised the army, or did
anything. It reads like the story of a
broken man.
Sir Sydney Buxton, the new G.-G. of
S’Africa, is an offshoot of the beerocraey.
Truman, Hanbury and Go., in which the
head of the Buxton family used to be a
partner, was one of the first of the big
brewery firms in England.
DICKY HAYNES, K. C. , Perth.
“B.” writes :—-
What I admire about these German generals is
their ability to rise superior to circumstances. Take
Yon Emmich. The cables told of this brave man,
humiliated by the first successes of the despised
Belgians, writing a heartrending farewell to his wife
in Berlin and then committing suicide. Three
weeks later he recovered sufficiently to receive the
sword of the Commandant of the Liege forts when he
surrendered to the Germans. Von Emmich's contempt
of death was equalled by Von Buelow. The cable
told a detailed story of how a Belgian youth, at
Louvain, slew Von Buelow, stole his pants and took
135,000 francs from the defunct General’s coat.
King Albert took the francs and allowed the young
man to keep the pants. On September 12 Von
Buelow (in a pair of borrowed trousers, it would
seem) conducted a retreating mob of Germans across
the Marne. These men are good examples of what
the German military system can produce.
Registrar Atkins, the “Lay Bishop of
Sydney.” has been presented with an ad-
dress signed by the Archbishop and mem-
bers (clerical and lay) of the Standing
Committee of the Archdiocese. He entered
the service of the Chapter House in the
first year of Bishop Barker (1554), and
has been hard at it ever since. When he
started clerical toil there were but five
bishoprics in Australia (Sydney, Melbourne,
Adelaide, Tasmania and Newcastle). Now
there are 21. The Lay-bish. never did any-
thing madly sensational in his time —he
didn’t build a local Milan cathedral, or
propound a new doctrine of faith, or any-
thing like that—but he has been a notable
stayer and a credit to his church. Hence
the parchment.
A brief word about a recently-dead Aus-
tralian expeditioner:—
Dr. Brian Antill Pockley, aged 24, who was killed
•at Simpsonhaven, New Britain, had soldiering blood
in his body, derived from the Antill side of the
family. Two brothers, Major John Antill and Edward
Antill, fought on opposite sides in the U.S. War
of Independence—Major John Antill on the side of the
British. His son, Henry Golden Antill, born in New
York, in 1779; entered the British army (73rd
Regiment) in 1796. He served in India against
Tippoo Sahib, and carried the regimental colors at
the storming of Seringapatam in May, 1799, where
he was wounded. He got his company in January,
1809, and came to Australia with the regiment and
Colonel Macquarie (Governor of N.S.W.) in the same
year. He was A.D.C. until 1815, when he became
Major of Brigade. In 1825 he retired on half-pay,
and went to reside on his military grant at Jarvis-
fuld, Picton, named after Macquarie’s estate in Scot-
land. There he died on August 14, 1852. He named
Picton after the Peninsular General killed at Water-
loo. His grandfather, on the male side, was Captain
Robert Pockley, of the mercantile marine, who, after
commanding several vessels on the Australian const,
settled at St. Leonards House (recently demolished),
North Shore. His son, the father of deceased Brian,
gained his diploma in 1884, and built up a very fine
practice in Sydney. Colonel Lassetter married a
daughter of the house of Antill. A sister of Captain
Brian Pockley was married in London on July 21
to Lieutenant-Commander Clinton Danbv, R.N.
A GREAT SEND-OFF.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A pathetic incident marked the death of the officer
who was fafall.v shot by a patrol of Uhlans while
carrying orders to Field-Marshal Sir John French.
He accompanied the Duke of Westminster in an
automobile, and, when mortally wounded, the officer
rose and attempted to salute,' but fell back dying.
Seeing his companion collapse, the Duke rose" and
saluted the dying officer, saying, “Good-bye, my boy.”
War item.
The captain sat in a motor-car,
A British duke beside
(For when a duke goes out to a war
He wants a motah ride,
And he feels, as ne’er he felt before,
A sense of ducal pride).
A German shot at the captain grim,
Beside the swell recruit.
The shot struck home to the heart of him.
His Grace exclaimed: “The brute 1”
And the dying man, his eyes grown dim,
Tried vainly to salute.
His strength gave out ere his arm could raise
His hand unto his head.
Then rose the Duke, and his ducal gaze
Was perfectly well-bred,
As be said, with pluck beyond all praise,
“Good-bye, my boy,” he said.
’Tis sad to know that a German thus
Can human life destroy,
Though such a fate we can all discuss
With thoughts of pride and joy.
Oh, would that a noble duke to us
Might say: “Good-bye, my boy” 1

After occupying the general manager’s office
stool for 21 years, J. 11. B. Coates now
retires. When he began the bank’s capital
was £250,000 and the reserves nil. Various
amounts have been got in since then, and
early in the co.’s last year shareholders
were offered a further 100,000 shares (£lO
paid to £2 10s.) at a premium of £1 ss.
per_ share. This brings the capital to
£750,000, and the disclosed reserves to
£050,000. The bank certainly had a stroke
of luck to get Ibis money in when it did —
it would not be quite such a simple matter
to induce the public to hand out the
£375,000 just at present. Besides the
£050,000 there are no doubt various undis-
closed reserves. For iusiance, as the retir-
ing G.M. mentions, in 1894 premises and
furniture stood at £126,000; since then
over £lOO,OOO has been spent on erections
sind purchases, yet the item now stands at
only £85.750, so there is a pretty consider-
sible inner reserve there. Besides that, vari-
ous large amounts arc regularly either
written off securities or put to the Pensions
sind Gratuity Fund, which now has a capi-
tal value of £95,100. The result is that on
shareholders’ capital and reserves combined,
tlie profit for the past year is at the rate
of £9.5 p.c. No doubt the profit for the
year lias been abnormal, irrespective of the
extra capital; and it is as well to notice
that the 13 p.c. on increased capital will
call for £97,500 (a figure which profits have
reached on only one occasion). So if Maori-
hind meets a set-back the big dividend will
probably be in danger.
This shows how the bank stands at pre-
sent —a particularly important matter, when
one considers the inevitable hard times that
■are ahead :

There’s certainly no reason for shareholders
or depositors to shudder when they look
down those figures. The bank has total lia-
bilities of £5,400,227. and of that amount
£2,527,394, or over 40 p.c., is held liquid.
Further, the shareholders’ funds (capital
and reserves) amount to £1,458,317; so
that for every £3 14s. lOd. that the bank
owes to the public, it has £1 of shareholders’
money. This remarkably strong position is,
of course, due to the recent big issue. So.
taken all round, nothing short of an excep-
tional national calamity is likely to put the
Maoriland bank on its beam-ends.

How long the consumers are going to stand
it. The Bulletin doesn’t know. A lot
depends on the way the next Queensland
elections go. If the pendulum swings, and
the Labor party goes in, it is more than
likely that Queensland will get: a Gas Act
similar to the N.S.W. measure; and it is
very nearly time it did. The present divi-
dend is something over 10 p.c. on the capital
that came out of shareholders’ pockets. And
he is a particularly mild gas-burner who. isn’t
going to revolt at that. Further, any
Government that will cause these northern
gas cos. to turn out decent accounts will be
flirice blessed. At present the financial
statements are bare, and the wind blows
through them like it does through a ceme-
tery.
The capital is now £240,000. Four years
back it was £160,000, and at least £31,842
of the added £BO.OOO was provided by con-
sumers, who now have the pleasure of pay-
ing 12 p.c. dividend on if. Just what a
good wicket the co. has been on may be
judged from these figures :

carrying out some big additions and im-
provements to plant, etc., and the liquid
assets have in consequence suffered, though
£32,000 of new capital has been got in
meanwhile. Since December, 1912, the
“plant, land and building” item has moved
from £211,475 to £252,017. Yet even after
paying the regular fat dividends, and mak-
ing all renewals, extensions, etc., £10.581 has
gone back into the business in 10 years.
And some idea of the size of extensions may
be got from the fact that gas sales for the
past year were £41,538, while for the corres-
ponding term 10 years ago they were £21,293.
It, is almost too good to last, and if the
Federal election results are anything to go
by the chances are that it won’t last.

On the strength of what are obviously boom
years the directors hand out another 10 p.c.
dividend, notwithstanding that there is still
a mortgage of £45,000 on the premises, and
that no provision whatever has been made
to pay the big incubus off. The co. might
be able to stand that sort of finance now,
but when Victoria strikes a bad season or
two and the bottom falls out of the boom,
things have a very good chance of going
back to where they were five or six years
ago. Then the 1.0 p.c. will be an impossi-
bility, and the fat dividends of the past few
years will arise between 2 and 3 a.m. and
sit heavily on the co.’s chest. And when
the boss goes round to renew the mortgage
the gentleman who floated the loan will
probably remember that monish is tight on
account of the war and sundries, and will ask
for a little more interest, and the co., hav-
ing forgotten that the loan would some day
fall due, will have to pay. One could under-
stand a dividend at the present rate if the
business were a gamble or there were
a lease due to expire shortly and
it was necessary for shareholders to
get their capital back in a hurry.
But, of the capital of £125.000, no less than
£103,625 is in city freehold, and no one can
wish for better security, for their capital
than a freehold in the heart of Melbourne,
provided it isn’t over-valued. A reduction
to 8 p.c. would save £2500; and this, along
with, say, £lOOO a year from unappropri-
ated funds, would make a very considerable
hole in the bier mortgage in very few years.
And the co. would then make a very consid-
erable saving in interest. Before the pay-
ment of the £6250 of. dividend for the
second half of the year, the co. had liabili-
ties of £24,726 and liquid assets worth
£31,644. which is pretty close sailing.
Naturally, when a concern has practically
every penny it possesses, plus a large
amount of borrowed money in the business, it
ought to make big profits. But the share-
holders would be doing themselves a good
turn if they insisted on provision being made
to release the mortgage, and on a check being
put on dividends until a respectable reserve
is accumulated.

This is, of course, the "off” term, but the
half-year has been further off than usual,
owing to the increased expenditure neces-
sitated by the taking over of the Ilobart
reticulation scheme of the defunct Hydro-
Electric Co. This big concern started off
with a bang, but got financially bogged in
early infancy. Then the gas co., no doubt
remembering what happened to its poor
relation at Launceston when the electrical
competitor arrived, bought out the whole
show at bargain prices. Up till now it has
been mainly a case of pay out, and the co.
found it necessary to look for more cash.
So another £17,000 was got in—£9ooo from
shareholders, and £BOOO per medium of
debentures —making, in all. capital £lOB.OOO.
debentures £20,000. Besides that there is
an overdraft of £18,789. And the bulk of
the new money has gone into electric plant,
land and buildings, which have moved since
.Tune, 1913, from £17,954 to £49.971. But
the revenue from that branch has increased
only from £5941 to £7038 in the 12 months.
The co. is now free from competition, and
has a permanent cheap power supply at the
Big Lake. And the Hobart City Council
evidently intends to see that, now the gas
co, has got the second branch in its hands,
it doesn’t loaf. But there doesn’t seem
much danger of that, and if the electric-
lighting business is properly handled, share-
holders should be able to look forward to
something even more cheerful than the pre-
sent dividend. Gas sales continue to move
in the right direction, and show an im-
provement from £14,543 to £15,710 since
June, 1913. The co. will, however, have a
pretty considerable interest bill to face, and
The Bulletin will welcome an early attack
on the overdraft, and then the debentures.

Capital (£57,600) and reserves combined
amount to £80,745, and of this amount
£76,125 is represented by shares in Carlton
and United Breweries Ltd., alias the Brewery
Combine. Consequently apart from a few
pounds received as interest on deposits, etc.,
the co.'s only income is the dividend from the
Combine. Out of its shake (£3045 for the
past half-year) the Foster Co. can pay its
expenses, which are very small, hand out an
S p.c. dividend, and still have a couple of
hundred to put aside. The Combine came
about in this way. For some years up to
1907 the Melbourne breweries had been at
war with each other, and most of them had
begun to feel the effects of the keen competi-
tion before they realised the absurdity of it.
They put their heads together, and the Carl-
ton and United Breweries, which now in-
cludes six of the big breweries, was the out-
come. Capital is £1,000,000, and the new eo.
had the right to take over the premises of the
various companies. Foster’s had property
valued at just under £48,000, which was
taken over. But in 1912 the big concern
decided that it had no use for it, and paid the
Foster Co. £BOOO to call the deal off. Later,
in 1913, the property was sold. Up till a few
years ago the C. and U. B. Ltd. had the
field practically to itself. Now it is up against
the Co-operative Brewery. To what extent
its figures have been affected cannot be ascer-
tained, as the Great Beer Combine or Ale Con-
federation publishes no accounts. Nor is there
any means of finding with what ease or other-
wise it pays its dividend, and naturally this
concerns the shareholders in the combined cos.
considerably. If the Carlton is blown out,
Foster’s, and sonic of the others as well, must
follow it. The Foster Co., by the way, has
only £290 of liabilities as against £7183 of
liquid assets apart from Combine shares.

So there are not very many more properties
to be disposed of. and the life of the “profit
on sales” column is consequently limited.
More than that, the present general upset
created by the war has already put a damper
on property values, so it is quite possible
that remaining sales will not bring in the
fat returns that the co. has been getting
during recent years. However, the growth of
advances, unaccompanied fortunately by any
rapid growth of deposits, should fill up any
gap left in the profits column by the cessa-
tion of sales. When almost every Victorian
building society fell in owing to the collapse
of values in the early ’9o’s, this one was
among the sufferers. But it was an honest
concern despite its misfortunes. It struggled
on till 1001. when it wrote off a large slice
of capital, and cleaned up its slate. Now it
is a reformed sinner, leading a pure and
blameless life. Liabilities (mostly deposits)
are only £45,813, and there are £132.732 of
assets; which means that property values
would be able to shrink almost 70 p.c. before
the co. would be unable to face its creditors.
And shareholders have done the right thing
in building up a solid reserve and the co.
could now pay the present dividend com-
fortably if profits from sales were to cease
at once.

The woollen manufacturing cos. in Aus-
tralasia ought to lie making piles of money
these days, but none of them seem to be
doing it. And the two big things against
them are the habit Australia and M.L.
hnv(‘ uf buying the cheaply-made, shoddy
imported article, and the idea the worker
has that if the concern shows a bigger
profit than it did last year, the least it can
do is to allow him to work four hours less
per week and do less work while he is there,
and give him more pay. Then they both
wonder why the local factory shuts down.
The death-rate is especially great among
infant manufactories. But this Welling-
ton co. has long got over the teething stage,
though it is by no means the able-bodied
citizen it should be. Although 31 years old.
its reserves are only £31,375, arid the
trouble is that the co. knows perfectly well
that it will need more capital as it goes
on. The only way for such a co. to
attract new money is to show that it can
pay dividends on what it already has, and the
result is that it pays out much more than it
should. Capital has grown by various additions
from £80.340 in 1900 to £122,099, and,
besides • that, there ate big liabilities of
£05,417. Of that and capital no less than
£107,784 is in stocks, and £80,313 in plant,
buildings, etc. Yet with all that actually
in the business, the company can show only
the poor irregular profits in the table and
the poorer reserves. Even then the profit
is shown before any allowance has been
made for depreciation. And in this kind of
manufacturing co. plant either wears out-
very quickly, or must be replaced bv the
latest model to keep pace with the foreign
concern that pays starvation wages which
would make the most confirmed sweater out
here shudder. ' But the outlook seems
brighter now. and. when things have settled
down, Australians who have hitherto been
Freetraders and habitual importers may
perhaps (only perhaps) howl for a tariff
to keep out foreign goods. Others may-
even go so far as to boast of wearing Aus-
tralian-made ma teria Is.

Things are certainly looking up consider-
ably for this concern, and although on the
face of things the profits would appear
capable of standing a more gaudy dividend
the reserves had been so starved till a few
years back that the right thing is being
done in keeping the dividends down and giv-
ing reserves a chance. More than that,
there, appears to be no provision made for
depreciation—the sums shown in the table
as written off have been spent on renewals,
but it is hardly likely that these would
counterbalance depreciation of plant, build-
ings and meters, which now stand at
£77,370. Sales of gas, etc., have improved
greatly. Five years back plant, etc., stood
at £57,497, and gas sales were £5390, or
at the rate of 9.4 p.c. on the book value of
plant, etc. ; for the past six months sales
were £10,526. a return of 13.6 p.c. With
such a growth as this there should certainly
be something better ahead of shareholders,
but they will be wise to hold off until a
decent reserve has been accumulated, and
they make certain that plant, meters and
buildings are worth the £77.370 at which
they stand in the books. Naturally with
so large an amount represented by these
fixed assets (the co.’s capital is only
£60.000) the cash position is not altogether
brilliant. Liabilities, including a “tempo-
rary loan” of £13,000, amount to £14.933,
and there are liquid assets of £13.184, of
which stock represents £3429 and book
debts £5545. And a concern with so much
in revenue-producing plant, etc., ought to
be showing better results than the Brighton
co. is. But, as remarked before, things are
looking up. Finally, if the co. can’t show
other gas companies much in the business
line, there are few that turn out better
accounts.

The biggest movement is in “premises, plant,
etc.,” and the big spring in this item is
principally due to the acquisition of a new
property in Brisbane, a portion of which the
co. intends to dispose of as soon as a favor-
able opportunity- puts in an appearance.
Hotel property is still at a satisfactorily
small figure, and it is the brewery that can
resist the temptation to buy pub property
that is going to feel the least sorry for itself
later on. However it is very unlikely that
the various property items stand in the books
at more than their real value ; as a matter
of fact, they are probably worth more than
the co. takes credit for. For in the over-
haul of about 12 years ago just on half
the capital was written off, and the pro-
perty item was attacked pretty savagely.
Another pleasant feature is the big growth
of trade receipts as compared with the slight
advance in expenses, and if this can be kept
up, and the shareholders don’t ask for more
than the present highly satisfactory divi-
dend, the company should be able to face the
world with a much lighter heart than it must
now have with the big debts and what
appear to be stormy times ahead.
* * * *
It seems that there will be some dis-
closure at last of the affairs of Earle Her-
mann Ltd., of Sydney. About eight years
ago the head and tail and most of the middle
of this concern arrived in Sydney and got
busy as a co. promoter and stock-jobber,
lie was a young man with a large imagina-
tion —a person of airy manner and persua-
sive speech. He waved his arm in a lordly
way and did the thing well. His
company’s suite of offices was soon among
the most sumptuous in the city. Presumably
to account for his large imagination and
bis habit of talking in big figures it was
given out that he had been private secretary
to Cecil Rhodes in South Africa. He may
have been that or he may have been an
ironmongery salesman. After a few small
flutters, chiefly in mining, he took a hand in
a concern for the direct production of steel,
and canvassers pushed the thing in many
parts of Australia. Shares were sold at a
price that must have left Earle Hermann
a substantial profit. Magnificent promises
were made, and whenever something was
needed to counterbalance the non-production
of steel commercially it was announced that
the Duke of Westminster or the Ahkoond
of Swat or some other potentate was con-
sidering a scheme to buy some of the rights
for a million or so and smash the old-
fashioned steel business. Nothing came of it
all, and the co. gradually faded out. How
it was formally ended, if it ever was for-
mally ended, no one seemed able to find out.
Still it kept things going for a couple of
years or so. Then the industrial boom came
along, and Earle Hermann, Ltd., openly or
covertly promoted a number of companies,
or was interested in their flotation. They
were mostly very bad flotations. Paris
House, Ltd., and W. E. Smith, Ltd., were
amongst them, and though they set off with
just the dash that might have been expected,
the quotations for their shares soon fell
almost to freezing point. Rowron Bros.,
Ltd., was a more ambitious proposition.
After many vicissitudes this was got off at
tremendous cost, and the management was
left in Earle Hermann’s offices. Matters
soon drifted into a colossal muddle. Even
the share certificates appear to have been
involved in the tangle. Finally the affairs
of this concern were taken over by a Maori-
laud management, whose comments on the
past history of the co. were blistering.
Mount Oxide also engaged much of the
attention of Earle Hermann, Ltd., and. in
connection with this the tangle was even
worse. It wars, in fact, the operations of
the office in regard to this North Queensland
business which brought matters to a head,
mainly through a shocking agreement made
by the Australian co. with a London finan-
cial group. If half the allegations made
concerning the principal’s operations directly
and indirectly regarding Mount Oxide alone
are answered. Hermann will be kept busy
for some time to come. Incidentally the co.
was an extensive stock-jobber, and no doubt
a lot of money was lost that way. To pro-
vide funds the co. added to its share capital,
nominally £500,000, by a debenture issue, and
some moneyed people of good standing
appear as creditors on this account.
Apparently they were impressed by the large
imagination and the sumptuous furniture
before referred to. The fact that the co.
could sell them shares at say 15s. cash and
offer to buy them back at 17s. (id. delivery
in three months struck these people not as
a sign of most expensive finance, but as
proof that there was a wonderfully able
financier on the premises. Possibly when
they view the dreadful tangle they now
begin to realise that the wonderful financier
might have been all the better if there had
been a plain, dull-brown book-keeper behind
him to tell him exactly where he stood. Just
at present the debenture holders are in
charge, and other developments are highly
probable. Meantime this Young-Man-in-a-
-1 lurry is understood to be taking a rest
cure.
* * *
The half-yearly accounts of the Barrier
Truth (the Labor-owned paper at Broken
Hill, N.S.W.) have been published. The
best the auditors can say on the figures sup-
plied to them is that the paper made a loss
of £395 on the half-year. As this particular
half year was about the most prosperous that
Broken Hill has ever known, the financial
result is rather depressing. The auditors
add that their real opinion is that the paper,
at the time of their investigation, was losing
at the rate of about £lOOO a year. The
statement of accounts shows that £2927 is
owing to capital (there is capitalism even
here !) and £6603 to debenture holders. There
is also £1247 owing for interest on the
debentures, £133 of which is for the half-year
under notice. The other £lll4 evidently
belongs to previous years. As the rate of
interest on the debentures is apparently 4
per cent., and the concern began to. issue
debentures less than six years ago, it is evi-
dent that very little, if any, interest has ever
been paid on the money borrowed. In addi-
tion £425 is owing to the bank. The assets
include the plant, valued at £5192, £1421
worth of “goods” (this valuation is not cer-
tified by the auditors), and £2491 for book
debts —which is a lump of money, and the
auditors’ remark that “a number of the
accounts are doubtful, at least,” seems not
uncalled for, especially as it is also stated
that about £6OO of the amount relates to
accounts that have been either dead or asleep
for over 12 months. The auditors respect-
fully suggest that the income be increased
or the expenses reduced, or both. A recent
meeting of the local Miners’ Association (it
is reported in the paper) proposes to take
over the whole concern and run it in connec-
tion with the union. The Miners’ Associa-
tion is the principal debenture-holder, and
offers to pay off all the other liabilities over
a period of years. From the outlook at
present the union funds will have to meet
heavy deficiencies, seeing that a large defici-
ency' has been incurred during years of
phenomenal plenty.
* * * *
According to the cables South Africa has
arranged that the banks shall, on the Gov-
ernment's guarantee, advance 50 p.c. on the
values of wool, mohair, skins and hides, the
market for which nas temporarily suspended
business. Certificates will be issued against
the product and held as evidence of security.
Presently, as already suggested in these
columns, the Australian Government will re-
quire to do something of the sort in Aus-
tralia, where the authorities have the benefit
of a bank upon which to rely. Up to the
present our Government has simply had word
with the private banks, and having been
told what they (the private banks) want in
order to meet their position, have swallowed
the dose, thinking that that is all there is
to it. But the State Treasurers must surely
see that they are just about at the end
of their four-notes-for-one-sovereign scheme.
It is good enough for a family, but it will
not support a State, let alone a nation, even
if it consist of only 5,000.000 mouths.

“S.C.”: Occidental Gold Mining Co.
(Cobar, N.S.W.) held its half-yearly meeting
the other day, but it wasn’t the quiet,
brotherly affair that it usually is. The eo.
has £5341 on fixed deposit at 31 p.c. per
annum, and an overdraft of £4212, for which
it pays 5 p.c. Besides that it has borrowed
£lOOO from the wife of the chairman of
directors, paying 10 p.c. for the money. Why
the co. should have to pay 10 p.c. on a loan
when it has assets standing in the books at
£lOO,OOO is a mystery. Another item of
£243, which appeared as legal expenses,
turned out to be portion of the £3OO or so
paid to a past manager as compensation for
wrongful dismissal. The Board didn’t know
the cost of treatment per ton of ore, but
thought it could be worked out! Deprecia-
tion was another matter that cropped up.
Apparently a small amount is allowed now
ami again, when the directors feel so in-
clined. Some half-years nothing at all is
allowed; for the term just ended £76S is
put under that heading. Finally, it
transpired that the co. had been offend-
ing against the provisions of the
Companies Act bv employing a director
of the co. as a watchman. Evidently the
company has reached that stage when it
must he handled with great care.
* * * *
T.ast month Hawkins’ Hill Reward
(N.S.W.) crushed altogether 105 tons of
stone for llOoz. gold. The proceeds, £4OO
or so, will do to go on with, and the manage-
ment seems quite confident that much larger
crushings will shortly be treated from the
320 ft. (the deepest 1 and the 200 ft. levels.
It should not require much more than a short
crosscut east of the Exhibition shaft to pick
up the reef at the 320 ft. level. Driving on
this new reef has been done at the 200 ft.
level for about 100 ft. (50ft. south and 50ft.
north), and both faces show encouraging pro-
spects. As ii rule, on this field the pay ore
exists in patches, and the streak is very
narrow, so the estimate of ore reserves is,
to some extent, a guess. The most important
work in hand is the crosscut at the 320 ft.
level for the new reef, which will, if success-
ful, greatly facilitate tin* opening of a small
block of stone between levels.
(Jreat Cobar has had another visit of in-
spection from Bellew Harvey, a director,
who apparently is not quite satisfied that
the big new plant on the Chcsney is an
absolute failure. It is rumored that he
has gone to Wallaroo and Moonta to have a
look at their concentration and flotation
plant, in the hope that he may afterwards
be able to remodel the Chesney machinery,
and qualify it to handle the silicious ores
of the Great Cobar properties. Bewick,
Moreing are said to have severed their con-
nection with the co., and the only work now
being done on the properties is the job of
keeping the water down.
Lord Nelson, St. Arnaud (Vic.), after
turning out a million of gold and paying
divs. of £265.000. hangs in the balance over
reconstruction. The people who cut into
the co. over the Lord Nelson North deal had
to rely on the market for what little profit
they made. So they are to sell to another
co. which is to get easy terms. The miners
are ready to back the show to the extent of
sinking another 200 ft., but there isn’t enough
enthusiasm among the old crowd that did so
well out of the (nine to put up the few pence
necessary to carry out the job. Lord Nel-
son, therefore, is threatened with the liquid-
ator’s hammer and a final dividend. After
that St. Arnaud's only glory will be Agent-
General Peter Mcßride.
* * * *
Mount Lyell (Tasmania) in four weeks
smelted 20,627 tons of ore for 601 tons of
fine copper, 32,3980 z. silver, and 8790 z. gold,
an average of 2.25 p.c. copper. That extra
quarter unit with the red metal at £6O is
worth 35., and on the 360,000 tons that
Mount Lyell* furnaces in 12 months there is
£56.000. If Lyell can produce 8000 tons
of copper in 12 months, this means a rise of
£7 per ton in selling price of SOOO tons of
metal sent to market. Another point to note
is that the capital expenditure on the Mount
Margaret scheme is intended to pull down
costs about 3s. per ton of crude ore, which
would square things up even if the extra
quarter p.c. of copper did drop off. An acci-
dent to the electric hoist during a thunder-
storm has prevented the hauling of the usual
quantity of p.vritic ore, and as a result one
of the furnaces was put out of commission.
Still, there is every reason to be satisfied with
the consignment of some 3269 tons of blister
copper this half-year, for before the close on
September 30 the total should be nearly 4000
tons. Plans for the concentration of low-
grade ore are taking more definite shape.
