* * • * *
I levberton (N.Q.) is practical in its
methods, and in order to carry on some busi-
ness the storekeepers are advancing 60s. per
cwt. on tin concentrates. The balance will
be paid when the market re-opens. Of course,
the store requires till money, so probably the
tin sluicers sell about as much tin as will
pay for their tucker. New England (N.S.W.)
is agitating for something better than tucker,
and some of the big mining and sluicing cos.
would also like an advance from the State.
The Herberton people, however, ask only for
an advance of £4O per ton of concentrates,
and :is the figure seems moderate, the State
may be induced to risk it.
From “Oof Bird”: Edna May (W.A.)
has had enough of incompetent and trouble-
some bailing tanks, and is going in for a 7in.
or Sin. Cornish lift. This should fill the bill
and enable the bottom level crosscut to be
extended without any further hindrance.
The new air compressor, or rather the
second-hand one which the co. befriended,
after years of rust and idleness, is working
as well as can be expected. At present it
is providing power for one drill, but hopes
are entertained that it will be well enough to
keep one or two more going when the bottom
workings are unwatered and crosscutting is
resumed. From all accounts the 150 ft.
level is opening up well, arid already thou-
sands of tons of 50dwt. ore have Ir en
exposed south of the main crosscut. This
makes up for the 12dwt. stuff north of the
crosscut, and the lower-grade material in
the smaller parallel veins. At the Edna
May Central a fairly good plant lias been
installed, but neither the official estimates
of ore reserves nor values are likely to come
up to expectations. Some wily share-
dealers, however, reckon the show stands a
good chance of getting the Edna May shoot
at depth, and are giving their bosom pals
the tip to watch and, if their theory proves
correct, prey.
* * * *
Efforts are being made to make gold mining
more attractive in the West by a reduction
in the charge for crushing at the State mills.
This, it is hoped, will induce many old
battlers who knocked off work to have
{mother go. It is also expected to re.irve
any distress that may arise on the fields
on account of the war by giving men who
have nothing else' to do a chance to bog in
and earn a bit of money. The reductions
will range from 10 p.c. on 9dwt. o T, e to 40
p.c. on ore under (idwt. These cut rates
will result in considerable loss to the Mines
Department, but it reckons that money
spent in this way will do more good than if
applied to the direct sustenance of men who
can’t get employment.
* * * *
Black Bauge West (W.A.) has applied
for six months’ exemption. Its reconstruc-
tion scheme was going fairly well when the
war came along and upset its applecart.
Now, as the co. is temporarily unable to
find a sound financial footing, it has come
to the conclusion that the best thing to do
is to shut down and lie low. Meanwhile the
time for applications for shares in the re-
organised concern has been extended.
* * * *
From Meekatharra (W.A.) : Commodore’s
new development is likely to prove important.
The find is 500 ft. north of the main workings
at the 160 ft. level, and so far the ore is of
good value and very wide in places. The pri-
vately-owned adjoining lease, the Ingliston,
alias Commodore South, has a new shoot of
ore from which 127 tons have just been
treated for an average of fS 18s. per ton.
An Eastern crowd had an eye on this show
some time ago, but negotiations came to
nothing.

WOMAN’S
LETTER
Sydney, September 14, 1914.
My Dear Akini, —
The city has been extra dusty, with the
grind of marching feet of late. An inter-
minable line of khaki tramped into camp the
other afternoon, amidst the cheerful whoops
of the crowds on the kerbstone. As no one
is allowed to know, or surmise, when this
lighting stuff is going to move off the prem-
ises. I suppose it is wrapped in a pall of
mystery. This is the favorite military gar-
ment of the moment, and it is cut on the
cross.
Amongst the first list of casualties comes
the well-known N.S.W. name of Pockley.
iJr. Brian I ockley, the fine, upstanding
,n 3n who was in the black-edged list
ot killed in the Australian force now pound-
ing the enemy’s posessions in New Britain
was a son of Dr. Antill Pockley, and a rela-
tive of the Picton Antill family. Brian
J ockley was a graduate of Sydney Univer-
sity. Also he was a great favorite both in
sport and stethoscope circles. As captain
ot the Army Medical Corps, his death was
one of the unexpected happenings of Aus-
tralia s dash for the enemy’s adjacent bit of
territory.
Penny Post Henniker Heaton, who died
in Geneva a few days ago. looked good for
another 20 summers when he was touring
this way in 1913. The Heaton title, which
took the form of that doubtful blessing, or
blight, in perpetuity, a baronetcy, was, of
course, given for services rendered in the
ilouse of Commons to the democratic cause
ot cheap postage. This is worth recalling
because so many titles appear to be shaken
out of a lucky bag and given for services
not rendered. The penny-stamp enthusiast
married the well-dowered daughter of the
late Samuel Bennett, who founded the Even-
ing IVews. Concerning the Order of
baronets (an ingenious scheme for raising
the wind created by that shiftv monarch
?•)« an English K.C. once asked:
\\hat is a baronet?” Sir Wilfred Lawson
(who ought to know) replied: “He is a
man who has ceased to be a gentleman, and
has not become a nobleman.” The acid is
chiefly rubbed into baronet Lawson, a once-
noisy advocate of cold water, in this freezing
comment. 6

A rush of band instruments to the heads

ihe Governor called for cheers for Our
friends in Europe (even Servia got a mild

order.

Still another outbreak of Death or Glorv
from our sex. This time it’s a more or less
legal brand of female volunteers who are
sworn to do or die (if need be) in the cause
or home and the perambulator. Various
pi ess reports of the corps are
agreed that blue and purple are to
be mingled (it s a horrible blend of
color) in the. uniform of the A.L.V.
tiee translation, Australian Leg View.
Girls with handsome understandings will
apply for front ranks on the march. Any
girl under 33 will be considered eligible.
Ao girl must use her rifle on a neighbor’s
goats or cats unless they are formally de-
clared contraband of war. Any neighbor
who objects to a Lauy Volunteer practising
with firearms in her own backyard, is liable
to be called “a brute!” Drill is to be a
serious business. Any girl who is caught
looting her comrades’ hairpins is liable to
have her ears, boxed. The female fighling
squad is still in embryo, but it hopes to be
in the soup later on.

should go with a garden roller. In the

A fierce controversy as to whether plain
citizens can go on spending exactly as much
as they did m the piping times of peace, on
diess and minor luxuries, is now rajring in
fhe press. From the assured standpoint of
a Cabinet Minister’s wife, the thing is quite
easy. Go on in the same way, and still
give out of your handsome income—to the
crop of patriotic and Red Cross charities
that hammer at your door. Unfortunately
all plain citizens cannot aspire to the salary
ot a successful politician. Even a small
shrinkage in a small income, when the neces-
sary calls on the total are more than they
were.a month or two ago, calls for a careful
revision of personal expenses. The plain
citizen, who may be a cabinet-maker or a
city clerk, has nothing to hoard just now.
Why implore him not to fill his coffers with
the spare gold he hasn’t got, while he denies
himself a new hat.
The Fat. Man can liug the good advice
g t l J en T7 J \ Mmister for Justice Hall and Mrs
Ada Holman. Let him spend freely and
avoid a chill in bank vaults. Timid folks
who rushed the Savings Banks to hide their
cash under a flower-pot can also pluck up
courage and go on spending in the same
thrifty fashion as of yore. But the average
breadwinner can’t make three-quarters of a
yard, of cash cover a yard of ordinary ex-
penditure plus War Chest, Red Socks—l
mean. Led Cross—bales, food funds, and
clothing for the destitute in brave little Bel-
gium. Even the State finds it difficult to
go on borrowing in the same open-hearted
fashion as it did yester-year. So I guess
the small fry will just have to go slow
lhe Austral String Quartet winds up its
present season on September 19. Pianist
Pd ward Goll, recently from Melbourne, has
been secured for the occasion, (1011, formerly
of the once-pleasant land of Bohemia, is a
compatriot of Kubelik. Bohemia grows musi-
cians by the cwt. It. is a place with a past.
Once it was something approaching an em-
pire and possessed a seacoast alluded to by
Shakspeare. Then it became part of Aus-
tria. It got angry with Austria and started
an insurrection. The Bohemian way of
throwing off allegiance was to cast the repre-
sentatives of the hated ruler out of a top
window, and this soil of thing created so
much annoyance that Bohemia hasn’t
amounted to very much since.
1 lie Girls’ Wild Blower Show committee is
counting up a small but blue deficit. This dire
result of six months’ strenuous preparation
to gather exhibits from every State in Aus-
tralia, may be confidently labelled “Made in
Germany.” A war is too big a side-show
for mere vegetation to bump against, any-
how. Even the waratahs sulked in their
little green cells and kept in hiding later
than usual this year, so the next best splash
of color the Exhibition could scrape together
was supplied by the giant native lilies from
I Stephens. Mrs. White —the Cullen
lady s mother gave most of these gorgeous
blooms. Some of the most remarkable sur-
vivals of the lately-deceased show came from
Westralia. These were the wild flowers
which had travelled with their stalks stuck
in tame, uncooked potatoes. From these
the greedy blooms sucked long drinks that
landed them fit and fresh in Sydney Town.

Easts drawing recently took first place.
11ns youthful pupil of the Tech. College
was a prize-winner at the Girls’ Flower
Show, where the competition was keen.
Several other demoiselles from the same
dusty address also put up a good record at
the defunct exhibition.

The Sydney Symphony orchestra gave its
last concert for this year in the Town Hall
the other afternoon. Conductor BradLv
took his usual amount of phvsical culture
for about two hours, and flung his hands
f “e four quarters of Heaven in steering
his fiddlers through the tricky Tscliaikowsky
’• ymphony. This massive tome of harmony
m E Minor resounds with the barbaric up-
roar of drum and cymbal so prevahnt in the
Kussian composer’s musii. There are dulcet
moments of graceful melody, when the
crash of instruments does not shake the
rafters. On the whole, it is like battle prac-
fice . and is too noisy to please the m-e
public Liszt’s Concerto for piano and
oichesti a wa~ the most popular event of
the concert. Edward Goll. at the keyboard
pranced delightfully up and down the notes
and looked as if he would go off into a faint
feu sheer joy in the honey-sweet interludes.
Golls hands dash wildly into the air, and
his hyacinthme locks have a look of the
pudding-basin domestic clip, but he is a
pleasing performer in spite of his antics.

Word drops in that one John Braga, who
has been piling up Australian experience at
Clear Hills, has packed his swag to join the
3rd infantry battalion for the front. He
lias seen active service before to-day, and
can chatter with the enemy in any language
it prefers out of a choice of German,
C hinese and Hindostani. Soldier Braga
will keep his best French for the Allied ear.
• I s , a relative of 0. Seddon, the present
British Administrator of Baroda, an Indian
State which has offered its entire fighting
force to Britain. Also, he is a man of
nniny inches, and plays a most respectable
bridge game.
An International Rubber Congress 13
going to tell all it knows about that useful
vegetation during October. The delegates
meet at Batavia on the 19th of next month,
and mark the event with an exhibition,
from India, Burma, the Dutch East Indies
and 1 apua (war permitting) exhibits will
pour into the show. The spectator can put
two and two together and find out whether
lubber tyres are likely to be up or down,
or whether they will simply go bang in the
same old way. A Melbourne man named
Bloomfield is likely to represent the Papuan
planters.

The play, “My Soldier Boy,” is a
lively farce, written by Alfred Maltby and
frank bindo, r a sure knowledge of
stage-craft. This amateur company was
much above the average at Saturday night’s
performance. The handsome girl, Ida Slate,
who took the part of the leading lady, Lydia
Mendle. is entitled to quite three cheers.
Ihe same goods may be passed on to Ernest
Goodwin, for his heroic effort to sabre the
atmosphere as Monty Mendle, a prevaricat-
ing young lawyer in a borrowed Hussar uni-
form. Daisy Shoesmith beefed out the broad
tarce of Martha’s slavey-of-all-work lines to
loud applause. James Teddiman wrestled vali-
antly with the brogue of Michael O’Doherty
and kissed Martha as often as his other duties
permitted. The rest of the company did no
damage to the plot. The Sydney Amateur
Orchestra (mostly Girl), in charge of
f rancis . Knowles, whacked out popular
tunes with enormous enthusiasm. With a
little more practice it ought to smooth a
tew lumps out of an otherwise fairly even
performance.
That persistent stimulus to extravagance,
the auctioned chocolate box which sells and*
re-sells at its weight in silver, is a serious
to the Union Jack at local shows for
the Cause. Of course, the flag vendor has
an unchallenged right to prevaricate, which
is denied to the man who wants £3 for a box
of sweets. Even a patriotic Ananias hasn’t
the face to say that a very new box of choco-
lates took a prominent part in the relief of
ivunberiey or waved over the gallant plume
ot General John French near the River
Marne. The man who sold a bit of
bunting for £2 2s. at amateur theatricals
the other night told quite an attractive fairy
yarn to a cheering audience.

VANDORIAN.
In less than four weeks of fighting the
Austrians have lost over 130.000 men cap-
tured, in addition to thousands and thou-
sands killed. That the Russian is a better
fighter than the Austrian Teuton or the
Hungarian is generally understood, but this
vast number of prisoners begins to look fishy.
In fact, it looks as though the Slav regi-
ments in the Austrian army are surrendering
wholesale to their Russian kinsfolk, and are
probably being sent north and west to belt
beer and sauerkraut out of the Germans who
are hurrying to the rescue of the tottering
Hapsburg Empire. If that is not the ex-
planation, then the Austrian generalship
and soldiery must be the dead finish and
limit m the way of incapacity.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, September 14, 1914.
My Dear Yarrie, —
When I begin to write about Melba’s
Patriotic Concert at the Auditorium I am
conscious of the inadequacy of the English
language. Words fail me. Diction falls
to the earth. Eloquence is carried out
dead. Remembering these things you will
understand, if my normally ready pen grows
a bit incoherent.
The advance guard of the audience got
mixed up in a melee with boy scouts and
14 battalions of professional Sassiety Pro-
gramme Sellers. The latter were utterly
routed. In fact it was a debacle. How-
ever, the S.P.S. re-formed ultimately and
charged the enemy, and sold heavily to them
once they had been safely manoeuvred into
their seats. Then came an army corps of
guinea ticket-holders. Some of its members
had paid £2O for one ticket. They lacked
the clan of the hoi jiolloi, and were easily
reduced. Boy scouts held all the passes—
I mean corridors —and did sentry work
outside. Punctually to time arrived the
Governor-General, with his party, and Gov-
ernor Stanley with his party. They were
met with an astounding blare of “God Save”
and “Rule Britannia.” made up of orchestra
and chorus, with Melba, resplendent in
dame color, taking the lead with all the
strength of her wonderful voice. Maggie
Stirling loomed up behind Melba in lead-
color, like a great warship, and thundered a
contralto backing. Overcome with emotion
I burst into tears, and sang the first eleven
bars of the Soldiers’ Chorus from “Faust.”
However, no one noticed in the prevailing
enthusiasm. Everyone waved flags, so that
the hall looked like a vast, windy yard on a
washing-day, when none but colored garments
had been dealt with. I have never seen this
sort of washing-day, personally, but my vivid
imagination supplies the parallel. The
chorus at the back of the orchestra and
organ was so huge that the topmost musical
heads made black dots right up against the
white ceiling. The third tier of the crowded
gallery presented the same decorative effect.
I was shocked to see one of Sassiety’s
most brilliant young men in a five-bob seat —
his monocle making a highlight that pre-
vented anyone from missing the stunning
fact that he was not among the guinea-pigs.
The Government House party carried flags
which had been presented to them by Melba.
Whenever she fixed her eye on them they
waved like fury. I grieve to have to men-
tion that the G.-G. was inclined to wave out
of his turn. Lady Helen, however, waved
with admirable precision. She wore a regal
cloak of red and gold brocade, with a large
red velvet collar-cape. Another accu-
rate waver was Lady Stanley, whose
cloak was a wonderful blue and gold
arrangement, with a huge Collar of
grey fox fur. Little Lady Doris Blackwood,
who always dimples along in a sparkling
way, was clothed in a pink silk, frilly cloak.
It was a great evening for cloaks. Mrs.
Leinpriere was amongst the vice-regalities,
and anchored or moored to her was a small,
pale-haired child, about whom I know
nothing whatever, but who is thrown in on
general principles, and as an evidence of
good faith, as it were.
Edward Solomon (he who won fame, re-
gardless of cost, at the patriotic matinee, bv
his gallant financial stand against the
Ferrrrguson bawbees) was in a seat behind
his old foe. Heaven alone knows what the
dauntless Irish patriot had paid for it. The
Bowes Kellys were there with their pretty
daughters, so were Mrs. Miller and her niece,
Miss White and grandmamma White, and the
I’ursey Chirnsides and a Fenner sister, and
1 don’t know how many others. I was in
tie very act of writing a few thousand
names down in my tablets for your benefit
when I swooned again, with sheer exeite-
•ment, and temporarily lost the hang of
things. Resuscitated by a posse of Boy
Scouts, I observed the Gaud Mayor and his
wife in the dress-circle. It was a gracious
spectacle and did me a lot of good. Then
came the taking of a flashlight photograph of
the brilliant gathering.
What a night! Melba sang repeatedly
and the house went quite mad after her
rendering of Depuis le Jour, from “Louise.”
“Louise” is an opera which is woven all
through with a music manifestation of the
fascination of Paris. Melba seemed to be
inspired with the memory of the city that
she knows so well.
Ellen Terry, in lavender and lovely old
lace, was in splendid voice. She did not
trust to her memory, but read Kipling’s “Big
Steamers.” and reduced every sentimentalist
present to tears. She wound up by reducing
herself to tears.
What with cheers, tears, patriotic music
supplied by Zelman and his orchestra, Melba,
Maggie Stirling, Ellen Terry, and the expen-
sive clothes that’were on exhibit, the crowd
got more than its money’s worth. And to
understand what that means you have only
to look at the sum that was raked in.
Airs. Burnham, who, as I have told you
r>u or more times before, was originally
Maggie Niall, is living at Aldershot with
Mrs. Hall, who, as I have mentioned
on at least 78 previous occasions, was
Holly Clegg. Their husbands are both
in the Queen's Bays (no, not Boys),
or if it isn't that it is the Fusiliers
or the Marines or the 17tli Fast
Middlesex Light Infantry or something else
equally exclusive. I now propose to give
these two young women a rest. They have
absolutely been bathing in the limelight of
late.
Little is heard but the dull crash of shat-
tered social functions. The Australian Club
Ball is the latest to fall in ruins. What
Cup week will be like without it I shudder
to think—more after the style of Hamlet
without the corpses than an ordinary race-
meeting at headquarters.
The Very Best have decided against house-
boats ( i.c ., coal barges decorated with flags
and flowers) this Henley. The alleged
reason is money-tightness, due to the war
and the exigencies of the subscription list
habit. Perhaps, again, they may be peer-
ing into the future and seeing visions of
themselves in mourning for slain relatives
when Henley day comes round.
Some of our departing braves have actu-
ally taken to advertising their pet wishes.
One wants a ’possum; another a bulldog.
I am not up in the ways of ’possums at the
Fx*ont, but on the question of bulldogs I am
something of an expert. You can always
tell a bulldog by the fact that it hides
under the house if it hears a shot. (Deaf
bulldogs are not considered, owing to their
rarity.) It is this miserable characteristic
that forced me to dispense with my bull-
dogs. They interfered with my usual after-
noon’s revolver practice. Bloodhounds are
more satisfactory pets for martial purposes.
At the first hint of warfare of any sort they
issue forth and pin someone by the throat.
The only disadvantage is their lack of dis-
crimination. They pin Britons and Germans
with equal zest. Still, all told, they are
superior to bulldogs. How about a sub-
scription list to buy the forces a few pet
bloodhounds?
Mrs. Blomfield, an Anglo-Indian who is
married to the Flag-Lieutenant of the Mel-
bourne, is inhabiting a Melbourne mansion
nat. She is the image of Gertrude Glyn,
who plays Lady Bobby in “Gipsy Love.”
The result is that every time the care-free
Lady Bobby sups gaily, or otherwise, at a
restaurant, the social wowser comments on
the cynical heartlessness of Mrs. Blomfield.
She gets the blame for fun she never had.
which is a most infuriating experience, I
always think. When I said the sailor’s wife
was the image of Miss Glyn, I spoke in the
hyperbolical language of the gossip-monger.
As an example of the lengths to which these
wretches will go, 1 myself have constantly
been mistaken for Bunty.
The Glaciariuni gave a Pageant for the
Cause last week. Sassiet.v has had so much
fancy dress of late that bizarre clothes re-
present everyday dress to most of the girls,
f This might account for the weird rigs they
often wear abroad —but that is another
story. Anyhow they take to a pageant
like a duck to water or a chorus girl to fizz.)
The Maddens, who have always been the"
main social stay of the ice house, are reduced
to one family-full of girls. That is to say
the Frank Madden’s daughters. How-
ever _ the chilly resort still" battles along.
Xassiety haughtily chose its own place in the
pageant. At least it tried to. What was its
exasperation and horror to find that a cheeky
little Belgian girl with pigtails and a turned-
up nose had butted into the most sacred pre-
cincts, dragging with her a Dutch boy-
partner. Despite glares, sniffs and even
muttered curses the two stayed on with the
irritating persistency of a'fly on a bull-
dog’s nose, and the event nearly caused civil
war. As to their being social nonentities, I
am only going, please understand, on hear-
say. I am fully prepared to learn that thev
are. in reality, members of our oldest
noblesse. I have been deceived so cruelly
in these matters in the past that my faith
is utterly shaken. The McGillicuddy pair
(or at lehst it looked like them) were well
to the front, and Mrs. Billy Simon Frazer
showed off her marvellous skating again. The
patriotic feeling was in blazing evidence.
Britannia and all her white handmaidens
preceded queens of all the Allies. Horribly
realistic ambulance workers made a “set” with
a wounded soldier or two hauled along in the
rear. Some parts of the parade took on the
look of the Waxworks Show just after some
sensational murder case. Bunting, which is
being hired out at heavy rates by the even-
ing all round this city, decorated the walls
and dripped from the roof.
There was a drenched meeting at Moonee
Valley for the Hunt Club races, but we
sporting characters recked little of the wet.
We just dived into our goloshes and waded
right along. Melba’s macintosh was there
at an early hour. It was—well, it was a
macintosh. It is impossible for even the
most frantic impressionist lo write more.
I counted dozens of Mitchell and Lempriere
gamps near the stand, and likewise several
brewery McCracken aqua-scutums. Melba’s
sun, George Armstrong, rode the favorite (to
wit Grandpapa Mitchell’s Ivia Ora) in the
big steeplechase, but the tiresome beast
fell. This horseman rides with a long stir-
rup, and the gallery yelled “Legs" with
great enthusiasm as he walked back in a
muddied condition. llow the galleryites do
love to see people flung off things! Francis
Syme’s young son won on My Angle—a 15
to one chance. This horse was also from the
Mitchell stable.
From my Adelaide correspondent :■ —-
the Show, Mawson and the steady tramp ot‘ men
leaving for the wars have been the features of the
week. The Show was a success, all things considered.
The stock was a shade less numerous, anil the trade
in motors slightly less brisk; also, the shops had
fewer opportunities of disposing of last season’s
relics and this season’s horrors, but otherwise things
were much the same as usual. The S.P.C.A. had a
pocket-edition pony on rhe grounds collecting funds
for the Society, and reaped a small harvest in pence
on the morning which was specially set aside for
attendance from the schools. Most of the squatto-
craey came down for the occasion, and the hotels and
hoarding-houses, not to mention the bars, gave three
hearty cheers, if not more, in honor of the event.
The Poole-Longbottom wedding is to be at the
end of this month, at St. Matthew’s Church, Kensing-
ton. He is a youthful medico, the son of Canon
Poole of this village, and she is the daughter of
IV. E. Longbottom, of North Adelaide. The ’groom
has just bought Dr. Curtis’s Penola practice.
Lady Galway left for Melbourne on Tuesday on Red
Cross business. Government House is rapidly taking
on the semblance of an old-clo’ shop, and she has
to see what she can do about it.
Dr. Harry Nott is engaged to Miss Marjorie Lowe,
once of Westralia, and now of this city.
The C. H. Angases, who have been in town for the
winter, arc returning to Lindsay Park this week.
Glenelg had its Patriotic Concert on Monday.
Patriotic concerts are like Death —they come to each
town soon or late these times. Miss Doris Coles
organised the show.
Mrs. William Milne died at North Adelaide on
Tuesday. She possessed a number of line paintings,
and a few months ago she gave some and loaned
others to the Art Gallery.
St. Peter’s and Prince Alfred’s Colleges combined
to say good-bye, in the Town Hall on Thursday night,
to their old scholars who are going to the war.
The Governor and representative men from both
colleges were guests, as well as the soldiers, and
lookers-on in the shape of present schoolboys in
the organ galleries and ladies in the dress-circle.
There were speeches and—but you know the rest.
George Castles, brother of Amy aud
Dolly, has been having much success as a
singer hereabouts and in adjacent States.
He is the owner of a lyric tenor of great
sweetness and volume, and has likewise a
highly alluring platform appearance. His
case goes to show that voices run in fami-
lies, like flat feet and a passion for alcohol,'
as Sir Oliver Lodge, or one of the other
scientists who have just left us, remarked
in the course of a thoughtful disquisition on
heredity.
Mayoress Hennessy has taken steps to en-
sure a proper welcome for Dolores, who
arrives here on the 17th. On that day Mrs.
Doctor Barrett will give a reception for the
singer at her home, Heimath, in Toorak—
the place where there is an orderly Italian
garden in one quarter and an Australian
bush wilderness full of kangaroos, ’possums
and emus in another. Dolores is giving all
tile proceeds of her first concert on Satur-
day. 19th, lo (lie Belgian Belief Fund. She
is French (her mother’s name was Gilbert),
and has a country home in Touraine in the
middle of France, where she goes to rest at
intervals.
_ Two things are worrying the nicest of our
girls who mote out to Broadmeadows to see
their young otticali pals. One is the fact
that these gallant lads are furiously culti-
vating lip-herbage. The effect of their
initial struggles with more-or-less drought-
stricken crops of bristles is horrible. Also,
the dear boys’ hands are roughened with
hard work. . “They don’t seem like the same
hands,” wailed one manicured doll. It is
this sort of thing that brings home the full
horror of war to many of our young women.
The Repertory Theatre is offering 25 per
cent, of the net profits of its next season
to the Red Cross Fund. Every vice-regality
in the city is pledged to be present, to make
no mention of our head Mayor and his lady.
Arthur Adams is coming over from Sydney
to produce his play. “Mrs. Prettv and the
Premier.” It occurs on Saturdav'and Mon-
day. September 26 and 28. Bren E. Doyle
will be the Premier and Miss Allie Robson
Mrs. Pretty.
On Thursday, Friday and Saturday, Octo-
ber 1, 2 and the Repertories will put on
Galsworthy’s “.Toy,” which is a dissertation
on the love of a woman for a man, versus
the love of a mother for her child. Norman
Carter. Mrs. Maurice. Dudley, Miss Ivy
Wengel and Gregan McMahon are on the
list for this play.
The first Australian soldier to die in
Europe was squatter Richmond’s second son
Leslie. He was in the Gordon Highlanders,
and was a good-looking and charming boy.
The eldest Richmond, a stepbrother, married
a Staughton girl. This George Richmond
arrived in London to rejoin his regiment
only a few days after his brother’s death.
The Alliance Francaise and the French
Club of the Melb. University are organising
a concert in aid of the French and Belgian
Patriotic Funds. It will liapnen at the
Auditorium on the 30th. The first half of
the programme will be musical, and the
second part will be a series of Joan of Arc
scenes and tableaux, written and staged by
Rose Seaton. Beatrice Day’s name appears
among those of the artists who are throwing
in their services.
The sudden death of popular Mrs. McKay,
wife of the Toorak medico, came as a shock
to the social world. A few days before her
death the lady returned from a visit to
Sydney apparently in the pink of health.
Then came appendicitis, an operation, and
the end.
Sam McCulloch and his handsome spouse,
who was a U.S. widow, have forgotten the
Drought, the World, and even the Labor
victory and the Devil in rapturous contem-
plation of a son and heir. By his first
marriage Sam had two daughters, one of
whom is married to a Baron Sadoine.
A lot of younger. kiddies who have heard
about clowns and pantomimes, but have
never seen them on the stage, will get their
dream realised next Christmas. The “Cin-
dt-rella panto is to have a harlequinade
tacked on at the matinees. Lupino is to
clown, and if the harlequinade sets the nip-
pers and flappers in a roar and has remini-
scent attractions for the grown-ups it will
surely be turned on o' nights.
Melba’s great concert was nearly blown
out at the eleventh hour. Whilst her motor
was rushing the Sovereign of Song to the
patriotic shivoo the brake-band fired, and
the. flames played around the petrol tank,
which is situated just under the seat of the
car. The discovery of the conflagration came
just in time. Melba’s first thought when
the ear stopped was to get her little niece
out of the explosion, for her sister (Mrs.
Lempriere) and the kiddie were with her.
Herself escaped in a very highly-strung
state. Even when she arrived at the Audi-
torium she was too upset to give Dr. Barrett
a coherent account of the averted tragedy.
All things considered. Melba’s subsequent
singing of the "Lucia” number, to Lem-
mone s fluting, was a triumph of art over
nerves.
Yours sincerely.
JOHANNA.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Coolibah” : “Marloo” in a recent Bulle-
tin discourses through the uppermost section of his
cabbage-tree about an imported pest which he styles
the “Queensland tick,” alleging that, though stock
in the Richmond River (N.S.W.) district are heavily
infested, no harm has resulted. Writer has a long
and highly unprofitable acquaintance with Ixodes
Boris, and has noted that this creeping plague of the
Straits Settlements and redwater always transpire
simultaneously. The microscopic organism or proto--
plasm responsible for the disease is carried within
the tick, and transmitted to the blood of the victim
on which 1.8. aforesaid happens to fasten, in the
same manner that the malarial mosquito does its
work. Introduced into the blood of other animals
such as the horse, marsupials, etc., the protoplasm
is a mere negative quantity, productive of no results
for good or evil; but in the blood of the bull and
his family, unless these have previously been ren-
dered immune by inoculation, the parasitic organism
breeds rapidly and immediately sets up a rapid
destruction of the red corpuscles of the blood. A
high state of fever results (tick fever), and in
advanced stages the waste matter from the destruc-
tion of the corpuscles causes congestion of the urinary
organs, which in about 80 p.c. or 90 p.e. of cases
ends in the death of the victim. The onward march
of the tick continues through coastal Australia, and
if he has not yet appeared on the Richmond he cer-
tainly will ere long. All stock owners are hereby
earnestly advised to inoculate everything of the
bovine species as soon as the pest approaches and
before fever breaks out. Don't depend on local
‘bleeders,” as the blood of these may not be effec-
tive, but secure “blood” from the Queensland Stock
Department. Inoculation of a herd after the fever
has appeared in that particular mob would only aug-
ment the virulence of the outbreak. When the pest
and its attendant fever first began to run riot in the
herds of Central and South Queensland the usual
quack came forward with his usual infallible remedy,
and stockowners were fleeced in the purchase of tons
of rubbish. There is no cure for redwater. Inocula-
tion is the preventive. Dosing a cow merely excites
her and sends the temperature up the extra degree
or two that kills. Writer found that those placed
in a cool, shady place, and merely let alone, recov-
ered much more frequently than those treated with
alleged remedies. “Marloo’s” ticks, so plentiful in
the vicinity of Lismore, are probably scrub ticks.
The only possible explanation otherwise would be that
they, or their immediate ancestors, had been bred on
horses or marsupials, and had thus not developed the
protoplasm productive of red water. Writer, though
a breeder of cattle in a heavily-infested area, has not
had a fatal or obviously bad case in his herd for
many years. Cattle in an infested area probably all
have the disease in a mild form when mere calves,
and become immune before reaching maturity. Fatal
cases among calves are very rare, as the vigor of
growth and the rapidity of production of the red
corpuscles in the blood of a young calf neutralise the
action of the protoplasms to a large extent. Humidity
and warmth are essential to the welfare of the
cuss, which accounts for the fact that, although he
has worked in west from the coast to the foot of the
ranges in most places, the tick is almost unknown in
the somewhat arid and frosty tableland or downs
country. Climatic conditions and not tick regula-
tions or inspectors are responsible for this, for., as
hereinbefore stated, marsupials carry the pest, and
the wallaby is a noted scoffer at authority and no
respecter of quarantine lines, Acts of Parliament or
other paraphernalia of the Law.
Lagani-Namo”: Some natives in the
Solomons having dropped in one day to have a yarn
with a Samoan mission teacher, found him, to their
surprise, apparently roasting a woman—on a big open
hearth. They hurriedly congratulated him on his
conversion to cannibalism, and signified their in-
tention of remaining for the banquet. Then he told
them that they were wrong in thinking that he’d
become a cannibal. The body had, he explained, been
rescued from the surf, and his object in putting it
on the fireplace was to try and reanimate it with
heat. At this they simply collapsed with mirth, and
remarked that he was a subtle dog; but that he
need not be afraid. “Mum” was the word with them.
M hen he angrily ordered them away, they took no
notice, simply recognising it as an attempt to bluff
them. And until the body on the hob suddenly
began screaming “Fife!” and leapt, cursing, into
the atmosphere, those hilarious youths fully believed
that they would be entertained at an excellent dinner
of hot, succulent girl.
* * * *
Ivuscobin”: There is one industry in
Australia that’s going to benefit by the war—and
that’s the “jigging” of .vacka gum in Kangaroo
Island (S.A.). 1 did a bit of this at the time of
the Boer war, and made good money at it. Yacka
gum is the gum around the stem of grass trees and
blaekboys (Xanthorrhia). The Kangaroo Island
variety is particularly strong in picric acid. Lyd-
dite and melinite are chemical picrates of potassium
and ammonium, so Kangaroo Islanders should reap
a harvest while the war lasts. The price went up
to £7 a ton at the height of the South African war,
and, with the real patriotism of commerce, was
mostly exported to Germany to be manufactured
thei e. The process of collecting is simple. Ordi-
nary chaff sacking is tied in the shape of a tree-
guard around the base of the yacka. With a tommy-
hawk, or, rather, a half-handled “Douglas,” the
gum is chipped off and the sacking catches it as it
flies. It is then put into a winnowing machine,
the ordinary device of the cocky, and cleansed of
the husks and powder. I’ve never heard of its
being locally used in manufacture, but I don’t think
there was an ammunition factory in Australia in my
time. Whether grass tree gum from the mainland is
us-d for the purpose I don’t know—if it were, we
should soon have another great national industry to
try and tempt Cohen with.

“Lngani-Namo”: Jonah isn’t the only
bloke who has travelled through the deep in the
inteiior of a whale. Away in the Long Ago, a boy
of Kadu (Torres Strait) was gulped down holus-
bolus into a fish, whilst he was. diving to clear his
net. He didn’t keep a log-book, but he knew by
the changes in temperature of his cabin that the
leviathan took him somewhere into Polar Seas. After
he had been many moons in his fish-ship, and had
begun to weary of it, he noticed one day that his
apartment was uncomfortably hot. It became hotter
and more hot, so that he was presently gasping for
air. He tried to force his way out
through the eyes, gills and mouth, but
his efforts were unsatisfactory. In
desperation he seized the pearlsliell
maili (crescent-shaped ornament)
which was hanging from his neck, and
with it cut through the fish, and
found that it had stranded on an atoll
that seemed familiar, to him. He
walked to the far side of the island,
and found a sister of his who had
married during his absence and shifted
to the new address. A few days later
he, with his brother-by-marriage and
five other boys, set out in a canoe for
his own village. Upon arrival no one
but his former wife recognised him,
and she would not acknowledge him
because she had a new man who
pleased her better. The upshot was a
fight between Jonah’s party and the
co-respondent’s mob of relatives, in
which Jonah had his usual rotten luck.
He and his five companions escaped
only by becoming flying foxes. Owing
to the strength of the wind the3 7 were
unable to make the atoll they had set
out from, but had to camp at another.
One day an old man, hearing them
chattering where they hung head
downward in the trees, climbed up
and killed them and stuck them
his belt. Guess his amazement when,
as his feet touched the ground, each
of the dead foxes became a live man.
Ilis eyeballs turned completely round
three times and his hair leapt out by
the loots and stuck into the faces of
the boys. When he had gathered him-
self and his detached whiskers together he con-
ducted them to his village, where they obtained
wives and settled down. One of them said he was the
liar who told me this legend.
“McNorth”: The remarks of “Te Pana”
(B. 2/7/ 14) arent the freeing of an anchor by means
of dynamite remind me of a disturbance of which
I was a witness some years ago in Papua. Nuba, a
half-caste in charge of a cutter, had got his anchor
foul of a large piece of “cauliflower” coral, and all
his efforts to free it had been unsuccessful. He
appealed to me. I advised him to take a cane hoop
from the mast, affix a few plugs of dynamite to it,
place it around the chain, which was straight '"up
and down, light the fuse and let the affair go. The
idea was that the hoop carrying the fracture would
s ide down the chain to the anchor, and the charge
explodmg there would remove the obstruction. Nuba did
all this, but when the infernal machine floated lazily

THE GASOMETER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Every time a German shell missed the gasometer
the Tommies cheered.
—Account of the, fighting at Mons.
“I was in the war,” said the soldier-man, as he
talked to a gaping friend,
“From the week that the thing began right
up to the end.
I was near blown up by a ’undred shells from a
’undred guns,
And I ’eld the gasometer the day we fought at
Mu ns.
“They ’umped us forward to do or die when the
dawn began to shine,
And a gasometer two mile ’igli was left of
our line.
And we was ’ungry, and cold, and wishin’ to
warm the chaps,
Some corp'ral brings a quart of that
Belgian schnapps.
“On an empty stummick it ain’t a joke; it ’its the
bull’s-eye fair ;
And the shells was kickin' up dust and smoke, but,
’strewth, we didn’t care.
Big Bunky Steevens was on my right, as drunk as
a ass ;
Says ’e: ‘We’ll give ’em a cheer if they ’it the
gas.’
“The gas was a target a furlong wide, or, any’ow,
near,
And every shell that was close beside, we give it a
cheer.
There was millions of tons of gas locked up in
the tank,
But we didn’t give it a thought with the ——
schnapps we’d drank.
“For more than ’arf of a hour we watched for
that gas to go,
And the shrapnel fell in a shower from the
guns of the foe;
Steevens was ’it in the neck, and took to the
rear,
But the gasometer ’eld its ground, so we give
it a British cheer.
“There’s nothink I know in a fight that gives
you the ——• spike
Like findin’ cover and sittin’ tight and ’earin’ the
bullets strike ;
But the ’appiest hour of all the war was the
mornin’ we bore the brunt
With the gasometer on our flank and the
guns in front.
“I ’ad days in Brussels and days in Rome, and days
when we reached Berlin,
And days in Paris as we. come ome, and them was
days of sin :
But the funniest day of all was the day
at Muns,
VFhen they missed the gasometer with all their
guns.”
N.S.W.
ADAM McCAY.

“Billy Blue”: The Sydney dailies have
been descr.bi.ig as the first volunteers in Australia
fiL orCe f T*n hy thG 1854 Act ’ b "»'»ht about by
of the Crimean War, and, as usual, the dailies
are historically inaccurate. The first volunteers in
Australia were enrolled, by Governor John Hunter, by
a proclamation dated September 7, ISOO Hunter
created two companies, one at Sydney (50 strong)
under Captain William Balmain (who was the prin-
cipal surgeon, and offer whom the waterside suburb

Ginger”; Only knew of one woman cook
to Shearerss. That was in a small shed outside Lock-
hait (N.S.M.), and Kate dished the mutton and spuds
up in fine style. She had her union ticket like the
rest of “the boys.” Likewise did her cheque in
in the way once beloved by shearers. The last time
she was on the howl she sidestepped into the
Murrumbidgee at Wagga and passed out. Peace to
her ashes, for she was a girl of the old brigade.

• Adelaide lias liad the wannest
August on record by six degrees, and the driest;
in fact, the one 35-point shower' was less than half
the previous record. This year, the city has had
just Sin., as against the average 15 for Jan. to
August. A bit further north there ’has been onlv
half even of what the city has got, and the wheat
harvest of much of the State is beyond saving.
" hat clouds there are seem, as usual, to avoid tiie
valley of the Murray. Morgan has had under 2Jin.,
and Blanchetown (where the first-and-lowest Murray
lock and weir will be) under 2in. since Christmas.
Record prices for cattle and sheep were
obtained at Addington (M.L.) early in August a
Hereford cross bullock realising* £52 and three
Romney-Shropshire cross wethers being knocked
: down at £l5 each. The latter is an Australasian
record for fat sheep as well as a Maoriland one.
Ihe bullock and the sheep were bought by prominent
t hiistchurch (M.L.) butchers to be slaughtered, and
the high prices were not due to the Yankee octopus,
nut to the competition amongst the local meat
retailers to secure a good advertisement and to get
extra prime animals for the annual display of meat
that is always made in the Pancake City during
National Week.”

\ Str “ ck , a burning-off camp near Coonamble
(N.S.W.), which is bossed by a Chow. Counted in
his gang three white men, two half-castes, two

per week and found. Also, lie believes in eight hours

A FEW NOtyELY
. . HINTS ON . .
BEAUTY.
By RITA MOYA.
Every woman slioulil lake pride in her personal appearance. If it is not possible to
be beautiful in the fullest sense of the word, at least you can have the attractiveness of a
pleasant expression, glossy, well-kept hair, eyes shaded by long lashes, well marked eye-
brows and a clear, natural complexion. My first advice is to avoid most manufactured
“beauty preparations.” Use simple, pure, natural ingredients. Use these regularly, and do
not make, constant changes and experiments. The various things I use and advise can be
bought in original packages from any reliable chemist. If they are not in stock he can at
once procure them from his wholesaler if you insist.
How I Discarded an Unsightly Complexion.
—How many women exclaim as they behold
their ugly complexions in the mirror: “If I
could only tear off this old skin!” and, do you
know, it is now possible to do that very thing?
Not to actually remove the entire skin all of a
sudden; that would be too heroic a method
and painful, too, I imagine. The worn-out
cuticle comes off in such tiny particles, and
so gradually—requiring about 10 days to com-
plete the transformation—it doesn’t hurt a bit,
Day by day the beautiful complexion under-
neath comes forth. Marvellous! No matter
how muddy, rough, blotchy or aged your com-
plexion, you can surely discard it by this
simple process. Just get some ordinary mer-
colised wax at your chemist’s, apply nightly
like cold cream, washing it off in the morn-
ings.
New Painless Way to Remove Hairy
Growths.—lt now transpires that the mys-
terious white paste used so successfully bv
many beauty specialists for ridding the’skin
of objectionable hairy growths is ifbtliing
more than powdered pheminol, which can be
found in any chemist’s shop. With pheminol
and water make enough paste to cover hairy
surface; apply, and in two or three minutes
rub off. wash the skin, and it will be free
from hair or blemish.
Shampoo and Hair Beautifler.— You comulain
of brittle, faded hair. You will And that keep-
ing the scalp clean and healthy is the most
effective way to restore the natural beauty
and softness, but in selecting your shampoo
avoid soaps or mixtures containing “free”
alkali. By dissolving a teaspoonful of stallax
in a cup of liot water you will have a mild,
soothing, cleansing mixture that makes the
head feel fine and removes all dust, dandruff
and excess of oil. leaving the scalp clean and
pliant, and assuring a beautiful growth of
long, lustrous, fluffy hair.
No Powder Necessary.—Yes, powder has
ruined more complexions that it has aided,
and while you use it you can hope for nothing
better than an imitation of a real complexion.
Take” my advice. Get from your chemist some
ordinary cleniinite and dissolve in a little
water, then you will have an ideal yet inex-
pensive lotion, which seems a part of the skin,
The result lasts all day long even under the
most trying conditions. To prepare the face
and neck for an evening in a hot ballroom
there is nothing to equal this simple and harm-
less lotion.
If Eyelashes are Short.—Eyelashes will be
greatly beautified if mennaline be applied at
lash-roots with thumb and forefinger. A
few such treatments make them grow long,
silky and curly. Thin, straggly eyebrows will
grow thick and lustrous by merely rubbing
mennaline on, but be careful and don’t get
any where no hair is wanted.
Wonderful Growth of Hair.—Long ago I
made a resolution to try and concoct a real
hair grower. My own formula, now perfected
after tedious experimenting, has had the effect
ot giving me a wealth of hair that is surpris-
ing. Obtain from your chemist an original
package of boranium and mix with this i-piut
of bay rum. Hub this into the scalp night
and morning with the finger tips. It sets the
hair roots tingling with new life. [6

The Scientific Scaler.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
f Sydney Post Office clock was strik-
| the hour of three. Upon the
second stroke the front doors of the
various city banks closed with a simultane-
ous crash. At the third stroke a young man
detached himself from a post in George-
street and leisurely entered the jewellery
establishment of Sparkel Bros. He was well-
dressed and of good appearance, but as he
stood in the shop swinging his cane and
glancing about with an air of indecision, he
gave forth an unmistakable odor of gum-
leaves.
“Willie Merino down for Easter,” ob-
served the manager to his assistant. “Get
him —quick ! He’s going to change his mind !”
The assistant darted out and cut off his
retreat.
“Yes. sir?”
“Gh —er, I was thinking of looking at
some gold watches, but it just occurred to
me”-
“This way, sir.”
With apparent reluctance he allowed him-
self t>' be towed to a case of gold watches,
concerning which the assistant was about to
expend considerable hot air when the visitor
interrupted him.
“Let me tell you what I want it for. A
young fellow saved my life recently at great
risk of his own—motor-boat capsize—can’t
swim young man dashed in, chanced sharks
and all that, and brought me ashore in spite
of my frantic efforts to drown us both ! Ha !
Ha! So you see I want something good.
Now, what about this—no, I won’t have that
—nor that —nor —what’s this? Fifty
guineas! ’M, don’t fancy it much. How
about this one—eh! Eighty pounds? Well,
it looks some class.”
He weighed it carefully in his hand, put it
to his ear, opened the case, and squinted into
it.
* Guess that will about do. Eighty pounds,
eh? A bit stiff, isn’t it? Bedrock? Ah,
well, I’ll take it.”
The assistant was about to make up the
package when the other placed a hand on his
arm and looked him squarely in the eye.
“I shall have to pay by cheque.”
The assistant stopped dead, raised his eye-
brows slightly and pursed his lips.
“Against your rules of business, eh? Well,
a man' can’t carry £BO about him in cash.
At least, I never do.”
The shopman “llem’d” nervously and
looked at the clock. The customer followed
his glance and gave a start.
. “After three, by George! I didn’t notice
it was after banking hours. But, look here ;
where’s the manager. I think this matter
can be fixed up.”
The assistant, with one hand on the watch,
semaphored across the shop, and the mana-
ger approached. The ciicumstances being
explained to him, his face at once assumed
an air of regretful non-compliance with the
request. It was an expression he had prac-
tised specially for such emergencies, and one
which he could produce at a moment’s notice.
The customer seemed surprised at first, then
smiled and shook his head slowly. Evidently
the refusal of his cheque was a quite novel
experience, and struck him as rather whimsi-
cal. He said he had often dealt at Sparkel
Bros, for cash. Did not the manager re-
member him? The manager regretfully
owned up to an entire ignorance of ever hav-
ing seen him before. Well, was it a refer-
ence the manager required? The manager
thought that a reference might—-
“Then ring up the proprietor of the New
Holland Hotel. My name is Clarke—Septi-
mus Howard Clarke, of Fleeceton Station,
New England.”
“Thank you, Mr. Clarke. If you will ex-
cuse me a moment”-——
He went away and rang up the New Hol-
land Hotel. The fact of the telephone being
situated at the farther end of the shop
enabled the manager to describe Mr. Clarke’s
personal appearance minutely without being
overheard by that gentleman. The New Hol-
land manager said he would have no hesita-
tion in trusting Mr. Septimus Clarke to the
extent of a couple of hundred. Sparkel’s
manager replaced the receiver, signalled “line
clear” to the assistant, and returned to his
visitor with an apologetic squirm. After re-
ceiving the protestations and prostrations
of both, Septimus placed the watch in his
pocket and left the shop.
Twenty minutes later he entered the fish
saloon of Garlicus Oisterpatti and called for
a plate of prawns. At the same table sat
Abraham Lewis, dealer in second-hand jewel-
lery. Garlicus waited upon Septimus in
person, and the latter engaged him in conver-
sation. He addressed him as “Bob,” and
appeared to know him well, which rather
surprised Abraham.
“Look here. Bob! I made a regular mess
of things to-day, and no mistake. You re-
member that motor-boat capsize off Brad-
ley’s last Wednesday? No? Well. if doesn’t
matter, only that I’m the man who fell in—
can’t swim a stroke, you know! Young
Mclvell, who was with me, jumped in and
brought me ashore. Well, I’ve been thinking
it was up to me to make him a presentation,
so I went to-day to Snarkels and bought a
watch —here it is. The price was £BO
—fair dinkum ! See, here’s the receipt.”
“But you—you say you mak’ a mess,” said
Garlicus.
“Well. I find now lie’s srot a gold watch.
That’s the mess! The thing’s no good to me,
because I’ve also got a gold watch—see
re !”
'That ees bad.”
“Rotten, I call it. The only thing to do
now is to get someone to take it off my hands.
I’d let it go for forty.”
Abraham looked interested and murmured
sympathy. Septimus acknowledged the re-
mark stiffly, and went on talking to the pro-
prietor. After all, he didn’t think he would
sell it. Didn’t like the idea of losing
£4O. Besides, there was always some-
one’s birthday coming along—what with
nephews and
“Would you mind me looking at it?” in-
terrupted Abraham, suavely.
Septimus handed it over, together with the
receipt. lie appeared to regard Abraham
with more favor than at first. He said he
hated doing things in this way—made him
look like a Jew peddler, but if Abraham had
a genuine desire to get a good article cheap
this was a splendid opportunity. He could
safeguard himself by ringing up Sparkel
Bros. In fact, Septimus would prefer that
he should do so. Abraham apologetically
accepted the invitation, and with Garlicus’s
permission rang up the firm.
It was a pale and very perturbed manager
who received the inquiry. Clearly he had
been taken in. Less than half an hour
previously this man Clarke had obtained an
eighty-pound watch from him by means of a
cheque, and now he was trying to sell the
article for £4O cash !
‘‘Don’t let him leave the premises!” he
bellowed back. “Haggle with him. Try
and beat him down—anything at all to de-
tain him while I get the police on his track.
The scoundrel!”
Septimus was easily detained. Tilting
back his chair he ogled the ceiling and
trifled with a toothpick while the agitated
Abraham carried on a pretence of bargain-
ing with him. Considering the dangerous
game he was carrying on, the man from
New England was surprisingly indifferent to
the flight of time, and amazingly obdurate
in the matter of price. lie refused £35,
and was just considering an offer of
£37 when the manager of Sparkel Bros.,
accompanied by a policeman, walked in.
The sight appeared to give Septimus a
shock. He turned pale and sprang to his
feet, then sat down again, folded his arms,
and glared at the jeweller as the latter,
with a preliminary “There he is, that’s the
fellow !” gave him into custody. The nature
of the charge had been already explained to
the policeman, and that intelligent officer,
ignoring the indignant protestations of Sep-
timus, took him by the sleeve and led him
away. As he went he loudly expressed the
humiliation he felt at being lumbered like a
common thief, and fervently hoped he would
meet no one he knew. Nevertheless, lie
resolutely declined to pay for a cab or to
travel in one if it was brought.
The number of acquaintances he met on
the way was amazing, and each in turn was
the last person he wanted to meet under such
circumstances. His readiness to salute
them struck his guardian as rather strange,
but not so strange as the fact (hat none of
them returned the salute—with the excep-
tion of an old gentleman who appeared to be
rather short-sighted, and who immediately
afterwards stopned and stared at the prisoner
with an expression of nurple indignation.
“Look here,” said Septimus, as they neared
the station, “do you’ know what you ale
doing? What right: have you to arrest me?
The cheque has not been presented yet—how
do you know it won’t be paid? I tell you,
officer, you are acting illegally, and if you
put the matter to the Crown Law Office
they’ll tell you the same.”
It was only after his charge was safely
lodged in the cells that the force of his re-
marks struck the policeman. It also struck
his superior officer when the case was put to
him, and that functionary immediately
’phoned the Crown Law Office. The expert
opinion was brief and emphatic, and next
moment the prisoner was given his liberty so
hurriedly that it almost amounted to as-
sault.
At 10 o’clock next morning the assistant
from Sparkel Bros, was waiting on the steps
of the A.B.C. Immediately the door swung
open he rushed in and presented Septimus
Clarke’s cheque, then braced himself for the
shock that was to follow. He got it, but
it was not the one he looked for. The shock
consisted of the nromnt payment of the
cheque! With £BO in his pocket the
young man walked slowly and thoughtfully
back to the shop and passed the shock on to
his superior. And before that gentleman
had time to recover, the postman entered and
handed him a solicitor’s letter, which, after
setting forth the social position of his client,
Mr. Septimus Howard Clarke, of Fleeceton
Station, New England, and his unimpeach-
able record, went on to describe the ignominy
and humiliation he had suffered through his
wrongful and illegal arrest at the hands of
Messrs. Sparkel Bros., and the immense and
incalculable damage to his reputation conse-
quent upon his being marched through the
streets like a common felon. Now, what
were Messrs. Soarkel Bros, going to do about
it?
What they did about it was to hand Sep-
timus a consolation cheque for £450, together
with a letter of apology. And the dazed
manager would willingly give a good round
sum from his own pocket to anyone who could
settle for him the following conundrum :
Was it all merely a horrible blunder, or was
the whole thing a “ready”? Communications
to be marked “strictly nrivate.”
W. G. DAVIS.
A Bush Grange.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(After reading Herrick’s “His Grange, or Private
Wealth.”)
A hut
I have, among the hills, and there
I shut
These days the little door on Care.
Rooms four
I count, snug built, with windows small,
Stained floor,
And sketch (by Conder) on the wall.
A shelf
I keep, with cheese and wine and bread.
Blue del'f,
Jugs, jars and autumn apples red.
The land
I own, bush, creek and sky—to-day,
And stand
And see my yard stretch miles away.
An axe
I boast, to chop dead logs for fire;
Two sacks
(For bark and chips) to blaze it higher.
A lamp
(Well filled) I sometimes take, and guide
To camp,
The traveller, through dark ways, with
pride.
A bird
I know, perched on the window sill.
Is heard
To peck for scraps, and chirp, and trill.
And wife
I have, who plays me Back o’ nights ;
Where strife
None is, the hush has all delights.
VICTORIA.
LOUIS ESSON.

Two friends saton the wharf, the last day of their holidays.
“Well ! we've had a jolly time," said the first man. “To-morrow, vunse luck,
we must go back again to the same old round. In a few weeks we shall forget all out
good times. Our precious holiday is over and done with. It seems like sheet waste .
“Speak for yourself," said the other. “My holiday has not been throw n awav.
Here in my Kodak I shall carry home all our happy days. You have nothing to
show for your holiday —hut, of course, a holiday without a Kodak is a holiday
wasted.”

The Australian War kSong
Dedicated to the Australian
'.8 R ft
Expeditionary Force*
TRALIA
AU
Words by FRANK JOHNSTONE.
Music by ROBERT JOHNSTONE.
Copyright
We’re from the land of gum trees, where the sun is mighty hot,
Where the ’possum and the kangaroo reside ;
From the far-back country and from many a lonely spot,
Where vou have to know your way without a guide.
From the land of tea and damper, from the forest and the plain,
We have come to help our kindred in the war , .
Because their blood, and ours, too, is of the same old strain
It’s British, and we don’t ask any more.
We can rough it with the hardest when the grub is getting thm.
And we’ll take our whack of hardship with the best ;
And when it comes to lighting we will take a big look m
We’ll be handy, very handy, to the rest.
We are lively in the saddle, and we all hang on like ginc.
And we’re'all a trifle handy with the gun;
But, beyond it all’s a feeling, it’s in ns through and through.
That each and every one is Britain s son.
We offer to our country our services and lives,
And that’s as much as any man can give;
It's hard to oart from parents, from sweethearts and from wives,
And go where it’s so mighty bad to live.
The girls we leave behind ns won’t forget ns when away,
Though many of us never will come hack ;
Their loving words will cheer ns through many a weary day
When facing bullets on the Belgian track.
Then here’s to “King and Country,” boys, to good old Britain’s Isle,
To all her bully hoys from every clime ; .
The lads who want no pressing, who go forward with a smile,
And face the music gamely every time!
And here’s"to bright Australia, our sunny home so dear;
Good luck to all the friends who were so kind.
We’ll try to keep our end up, and in the charging cheer
Be sure Australia won’t lie far .behind !
Chorus. —For Britain! good old Britain 1
Where our fathers first drew breath,
We’ll fight like true Australians,
Facing danger, wounds or death.
With Britain’s other gallant sons
We’re going hand in hand;
Our War-cry “Good old Britain.” hoys.
- Our own dear motherland.

A Simple Recipe for Clearing
the Complexion.
Exposure to hot winds and sun makes the
nerves and muscles of the face tired and life-
less, and the skin shrivelled, wrinkled and
unhealthy.
If anyone with a sallow, blotchy, wrinkled
or pimply complexion will only take a little
trouble in following the directions here given
they will find a marvellous improvement in
their complexion. Get some Puritol Wax
from the chemist or draper, and apply at
night, washing off in the morning with warm
water and soap. Then lap a little cold
water 10 times upon the face with the hands,
allowing it to get thoroughly round the
closed eyes. The treatment should be fol-
lowed out in every detail to ensure success,
and undoubtedly accounts for many of the
clear complexions so much admired by those
who do not yet know the secret. See that
you get the right kind, it will not injure the
most delicate complexion.*

POINTS
[Signed by deputy, and in the hopes that the Law
wont notice the difference, the actual perpetrator
bung temporarily blind or thereabouts.—S H
I Rior, 214 Ceorge-street, Sydney.]
A war item of the week was the Czar’s
announcement that he would enter Berlin
even if it cost him his last soldier. Here-
abouts is a subject for a picture. It would
show the last German and the last Russian
killing each other at the entrance to the
Unter den Linden, while the Czar and his
maiden aunt marched proudly down the
great avenue into the empty city—the city of
the dead.
The size and swallowing capacity of the
conquering Bear were nicely indicated in a
two-line cable message of last week. This
was the hurried testimonial:—
mi,„ t> ■ London, Monday Afternoon.
The Russians are spreading west of Lembero-
engulfing other Austrian fortresses. g ’
Isn’t “engulfing” a beautiful word? And
how many fortresses and cities will the
Lear need to engulf before its tremendous
appetite is satisfied? No wonder that Ger-
man ambassador shook like a wet dog when
he ambassed the calamitous ultimatum that
roused the sleeping animal.
Ihe people who pray for peace or rain
or other blessings are a curious crowd It
was announced in the United States on
September 9 that October 4 was set apart
as a day when prayer was to be offered up
01 the cessation of the war. During the
intermediate 25 days men would be
slaughtered, women turned into widows,
“ ade fatherless, cities and towns
crops burned, and all manner of horrors
I cipetrated. Now, if there was any real
i '“L P™y® r > J vh -V didn’t all the churchy
hosts °f Uncle Sam pray on September 5,
and thus save 24 days of devastation?
Cabled that a certain Professor Hale,
“one _of the leading educationalists of
America,” believes the United States should
declare war against Germany. The United
States has an ill-disciplined force of about
00,000 men. .Tolfre and co., rushing to the
front, would tread on the thing, mistaking
it for a mosquito.
If the Australian Labor party’s streak of
luck at next election is as notable as the one
it has just enjoyed, the last Liberal will
vanish out of politics about 1017. Then it
is to be hoped the Caucus will be abolished,
anil the Ministry be elected openly by a joint
sitting of the two Houses. The only excuse
for the. Caucus is that it is advisable to keep
the deliberations of the party a secret from
its opponents. When there are no opponents
wliat reason is there for secrecy?
[James Edmond, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
Andrew Fisher is busy forming his third
Federal Ministry, which is the tenth Minis-
try Australia has had in 14 years. Also, it
is the fourth Labor Ministry. Alfred Dealcin,
like Fisher, ran three Governments. The
other men—Barton, Watson, Cook and the
double-barrelled Reid-McLean had one turn
apiece. In Cook’s case it was clearly one
turn too many, but the country could have
stood another spell of Deakin, if only because
he is such a perfect gentleman.
[James Edmond, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
Liberalism did itself a bad turn in Vic-
toria when it decided not to oppose Janies
Mathews for Melbourne Ports. The Liberal
idea undoubtedly was that, if Mathews were
returned unopposed, the Ports vote for the
senate would be comparatively small. The
local Labor organisation regarded this Lib-
eral strategem as a challenge and got busy
in a remarkable manner. Prior to the elec-
tion Mathews addressed frequent meetings,
as though he were contesting the seat with
an opponent, and his supporters worked
among the electors on the same principle.
Un polling-day motor-cars and vote-getting
vehicles of all descriptions swarmed through-
out the electorate, while armies of can-
vassers took the division street by street
and sucked it dry of Labor voters. The
result of these tactics was that the Ports’
vote was a record for the Commonwealth.
Drank llentv, Glen Iris, Victoria.]
FOR HIS COUNTRY'S SAKE!
....There was a long discussion, but eventually- the
Council accepted the Assembly’s amendments and
decided not to insist on its own amendments. The
Hill, therefore, has passed both Houses in practically
its original form. —Daily paper.
Victoria's political fat boy falls into line at
last—but rather reluctantly.
The suspension habit is becoming very
strong in these days of war and calamity.
Among other things it is proposed to suspend
the rents of the unemployed Government
tenants in the Rocks area of Sydney, to do
a similar suspension at Dacey ville; to sus-
pend the awards of the N.S.W. Wages
Boards ; to suspend the building of the capi-
tal at Dryberra ; and to suspend increments
in the Public Service. It is also suggested
in some quarters that all work on the N.
Territory railway should be suspended.
Leans have taken the matter in their own
hands and suspended themselves. In fact
everything will soon be suspended except the
importing craze.
[James Edmond, 214 Ceorge-street, Sydney.]
“Moana” from Maoriland :
The recent yelp to have the Maoriland general elec-
tion postponed indefinitely appears likely to end in
noise only. The Government is very much against
the proposal, feeling that this period of Imperial
activity is Massey's opportunity. The Opposition
feels the same way, and so it is rushing round with
petitions demanding that the election be postponed
“until after the war is over.” In the name of
.Heaven, why? We may all be dead before the war
is over. Wars have been known to last a hundred
years. This rush to take a half day off to witness
the European mix-up is wearisome. Of course those
in the House of Reps, who know they are going out
on their ears want to put off the day of dis-
aster. They are anxious to go on drawing £3OO
per annum a little longer, but Maoriland doesn't feci
inclined to oblige them. Having waited three years
to hit some of them with an axe it is anxious to get
to the job.
ENCOURAGING NEWS..
Labor has won the elections Australia must
now endure Labor rule, in addition to the war
scourge and the drought scourge, etc., etc.
—Melbourne Argus.
The event will be seized upon by the
Umpire’s enemies as the basis of further lies about
the loyalty of the Dominions. The German neople
will, in all probability, be told that the Australian
Government has been put out of office for promising
troops to assist the Mother Country.
—Sydney TELEGRAPH..
THE SAME OLD FOREIGNER: “ Only a small
item; but every little cheers and helps us
on .”
[Done by Edward Colles, Belgrave-street, Manly,
N.S.W.]
‘‘Palace of Peace”: When the last rifle
has been discharged and all the treaties have
been written out afresh on flimsy paper, I
suppose the surviving kings and emperors
will once more start the useless old game of
appointing one another colonels-in-chief and
idmirals. The Kaiser showed how he valued
his foreign complimentary honors when he
(this is the cable, mind!) sold his British
decorations and ordered his officers to do
likewise. If these things have any value at
all they should only be conferred on persons
of undoubted respectability who are not
likely to enter into rash speculations, be-
come hard up, and pawn them.
Willy Watt is now entitled to rank with
Wolsey, Lucifer and other victims to their
own ambitions. Watt resigned the Vic-
torian Premiership and graciously accepted,
amidst a tumult of protest, the safe Federal
seat of Balaclava, in the full confidence of
a Fusion victory, and a place for himself
in a reconstructed Cook Cabinet. But.
alas, he is now condemned to the chilly
obscurities of Opposition—-luckily for his
health. lie has been Postmaster-General,
Treasurer and Minister of Labor, and twice
Premier-Treasurer. So perhaps a rest will
do him good.

The spots of the tame Freetrader and the
useless importer may be changed, but they
won’t wipe off. Thus Sydney Telegraph,
which brays for the futile animals (4/9/14) :
Anri it may be that in a couple of years hence
our very pencils will be branded “Made in Rhode
Island,” or “Made in Ceylon,” instead of “Made in
Bavaria.”
What a relief to know that, if the worst
comes to the worst, the impotent, worthless
Australian can rely on the hand and brain
of the New Yark cheap-jack and the nigger
of India’s coral strand.
[John Barr, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
_ The Senate has a problem before it. The
Constitution provides that, at its first meet-
ing after a dissolution, the Federal Upper
House shall divide itself into two sections —
one timed to face the electors again at the
end of three years, the other being assured
of its salary for six.years. When the Senate
met for the first time, in 1901, it selected
the last three on the polling lists in each
State to face the executioner first. But the
election of a Liberal, Shannon, at the head
of the South Australian Senate list, on
the combined Labor and Liberal vote, may
be sufficient excuse for the caucus to depart
from the, precedent of 1901.

Sydney, last week, Frederick Squire Farnell,

Before he possessed the cow he had to be

King George's message to the
Governments of the self-governing
Dominions contains too many per-
sonal pronouns in the possessive"
ease. It is as though he were put-
ting himself in direct contradistinc-
tion to the Kaiser. “My whole
Empire,” “my voice,” “my King-
dom,” “my naval and military
forces.” “the calamitous conflict is
not of my seeking,” and so forth. A
constitutional king may be careful
enough to act constitutionally, but
lie can never think or talk of him-
self in a strictly constitutional way.
The circumstances of kingolatry
are too strong for him. The blessed
Anthem tells him that when the
Empire goes to war he is It. “Send
him victorious.”
Du list, of S.A. Assembly, made
a rather neat point at a meeting at
Greenock, in that State. Richard
Butler had been saying, in a
pleasant, patronising way, that the
many German-extraction residents
of that neighborhood could be
trusted to be loyal Australians.
“Certainly they can,” said Duhsl.
innocently. “The last British am-
bassador to Berlin, Goschen, is but
a naturalised Englishman. The
First Sea Lord, Louis of Batten-
berg, is another.” And Duhst
might have added that King
George’s own mother was a Dane naturalised
i o Britain, and his father’s father was a
German ditto, and his grandmother’s grand-
father’s grandfather reigned as George the
First of England without being able to speak
the English language at all.
“Quidam’s” eulogy :
Arthur Picard, librarian at the Mechanics’ Insti-
tute, Stawell (Vic.), who has just died, made some
history in his time. lie was wounded in the Franco-
Prussian war; picked up by a German ambulance as
a hopeless case; but recovered, and was returned on
parole. He proceeded to Ireland, and formed the
Irish Ambulance Corps. When the town of Chateaudun
surrendered to the Prussians a German soldier was
shot on the altar steps of a local church, whereupon
\on Wittich, the German general, demanded 20,000
francs, or threatened a second bombardment. Picard
paid 5000 francs from his own pocket, and persuaded
the German general to institute an inquiry, which
proved that the soldier had been accidentally shot
by his own revolver. This gained him a decoration
and a memorial in Chateaudun. Later on, he was
sentenced to death for having transported arms from
dead and wounded French soldiers through the Ger-
man lines. However, the slaughter never came off.
He earned about a do/.en decorations, and perhaps the
worst stroke of luck in a fortunate life was his death
at a time when there seems every chance of his
country avenging the crosses it endured in his youth.
COUP DE MAIN.—(A BOLD EFFORT.)
President Wilson, in a proclamation, calls upon
the people of the United States to pray for peace
in Europe. He has fixed October 4 as a day of
prayer.— Cable.
THE BIRD OF PEACE : “ Lei’s all pray.”
The beautiful ,T. H. B. Coates, general
manager of the National Bank of M.L.
for the last 21 years, is about to retire and
go in pursuit of a well-earned rest. He
has spent 46 years in the service of
the company, and it is mainly owing to
his energies as g.m. that the institution
has attained its present satisfactory posi-
tion. An effort was made to induce him
to remain on the bridge a little longer, for
men with his wide knowledge of finance are
of value just now; but Coates, who is 63,
shook his head. He will probably go on
the board of directors.
CHANTY.
(For use principally on jerry-steamers and horse-
punts.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ho! the owners took a tumble,
And they chucked the skipper out;
His bundle’s down the gangway,
His ticket’s up the spout.
But we’re not going to sling the ship
A-spite of Nan and Flo,
So man the capstan bars, you blokes,
And: “So-long, Joe!”
His bos’n’s name was Irvine.
We called him Freezin’ Bill :
You couldn’t meet him on the deck
Without you caught a chill.
And when with cold sou’-easter gales
The time was ripe for rum,
He handed lemon water out,
Which turned the “innards” numb.
We all was hard junk whaling men
Who’d signed for grounds afar,
Me pottered round the bloomin’ bay
And never crossed the bar.
And if we chipped him, askin’ when
He’d turn her beak to sea,
He’d snarl inside his whiskers,
And he’d talk of mutiny.
The owners came aboard of us ;
Says they: “M r ell, what's the catch ?”
For any blinded old excuse
The skipper had to scratch.
“The crew was such a rotten lot
I never left the bay.”
Moll, strike us pink!” the owners said.
“Come orf and draw your pay!”
They sent another skipper on.
We’d sailed with him afore.
He’s not the sort to funk a bar
Or hug a ti-tree shore,
So walk her round and heave her up,
There’s grub and grog to spare.
M iHi Andy Fisher at the helm
We’ll sail for We-don’t-care.
It’s Billy Hughes is bos’n now,
We’ve sailed with William, too.
He is the queerest little cuss
That ever wind blew through.
He cocks his head just like an owl,
And, love us ! what a tongue !
When Billy passes compliments
You’d wish that you was hung.
But William knows the whalin’ tricks
(And many is the same),
There isn't much to teach to him
About the deep-sea game.
And if the whalin’ season's slack,
He tries the trawlin’ ground.
When Billy throws his bit of bait
Yer know (here’s somethin’ ’round.
So heave, he3'-ho ! and mind your eye,
The mud-hook’s showing out,
Her boko’s pointed to the sea—-
It’s there the big game spout.
So, ho revo to Nan and Flo,
And one, two, three for a last heave-ho.
But you was a rotten skipper,
So it’s, “Good-bye, Joe!'
