“Some time ago I suffered a severe ner-
vous breakdown. I cannot describe the
symptoms, but I know I was in a most
dreadful state of hfealth,” writes Mrs. R.
Clarke, of 385 Cardigan Street, Carlton, Vic’
A friend persuaded me to try Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills, which I did. The re-
lief was almost instantaneous, and after a
course of some months I am pleased to say
they restored me to almost perfect healtli.
In addition I had suffered nearly all my life
from Biliousness and Sick Headaches,
which since the use of your pills I rarely
have an attack of, and then only in a mild
form. I beg to thank you very much for
all the benefits derived from the use of the
pills; in fact, I would not be without a
bottle in the house if they cost me the last
shilling I possessed. I am sending you this
from sheer gratitude. You may ' publish
this if you so desire.”
The above testimonial, written in a simple,
convincing manner, is only one among manv
of those received from people who have
derived the greatest benefit from taking Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills. Many of these
people had tried other so-called remedies
until they had despaired of getting any bene-
fit whatever. No other medicine, whether
it be pill or tablet, liquid or solid, has ever
been so universally and successfully used
by all classes of the community, both rich
and poor. They are not advertised as a
great “Cure-All,” but as a safe, sure and
reliable remedy for the little ills of life, for
which they are unexcelled.*
SPORTING
NOTIONS
Tlie i 'turn scrap between Les Darcy and
Fritz Holland at Sydney Stadium'on Saturday night
found t.ie Maitland blacksmith a much-improved
boxer, and all the skill and ring generalship of Fritz
Holland could r.ot prevail against his youthful vim.
lie revelled in the battle. Holland drove some
terrific rights on to his dial in the first round, but
Darcy regarded them as so much thistledown.
Although there was practically no difference in their
weights, Holland scaling list. 1 Jib. to Darcy’s list.
3Jib., the Australian was much the stronger of the
two, and he could hit about twice as hard as his
lighter-looking opponent. His chief trouble was over-
eagerness. He went berserk, and gave the wary
American chances. Holland started out with the
deliberate intention of closing up the hostile army’s
left eye, and twice he got on to it with the right
and made it bulge considerably. Later on he cut it,
but-the optic was never put out of action, and when
Holland tried to besiege the right eye, he had no luck.
Darcy, by the way, has developed considerable agility
with his head, and the way he flung it about, over
his shoulder, or down between his knees, or under
his arm caused Holland to miss badly and frequently.
It was a splendid battering match, and they slogged,
both of them, with all their souls-and both their
hearts. Had -Darcy been quicker he would have scored
a knock-out early in the fight, for so great was his
force and his imperviousness to punishment that
Holland’s straight left could not keep him off, and
the light-footed Yankee had to back and side-step to
get away from the bull-like rushes. There were times
when Darcy’s head was knocked right back with the
speed of his own arrival when Holland met him with
the left. But Holland’s luck was right out. He
made many attempts, but never succeeded in meeting
those bovine charges with one on the point. Darcy’s
mouth was always in the way, and Darcy didn’t seem
to mind what he got on'the mouth. Holland required
a horseshoe in his glove as a mascot. And it wasn’t
there. He fought a clever, cool fight, but he was
being steadily walloped and over-pointed. When,
suddenly, Darcv went mad, or became blind in his
wild eagerness. He had piled up a considerable
margin pf points when he commenced to hit low.
Blow after blow landed below the belt, and once, duck-
ing, he swung his head into Holland’s stomach.
Once Holland.was down from what was certainly an
illegal blow, which hurt him considerably, and his
courage and grit in getting up and seeing the round
through made him the popular hero. Darcy lost his
friends in one brief minute. Although warned by the
referee he whaled into Holland, and, utterly reckless
of where and how he hit, roughed and hustled Holland
across the ring, hurled him on to the ropes, hit him
10 times simultaneously, fell upon him, stepped back
and swung another foul blow —and was disqualified.
And 'he richly deserved it.
A crowd of 10.000 at Sydney Cricket
Ground watched the final of the Rugby League’s com-
petition for the City Clip. It, was a dull, miserable
afternoon of dirty grew and the Cricket Ground was
not the most comfortable place on earth. But the
enthusiasm of the contending teams sent a warm
breath over the bleak landscape. The high contending
parties were Eastern Suburbs and South Sydney, and
they seemed so evenly matched, and both so sound
in defence that the game threatened to end in a score-
less draw. Neither side notched a point in the first
half. East was hard pressed at the opening of the
second half, but eventually managed to clear the
line, and following up with a counter charge broke
through and seored amidst tremendous excitement.
Messenger failed to add the extra points. Again East
scored, and once more the wind blew Messenger’s kick
wide. Then South came on the wings of the wind,
scored and converted a try, and East was six to five.
The excitement became intense, and South, which had
won the premiership, made desperate efforts to win
the double. Rushing down the field the team, with
the hands of the clock trembling to the moment, fed
Hallett, and right on the moment he kicked for goal.
It fell short, and as it curved downward and struck
the earth, the whistle blew, ard Eastern Suburbs had
won by a margin of one point.
In tlie Union division the excitement
centred around the struggle between North Svdnev
and Western Suburbs for the supremacy of the “B”
division of the First-Grade Competition'. North Svd-
ney astonished tlie natives, itself and everyone else by
the way it got over the formidable Western team,
which never had a dog’s chance. It was stamped flat,
rolled in the dirt, and jumped upon by 13 to three.
Had West secured a draw or a victory it would have
had the honors ; hut a defeat left the two sides dead
level in the competition, and a deciding battle will
be played off next Saturday.

Tattersall’s Club meeting at Randwick
(N.S.W.) on Saturday last is regarded as being
equal to the best. About 30,000 people were present.
There were 122 starters for the £3OOO added money
put up by the club, and some of them came from
Victoria, Queensland and Maoriland. The course
being good the racing was fast and apparently clean.
The only fly in the marmalade was the fall of three
horses in the steeple, but as neither riders nor horses
were seriously hurt that little incident probably added
to the excitement. Not a single owner or jockev
scored a double event, so the prize-money was well
distributed, if the betting monev was not. In the
Novice Handicap the chestnut four-vear-old Speed-
fast, starting at nine-to-two against, stamped his
way to the front at the tail end of time, and won
easily by four lengths, having cut out the six fur-
longs in lmin. 14see. The Tramway Handicap was
•a joyful circumstance for the man who imagines he
can pick winners. In this case the public splosh went
Jargelv on Royal Laddie at threes, with Gigandra next
best-liked at sixes. The Laddie went to the front
early, and the c-hances looked all against his ability
to stay there, but he did it, beating off Tofua, Amata
anu Gigandra in tbat order, and winning by a neck
in lmin. 26?ee. for the seven furlongs. The gilded
plum of the meeting, the Chelmsford Stakes, lured °2
starters with its swag of £lOOO added monev. The
sweepstake amounted to £220. Again the pickers of
horses were in luck. After piling their coin on
VYoorak at five to four against, they had Hie satis-
faction of watching that noble neddie shake all others
off and romp home two and a half lengths from
Mountain hnight, with the exhausted Ravello third
Moorak had covered the nine furlongs in lmin!

. .f.,' something more than a possi-
bility in the corning Derbies, for the win was accom-
plished i A stylish company, and the opposition included
no less than 10 other Derby candidates. Also, the
colt humped a seven-pound penalty, and won con-
vincingly with something in reserve, up his sleeve
as they say in the classics. The boil-over of the meet-

cap, a mile and a quarter spasm worth 600 added
sovereigns, for which Lochano started favorite at
fours, with Limelight second favorite at sevens. Evi-
dently the man with the hag knew something, and
so did the punters, when the 33 to one chance, St.
Spasa, came round the outside and beat Firm and
Pongo, winning from Firm by half a neck. A most
spectacular finish, but a disastrous one for the public.
And so was the Welter, though not quite so bad.
It fell to the 10-to-one-ehance Giru, while Kings-

pared to his usual experiences.
There was wild excitement- in Sydney
lawn tennis circles last Saturday, when the Eastern
Suburbs team, which had come through the prelimi-
naries and ali the semi-final stages without a single
check, came to a regular Lemberg at Double Bay.
Sydney I. defeated the so-far-victorious team by 11
sets to five. It was terrific.
The chief excitement among the followers
of Soccer in Sydney on Saturday was the inter-
national struggle, Australia versus England, at the
ancient Eppifig ground. It was a one-sided affair, in
which Australia, at half-time, led by two goals to
nil. During the second half the Australians added
three more while the struggling English team could
make only one. Australia therefore was victorious
dead easy with five goals to one. What the Soccer
game in Sydney requires is a conscientious and reli-
able press agent. The game is never adequately
described, neither is there any proper record given
of the progress of the various competitions, so that
it is quite impossible to get a coherent view of the
game as a whole. But it is the fault of the lords
ot the game. To report all the Soccer matches alone
would require an army of about 40 reporters on each
paper. The lords of the game should issue sum-
maries of the day’s casualties and a brief review of
the campaign as a whole.
* *• * * *
~N. S. Wales 25-miles’ walking ehampion-
•“Pft Parramatta on Saturday almost shook the
Ma State s old town off its axis. The event began
after the noon-day meal, and the competitors tore like
mad round and round the vehicular roadway that lies
in the shadow of the sometime Government House,
now a residential portion of the King’s School. Also,
that pleasant way winds through the shadow of the
substantial and commodious lunatic asylum and gaol
The event ranked second to that of the famed march
ot the Israelites in 1451 n.c., as recorded in Joshua
w., LO the ones who tramped around Jericho for
seven days, after which the walls fell down flat. The
oldest son of the oldest native quaked lest something
ot the sort should happen to Parramatta, but what

Consider the progress of Englishman
Ernest Barry, who is piling up quite a monument
of victories in the sculling world. In world’s and
English championships, the scalps of Dick Arnst,
Harry Pearce, Jim Paddon, Geo. Towns, Billy Fog-
well (all from Australia), E. Durnan (Canada), and
\V. Albany, have all fallen to him; but to the bank-
sitter it looketh as if the knowledge of every ripple
of the Thames is the principal factor in his success.
Dick Arnst has thrown to the subscribing public the
notification that he is prepared to again tackle
Barry if the public raise £5OO “expenses”—a sup-
porter having guaranteed the “stake money” of £5OO.
The burly Richard has been shifting superfluous
tissue during the past three months, and if his scull-
ing punch returns in the proposed race against Jim
Paddon, the necessary boodle to make the trip may
be forthcoming. Everything rests on that clash
these giants of professional sculling.
According to the coble Arthur Barry
backed out of some arrangement to allow Australian
sculler James Paddon expenses. Barry contended
that the intention to allow expenses was . made before
the war started, when there was a reasonable chance
of the victor raking in stacks and shoals of gate-
money. But when the Kaiser’s war-note blew off
things changed, and, apparently, the crowds who
would otherwise have followed the boat-race were
preparing themselves for a struggle in which all
parties help themselves to expenses and other things.
So Barry decided that the changed conditions altered
the case. Paddon is said to have been furious. How-
ever, he is returning to Australia by the P. & 0.
boat Medina, and the Maoriland sportsman, Seifert,
who stood behind him in England, is said to be will-
ing to find his stake-money for a match with Dick
Arnst, presumably on the Parramatta.
Melbourne Age says that “Edward Barry,
if not absolutely the best sculler that England has
produced, is certainly the most popular.” Which is
nonsense. Professional sculling went out of popularity
m England when Hanlan came along and made a
holy show of Trickett, who had previously beaten the
local Sadler. The popular idols of their successive
days were, firstly, Chambers; then Kelly, who beat
< hanibers; and, lastly, James llenforth, who beat
Kelly. These men in their turn had always beaten
American challengers for the world’s championship.
Renforth and Kelly went to America to row a pairs
match, but at the starting post Renforth fell back
and died of heart disease, in Kelly’s arms. The tragic
scene was sculptured, in bas relief, on a Renforth
memorial erected in his native town of Newcastle
(Eng.) Both men were easily better than Joe Sadler,
who became nominal champion only when Renforth
was dead and the veteran Kelly had retired. Barry
is Britain s first representative oarsmen in 35 vears
and. meanwhile, the sport has ceased to be a matter of
national interest, for the reason that national pride
can get no gratification from it.

Steve Barker, who is second on the
Senate poil m Victoria, is the only member
of the Parliamentary Labor party who per-
sistently refuses to wear a tie. Instead he
favors a neat, ungaudy spring-stud, boldly
of C whiskers WO behind a c sS
[Frank Henty, Glen Iris, Victoria.]
B.” : Foreign ships were held strictly to
good behavior in Port Jackson when Gover-
nor ilnllip reigned at the Tank Stream,
lie set apart a space of the harbor where
they could anchor without being a menace
to the settlement. That place he considered
tar enough away to afford the settlers
advantages if need for hostilities should
arise. He described it as a large bay, with
great depth of water; had it surveyed and
sounded, and christened it Neutral Bay.
Thither all foreigners were to be towed and
there kept until their business in the port
was finished.
When “Charlie” McGrath was suspended
by the order of Joe Cook, it was freely
stated that one of the first acts of the next
Labor majority would be the expunging of
the suspension motion from the records of
the House. But McGrath won’t hear of it.
'lhere is more important business facing
us,” he says. “Let the. motion stand. It
will hurt the Fusion crowd more than it ever
worried me.”
[Morgan McDonnell Fay, Raglan-strect, Ballarat,
Victoria.]
Mrs. Elizabeth Muter, who died in Eng-
land in July, was well known in Maoriland.
Her husband, Colonel I). D. Muter, was one
of Canterbury’s earliest settlers. He went
through the Indian Mutiny and the Chinese
war. Mrs. Muter accompanied her hus-
band on active service, went through the
Mutiny, was present at the siege and cap-
ture of Delhi, was at the occupation of
Tientsin, and is said to have been the first
Englishwoman to visit Peking. After her
career of adventure she employed her time
in writing two good books: “Travels in
Maoriland, India and China,” and “Itecoh
lections of the Sepoy Ilevolt.”
Shannon, who headed the Senate poll in
S. Australia, should get a pension or a medal
or a portfolio or something of the sort, as
the only man whom practically everybody in
his State voted for. He was on both tickets,
for when McGregor died the Laborites had
to adopt the most promising of their oppo-
nents. ■ So Shannon, being compelled to be
all things to all men, may be described as
an Orange Pope from Belfast, or a Quaker
Napoleon, or a submarine aeroplane or auv
other contradiction. It would be a good
idea to make him Government representative
in the Senate, and also leader of the Opposi-
tion and President and Sergeant-at-Arms,
and likewise send him to London as Tali
Commissioner.
[James Edmond, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
A defunct soldier :■ —
Colonel Charles Fyshe Roberts, who died at Double
Tlay, Sydney, last week, aged 76, was son of Captain
Charles Roberts, of the 59th Regiment. His mother
was a Hornsby, and his wife a Goulburn Bradley.
He entered the Artillery in January, 1853, served in
the Crimea from March, 1855, and was wounded at
Sebastopol. Eater, he saw service in India in com-
mand of artillery under Colonel Gawler. Was men-
tioned by the Governor-General, and raised to be a
brevet-major. For three years in the mid-sixties
he was at Dawes Point Barrack (Sydney) in charge
of a battery of artillery, and acted for a time as
pi irate secretary and A.D.C. to Governor Young. In
1871 Roberts sold his commission and became Acinio-
- to the Agent-General, Charles - Cowper
( Slippery Charley ). The year 1576 found him in
command of the N. S. Wales Artillery and second in
command of the Forces. In 1892 he was appointed
Under-Secretary to the newly-established Defence
Department.
“R. 8.” revives some business memories :
Died at Melbourne, William Weatherly, whose
house at Brighton (Billilla) recalls Billilla sta-
tion, which he partnered with Chirnside before
Bowes Kelly’s purchase of a fourteenth of Broken
Hill put him on Easy-street. Sid Kidman had left
a dozen bullocks or so at Mt, Gipps, and McCulloch
gave him orders to remove them. Kidman couldn't
find a buyer with money, but lie found Sam Poole
with a fourteenth of Broken Hill, and Sam Poole
traded the fourteenth for the bullocks. Kidman hastened
_P a i r f w ifb the interest in Rasp's “mountain of
tin” in the horse paddock, and left it for sale with
Harris and Deiiger, brokers, of Silverton. Bowes
Kelly, representing Weatherly, George Kelly, and
another and himself, bought the fourteenth for £l5O.
dhe party didn’t think this a very splendid pur-
chase, but it held on; and the fourteenth, representing
1000 shares in the original 10,000-share company,
moved up in value to a million. Weatherly was'a
genial, kindly soul, and money didn’t spoil him.
The bullocks that Kidman traded to Poole are cer-
tainly dead by this time. Everything dies sooner or
later, and one of these days Broken Hill will be a
large, silent hole in the earth, with nothing in it
but a vacuum and some intervals in the atmosphere.

Business, Bobbery, etc.
Kuscobin”: Already Maorilanders are
I eckoning on the fillip that will be given to
the woollen manufacturers of England and
I I lance in the event of Germany being put
out of business. Now the woollen mills of
M.L., to mention only Kaiapoi, Mosgiel, Pe-
tone and Roslyn, make up tweeds the equal
of those woven anywhere, yet of recent years
they have been forced to act more and more
as distributors of over-sea woollens. The
duty and freight do not altogether equalise
the cheaper wages and cheaper working con-
ditions of Europe, so the local manufacturers
have been forced into tin's position. If the
fool politician were to thump up the tariff
the mills in Maoriland would be justified in
working up all the raw material on hand.
i.he Maonlander who to-day sneers at his
own excellent goods would then be forced to
wear them or go about in the little that was
left of imported stuff. There’d he more
practical patriotism in this than all the
anthem howling at the street corners, or the
tool pleasantries about saveloys that to-day
pass as the real thing in humor.
The bosses of the energetic Union Steam-
ship Co. of Maoriland must heave a whole
stomach-full of the sigh of relief over the
swift disappearance of the German banner
from the Pacific. A while ago Frank Coffee,
who is Australia’s most persistent traveller
across the Pacific, was asked what was the
biggest change he had lately noted in his
ocean wanderings. He didn’t have any
hesitation in saying that it was the rapid
growth of German trade in the islands. The
Union Co. still carried its house flag to many
of the groups and brought copra to Aus-
tralia, where it was put into German bot-
toms. It was primarily to collar' this trade
that the N.D.L. only two or three months
ago decided to open up a direct line with
.Maoriland, and the Union Co. felt sick at
the prospect of a still deeper Teutonic cut
into the profitable island trade. Now, unless
something dreadful happens, the Germans
won’t have many ships available for this
choice titbit, and the Union Co. will hold
its own and get a bit more into the bargain.
Meantime.-however, the copra trade, having
largely handed itself over to Germany, has
sustained one of the worst slumps on re-
cord. An island trader who had been pay-
ing £l6 .lately wrote that he was now
chancing it” at £3.
* * * *
F. 11. Faulding and Co., wholesale
druggists, Adelaide, write : —•
An Adelaide paper points out that “owing to the
stoppage of German supplies of anesthetics, it is
stated that Melbourne is faced with a serious shortage
of chloroform and ether. Chemists are already taking
steps to conserve their supplies, by selling only small
quantities of the drugs.” The anaesthetics, ether and
chloroform, are made from pure alcohol. The manu-
facture of these and hundreds of other products has
always been denied to English makers because the
British Government has refused concessions which have
been freely granted to manufacturers of similar goods
in Germany. The same restrictions on the use of
alcohol have retarded progress in Australia. In the
Lancet, which came to hand last week, it is explained
that ether made from methylated spirit is in use in all
the London hospitals. Such stuff is not good enough
for use in Germany, but owing to red tape and British
Government restrictions, it is quite good enough for
English-speaking people. It is to be hoped that the
authorities in Australia, as well as in England, will at
last realise that the prosperity of Germany, in the
chemical industry at any rate, is very largely due to
the liberal use of pure alcohol in the manufacture of
synthetic and other drugs and preparations.
By reason of the fact that the patriotic
and Freetrade Australian prefers German
pianos to the British or Australian manu-
factured instrument, practically the whole
of the trade is in the hands of the ravening
hordes who are spilling the blood of our
kindred and friends. At more than half the
patriotic concerts in Australia the National
Anthem. 'Britons Never Shall be Slaves,”
Advance Australia Fair,” and so on, are
belted out of pianos made at Blooduntguts-
burg, while the audience waves its imported
and anti-British hat enthusiastically. All
ine same, there is likely to be a shortage in
leuton-made pianofortes, and a party 'of
importers agents were hurried to* the
Lnited States by the last ’Frisco mailboat
to see what help Uncle Sam can give (he
useless, helpless, poor devil of a bushwhack-
ing Australian.
MAG. SULPH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Owing to the war Epsom salts have advanced 334
per cent, in price. 3
I did not much mind hearing- that the Germans died in
scores ;
1 did not care if Austrians descended on our shores.
Ihe enemy is not so bad although he has his faults
But just regard the awful rise in price of Epsom salts
(I Ear is Bell.)
It doesn’t matter much to me if shells fall in Berlin
I’d slap the Kaiser or, the head for all his frightful
sin.
I don t much care if Prussian corps are sent to
funeral vaults,
But damn the Kaiser when he skies the price of
Epsom salts.
(Loud cheers.)
1 hey ve shot the Town Hall at Louvain, they’ve killed
a pig in Ghent;
They’ve made a mess of Brussels, those scoundrels
Satan sent.
Tot 1 forgive them all they did—those hounds of
wursts and malts
But I can not forgive them for the rise in Epsom
salts.
{Kill Bill.)
The skulls of men bleach in the fields from Maubenge
to the Rhine,
And the grapes of next year’s vintages shall drip a
ghastly wine.
But now this verdammt Kaiser, he has crowned his
murderous faults,
And there’ll be a fearful reckoning for the rise in
Epsom salts.
{l’ll cut his braces!)
N.S.W,
RANDOLPH.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appeal on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
Square Dinlcum: The Bulletin’s ver-
dict is: When anybody offers you anything “free,”
have nothing to do with him. When he offers you
£3OO worth of anything for £l5O, avoid him.
When he says he is willing to give you his un-
paralleled £75 gold watch without charge in order
to advertise his ten-bob pants, kill him. When he
explains to you that he will give you a £lOO draw-
ing-room suite, and a grandmother, and a mother-in-
law, and £lO in cash provided you take them all
away in his free-gratis-and-for-notliing pantechnicon,
with a coupon thrown in, bury him. That is all the
wisdom that the paper has about it just now. Inci-
dentally, if you want to be robbed, the editor is
willing to sell you 1000 Mount Caimimlah copper
shares. They represent the last time he fell in....
J. Bromer: The “blanky’’ Australiiise lias been re-
printed in The B. It is published in “Baekhloeks
Ballads,” by O. J. Dennis (any Australian book-

Tour appeal for an extension of the idea
of the 44-hour working week is interesting. And as!
the trade returns for July are just in, we are struck -
by a coincidence. That month was the sort of pinna-
cle of a wonderful career. By reason of importing
much and. exporting little, not making anything-like
enough goods to supply our own needs, and borrow-
ing immense sums to pay for short hours and good
food and casual methods- —by reason of these things
the month of July saw Australia drifting into debt
at the rate of about £46,000,000 per annum. Do
you think this is a time to do less work than we
have been doing?. ... Arameo: (1) An ambassador is
a diplomatic agent; a consul a commercial represen-
tative. The former is generally of high political
rank in his own country, whilst the latter is selected
for his business ability ; (2) all accredited represen-
tatives of a foreign country are protected, and when
war is declared they are allowed to pack their port-
manteaux and are graciously escorted out the nearest
door; (3) the merchant ships of an enemy are always
liable to capture and confiscation in war time. There
are a number of agreements of the Hague Conference
of 1907 dealing with the subject (which you will find
in almost any of the locally-published pamphlets),
but international law is an extremely confused animal
at present, and any old thing might happen-'....

THE DRUNKARD.
I am down ! down ! down !
Down in the depths of sin
And man with his awful frown
Cares not the prick of a pin
Though lie may with a leer
Give me the price of a beer,
Yet I am down down down.
2.
Down in a sea of woe
My only pleasure to drink
As on the stream they go:
The rich who do nothing but think.
Once I was companion with these
Who now just passed with a snee/.e
For I am down down down.

AN ODE FROM A CAPTIVE.
All is quiet in the village.
1 sit and muse on a stump,
And dream-of a far away land.
Thousands of miles across the foam.
But Hark! The sound of battle
Awakes me from my dream.
And 1 am suddenly brought
To my senses by a bullet in my Thigh
I try to stagger to my feet
but alas it is all in vain
For suddenly looking ahead
I see a dirty German Swain
I am captured, and taken prisoner
And bound with horrid chains
For I know it will not be
for long
For I know the Australian Expeditionary
Force, is rapidly marching this way.

. ...R.C.C.: The N.S.W. Old Age Pensions Act was
introduced by William Lyne in 1900, and came into
force on August 1. 1901. It was based to a great
extent on the Maoriland system. The State Act was
superseded by the Commonwealth Act of 190 S-9. It
would be too wearisome a job to look up what George
Reid had to say about the N.S.W. Act. In fact, it
is too wearisome a job to look up what George Reid
said about anything. Sometimes he. said something
about something, but nothing: be said about things in
general called for attention. . . .Tomago Water: Web-
ster says origin is Jack Kangaroo; and Jackeroo is
just as correct as Jackaroo... .J. Kemp: Some State
floats a £3,000,000 loan in London. It collects the
money from the lenders and puts it in the London
branch of the Bank of N.S.W. or the Bank of Vic-
toria, or any other old bank that it banks with.
The Australian office of the bank has to, pay heaps
of money in London on behalf of its customers —
merchants who are sending money to Europe to pay
for imported goods. The bank acquires a great idea
or inspiration. Instead of carrying one lot of
sovereigns to England and carrying another lot to
Australia, with a great chance that they may be
wrecked on the way, it sets one lot against the
other —that is to say, it pays its own accounts in
London out of the loan and pays the Government its
£3,000,000 here with the money that its customers
deposited to pay for the imported hats and boots.
Sovereigns are only risked on the high seas when it
is absolutely necessary. This is the best explanation
we can offer... .Mid-Channel: Don’t remember seeing
it even. ...L. Mackintosh: Suggest it to one of the
dailies Recording Angel: You should know better
than to jamb your lines so close to one another.. ..
N.Z.: No use crying out “Woe!” on that subject;
Tiie Bulletin’s honorable censor would cry the same
thing, but he would spell it “Whoa 1 !” R, O’Hal-
loran: Like many other people, you seem to regard
the Australian manufacturer, who pays the ever-
increasing union wages for short hours of labor, as
a public enemy. Haven’t you anything to say about
the importer, whose duties don’t increase, and who
pays very poor wages to his serving men, and floods
this country with German and Austrian goods. Of
course, Australian manufacturers have “honorable
understandings” among themselves. Otherwise, how
could they live, with an almost invisible tariff and
unlimited cheap labor competition? Have you been
hired by Kaiser William, or what is wrong with you?
Y. : The Rev. Sam Marsden, the cen-
tenary of whose first visit to Maoriland is
soon to be celebrated, was the unintentional
cause of the Maori war. When he went
across the Tasman Sea in ISI4 he adopted
a young Maori boy. lloani Heke, and set
about teaching him the ways of the white
man. He took the youngster to Sydney,
where he used him as coach boy, and after-
wards carried him to England. Marsden
was jingoistic, and the power and might
of England were his constant themes. But
Heke had an affection for his race, and
began to feel that Sam’s religion and
patriotism would end in the subjugation of
Ins brown -brothers and the loss of their
lands. Finally, he broke away from Sam-
uel and returned to his people, with all the
knowledge of guns, powder, shot and shell
tbe corpulent parson had instilled. It was
Heke who, because of this knowledge, gave
the regiments of regulars sent from Sydney
such a hot time. lie was a skilful strate-
gist, but the time was not ripe, so lie threw
in bis alley and subsequently died a good
Christian at the age of 42. His luck was
out.
There are many who want to send winter
clothes to the Belgian refugees and the
British poor, but don’t know how to do it.
Any such may consign the goods to the
“Committee of Farmer’s Provident Society,
c/o Farmer and Co., BGo Kent-street.
Sydney,” and be satisfied that they will go
where they are likely to do most good. The
things may be either new or second-hand,
but if the latter they should be clean, whole
and free from moth. The destitute victims
of Germany’s tyranny are too wrapped up
in their sorrows to be able to do much wash-
ing (apart from the fact that they possess
neither soap nor washhouses), and they
can’t afford moth-brick. All gifts must be
labelled “For British and Belgian Poor.”
It is up to the well-to-do owners of winter
clothing in Sydney to get busy without
further delay.

of JAMES GRIFFEN, otherwise known as JAMES
fI’HARA GRIFFEN, late of Dunedin, Insurance

The Forest, the Sea and Myself.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At the end of a hard-fought year,
With wearied body and brain,
Lo! I turn to you again,
O Sea ! With an eager ear
I listen to catch your song
On the ocean beaches long.
The thunder of that refrain
Is echoing here inland;
The clashing of surf and sand
With the wind’s great reedy strain
Is taken up by the trees,
That whisper and start and sway
Like tiddlers trying to play—
An orchestra ill at ease.
At the end of a working year,
When the wind with trumpets comes
And a stirring roll of drums,
Then the call is sweet and clear,
The trees in their mimic way
The sea’s great symphony play.
Then the restless tempest roves
Till each tall instrument
Its flowing voice has blent,
And ocean roars in the groves.
Then One below, amid leaves —
Dead leaves that glimmer and rush,
Like waters that flow and gush,
For the distant Master grieves.
Oh, Forest and Sea apart,
And the courier Wind between
To bring to the list’ning green
The song of the ocean’s heart!
Straight from the smoking crest
To the trees in splendor drest
The message of strength is blown.
Over the dunes and hills
The hollows of clouds it fills,
Till the splendid key is known.
Then you hear the immense duet
Of the Forest and distant Sea,
Which they joyously sing to me.
And I listen to it, you bet!
N.S.W.
THE RELIGIOUS EDITOR.

FINNEY’S
The Big Block, BRISBANE.
IN BANANA LAND
No district in Queensland is more closely
affected by the war than the Logan. This
locality is thickly peopled by Germans, many
of whom are intermarried with British folk.
Veterans of Weissembourg and Worth
(1870) are common among the Logan
Deutschers.
THE FINAL APPEARANCE.
LOW COMBDY : "Much of a crowd at old Marmadke's funeral?”
TRAGEDY: “Remarkable, my boy, remarkable! I assure you we were positively turning
them away."
Recent press announcement re probate—
“ Dame Margaret Millar Bell, widow of
Joshua Peter Bell, K.C.M.G.: Realty £3l,
personalty £240.” All that was left of the
one-time greatness of Jimbour.
“Machete”: Commander J. A. H. Beres-
ford, R.A.N., who had the honor of lead-
ing the first R.A.N. force into action at
New Britain (otherwise New Pomerania)
the other day, hails from Queensland, where
he put in a considerable time in the naval
forces. He rose from warrant rank to be
holder of a commission, and finally passed
into the Australian navy. His promotion
has been rapid of recent years and well
deserved.
Major “Tommy” Dods, D. 5.0., A.A.G.,
has been promoted to A.G.', with the rank
of Lieutenant-Colonel and a seat on the
Military Board. “Tommy” first began his
military experience with the Queensland
Teachers’ Corps, a volunteer regiment re-
cruited from public-school pupil-spankers.
He went to South Africa as adjutant of the
sth Queensland Bushmen, and the D.S.O.
was handed to him. On his return he joined
the General Staff, and since then he has
never looked back except to see if the force
behind him was keeping reasonably sober.
Lieutenant Rowland Bowen, R.A.N.,
wounded in the capture of the German wire-
less station at Rabaul, is a well-known
Brisbaneite who was for some time in the
Naval Reserve. He was one of the first
reservists to pass into the Australian navy.
In Brisbane he made a hobby of flying,
and constructed a biplane “glider” with
which he used to experiment from the im-
mense, sloping sandhills of Moreton Island.
Though he made several attempts to hurt
himself the soft surface proved an obstacle
every time. Apparently the native police
of German. Papua have been less consid-
erate.

Yarra P.O.

the staff 10ft. deep.
Hans Blumenschein.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’d like to be a Deutscher stout and be a
Briton goodt;
I’d visk to fight for dis landt undt for dat
landt if I could ;
Mein Frau, she iss a Sydney girl, und-lofes
der Union Jack,
Und says she hopes der Kaiser’s crowd is
beaten blue and black;
But I vas born in Preussen und I lofe der
Vaterland,
Und vhen I hear “Die Wacht am Rhein”
mein bosom vill exshpand.
And vhen dey run der Shermans down, dese
patriotic crew,
Bei Gott —mein Blut it gets so hot—l don’t
know vot to do.
You see, I’m goodt Australian, und I’ve
got my leedle plot;
Mein Kinder are Australian too, und
Deutschland is forgot;
I)ey read dose daily gables of der Deut-
schers’ grueltie,
Und vhen 1 say it’s mostly lies dey look
so shly at me.
Und Fritz (he iss der eldest) goes a-marcli-
ing mit his gun,
Uhd shows his leedle Briider how he make
der Prussians run,
Und in der school dey sing, a song of Red
und Vhite und Blue,
But “Deutschland fiber -Alles” iss a Lied
dey never knew.
You see I lofe dis gountry mit its Freiheit
und its clear
Blue Himmel—dough you cannot touch old
. Deutschland’s Lager Bier.
Mein Vater left his Vaterland mit bitter
Schmerz behind,
But leedle shparks of Deutschland keep up-
springing in der mind,
Und, dough, der. Kaiser’s half verriicht for
Glory und for Blut,
I feel mit pride in deepest Herz der deutsche
stuff is goodt;
Und if I don’t rejoice myself at all der
Deutschers do,
I cannot shout mit Britishers when Sherman
luck is blue.
I vould not like for tousand pound to have
der Kaiser here- —
(I know der vorth of Freiheit und I holdt
her blessings dear)
But efery morn I feel a tug-of-var vithin
my breast —
Der Frau und Kinder battling mit der
Vaterland for best.
Dey do not mean to vound old Dad —but
vhen der crazy town
Is filled mit joy to hear der void: “Der
Sherman’s going down”
I hear mein Jack und Greta raise a vild
“Hip-Hip-Hurroo!”
Und say to me, “Hans. Blumenschein, vot-
ever villst thou do?”
Mein Fluch upon der crazy head vot
plunged us in dis row
Und soaked a landt mit honest blut und
vidowed many a Frau.
Old Bismarck’s “Blut und Eisen” is a Geist
you cannot lay,
It haunts der Kaiser’s palace und it drives
his peace avay ;
Und hier in far Australia der simple Mann
must groan
To dink votever side goes down der sorrow
is his own;
If England lose—mein Frau is sad, mein
Kinder sorrow too;
If Deutschland fall old Hans must veep dot
Deutschland’s luck is through.
I gannot read dose bapers now-—such
schrecklich tales dey tell
Of lofely towns in Belgian landt dot
Deutschers made a hell;
Of hundert tousand Prussians who haf gone
to Kingdom Come
And all about a cause vhereon der soldier’s
voice is dumb.
I bet mein life if var' vas made by common
Deutsch und French
No Tropfen Blut vas on der grass, no rifle
under , trench,
But Ach! der cursed ding is hier dot von
und all must rue,
Und I can only cry: “Vot can Australian
Deutschers do?”
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, September 12, 1914.
My Dear Thargomindaii,—
Dulness has brooded over us this week,
from a social point of view, like the shadow
of a forgotten lOU.
The races to-day were not largely at-
tended, and most of the frocks wore a
familiar aspect. Jean RosstM unro possessed
one of the smartest decorated figures on the
lawn, and Mrs. Stanley Chapman, Mrs.
Stuart Hyslop and Mrs. Bob McNab also
ran into a place.
The Arts and Crafts’ Society opened its
second annual show this week. A lot of
excellent work is shown, noticeably Mr.
Moroney’s pottery and Mr. Ronaldsen’s
quaint china reptiles and birds. These last
are marvellously lifelike, especially some
frilled lizards, and an iguana or two. Miss
Overman, a very old lady, has put in some
creditable wood-carving, and Mrs. Cortis
Stanford has done exquisite embroideries.
Little Agatha Fitzgerald exhibits some
illustrations for a' fairy tale —original and
artistic. The drawing is the weak element.
Two weddings are looming nearer to the
fatal hour. On the 16th of this month
popular Brenda Pike and “Jim” Thorpe (he
plays lacrosse for a living or lives to play
lacrosse —I forget which) will agree to
chance it. The other gamble particularly
concerns little Miss Jo Frost and "Mr. Chas.
Hall. The Frost pere and mere have sent
out invitations to an At Home in daughter’s
honor for September 28.
Hospitable Mrs. “Billy” Ahles is another
lady who will spread the festive food-stool
next week. Pier party, I believe, is some-
thing in the nature of a house-warming or
a fortress christening, she having evacuated
one suburb and taken up a strong position in
another.
The first news of fighting in German
Papua has just arrived. Lieutenant Bowen,
who was married about a fortnight ago
to a pretty little Brisbane girl, Grace Bell,
is among the wounded. Commander
Beresford, who lived here for many years
and whose wife is well known in Brisbane,
led the attack.
The United Service Institute on Wednes-
day night farewelled Lieutenant-Colonel
Sutton, Major Plughes and one or two others.
Last night (Friday) the Johnsonian Club
also got into action, and good-byed Lieu-/
tenant-Colonel. Sutton, Captain Marks and/
Captain Conrick (three popular medicoes,
who are a fraction of the Army Medical
Corps), and Lieutenant Urquhart, of the
line. Two of the rank and file, one a son
of Dr. Shirley and the other the son of
G. H. M. Addison, the clever architect, were
so-longed at the same time.
The Methodists got_ into line one night
this week, too, and adieued their adherents
who have loosed the war-yell and gone to
shake hands with the Bear at Berlin.
A local firm contributed £lOO to the
Patriotic Fund. Then its debt collector
spent a busy few days at the concentration
camp, making things unpleasant for budding
soldiers who still owed a few shillings on
recent suits and hats.
From the Liberal point of view, one of
the humors of last Saturday (polling day,
if you remember) was that an energetic
Liberal dame unconsciously commandeered
a Labor car, and plied briskly among the
voters of her own side for some two hours
before she was discovered.
Yours with the glowering- eye of the down-
trodden,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
“Billy Blue” : Brigadier-General VY illiam
Throsby Bridges, to whom the command of
the Australian Tommies going to the war
has been given, is a son of the late Captain
William Somerset Bridges, R.N.R., who, on
his retirement, filled for some years the
position of C.P.S. at Moss Vale (N.S.W.).
The late captain was married to a daughter
of the equally late Charles Throsby, of
Throsby Park, Moss Vale. She is also
“late,” but she survived her husband and
died within the last year. The Throsbys
are amongst Australia’s oldest families.
The first of them came out as a surgeon on
the transport Coromandel in charge of 138
convicts in June,' 1802, and settled in the
State. He was appointed superintendent
of the Newcastle district when it was a
convict settlement. He afterwards did
some good work exploring, and discovered
the Bong Bong country. Of this lie was
granted a good slice, and worked it into a
high-class estate. The Brigadier-General,
though he was born in Scotland in 18(51,
has had his military training in Australia.
Ilf obtained his first commission in the
N.S.W. Artillery in 1885.
“Nemo,” concerning an old friend :
Archibald Currie, who died a few days ago at
Melbourne, aged 84, had some uncommon experiences.
His schoolmaster in Ayrshire was one James Service,
who, years after, became Premier of Victoria; and
when, after the bank smash, the Commercial Bank
was “reconstructed,” Currie was a director and his
old dominie, - Service, was chairman. Currie went to
sea at 14, in the American and China trades, and
came to Australia in 1852 as third officer of the
barque Sir William Molesworth. It ran on a sand-
bank at King George’s Sound, and remained fast.
Currie and the second officer, Taylor, were nearly
drowned. When Archibald got to Melbourne he and
three of his shipmates went to the Bendigo gold
diggings. But they got no wealth. They tried
Mclvor, now called Heathcote, and had no luck there.
Then Currie went back to the briny. He entered the
Australian-Maoriland trade, and became a skipper at
22. His “trade master” with the Maoris was
William Thompson, alias Wiremu liingi, the “King
Maker.” Then Currie struck into the China trade,
and was the first to bring a British ship into the
port of Bangkok, Siam. That was in 1859. He
took a shipload of miners to Port Curtis, but the
diggings bedame an historic failure, and, further-
more, his ship struck on a reef. While at Port
Curtis Currie nearly lost his life trying to save people
from the wreck of a ship called the Tamandra. Next
he went into the San Francisco trade, and saw some
of the North and South war in America. After this,
he bought four ships and started business in Mel-
bourne as a merchant. It was he who discovered
and surveyed the harbor at King Island, now called
Currie Harbor.
The Reflections of “10-2-4”
A notable feature of the war is the resolute way
in which many of the gallant fellows who have been
covering themselves with feathers and gold braid, dur-
ing the past 10 or 15 years, are remaining at home.
The Expeditionary Force has very few of them as
officers, the latter having been mostly recruited from
the ranks of those who don’t mind a genuine fight,
but have no use for blither or display when there
seems no prospect of war. Exactly the same thing
was noticeable when the Soudan Contingent went
away 30 years ago. In those days the attractive and
ornate uniform of the N.S.W. Lancers was in great
demand for service at Government House, and for
social work generally. It was one of the jests of the
period that hardly any of the best-known wearers
of the famous uniform left for Egypt. The men that
did were of a very different type. Bevan Lewis, whose
book on “Normal and Morbid Psychology ” is a
modern classic, gives the key to the situation. He
reduces the character of every individual human being
into so many factors, and allots a certain number of
marks to each. “Stability,” “caution,” “reckless-
ness,” “resolution,” “love of notoriety”—each man
has more or less of these, and a hundred other
qualities, in his composition. The class of citizen
who has 80 per cent, “love of notoriety” in his
make-up joins a regiment with a taking uniform, and
wears the latter on every possible occasion, until the
outbreak of war. If in addition to 80 per cent, “love
of notoriety” his character shows 75 per cent, of
“caution” —or, in other words, plain funk —he then
proceeds to urge somebody else to face the risk and
discomfort of active service. A soldier in whom love
of display is balanced by courage and what Lewis
calls “stability” is a valuable asset to his, country.
When he adds to these qualities imagination and
high intellectual gifts he becomes almost priceless.
The rarest thing in life, and one of the most admir-
able, is a character which is all stability and no
vanity. Etienne Dumont, whose Letters hjtve just
been published, would seem to have had 100 per cent,
stability and not so much as a fraction of the
notoriety craze. He stands revealed in those docu-
ments as the trusted adviser of the younger Pitt,
Wilberforce, Bentham, Greville and a dozen other
great men. All these historical characters sought the
counsel of the unobtrusive Frenchman in his English
home, but he was the last to want to talk about it.
Kitchener would score highly for stability, and, of
course, his courage and resolution are undoubted. HiS
“love of display” factor would work out at about
five points at the outside. One of the most pleasing
traits of the best type of British soldier is the resolute
way in which the vanity element in him is suppressed.
Of course, it is there in many cases, but it is never
obtruded. All the world over, as a matter of fact —
whether in Paris or Pekin or Manly Beach —one of
the most reliable measures of a man’s status and
education is his freedom from braggadocio and
blatant vanity generally. A great gift is stability.
Life would be pleasanter in our great city bars if
there were more of it about.
“Old Colonist” writes: —-
Permit me to reply to “0.K.” (B. 3/9/’l4), who
lets loose on an innocent German national song,
“Deutschland Über Alles.” He interprets them as mean-
ing: “Germany wants to dominate the earth, sea and
sky,” whereas they are merely an expression of love of
Fatherland. Adding the eliminated word “beloved,”
and reading, “Germany beloved above all” (by its
people) the meaning becomes clear. There is not an
aggressive word in the whole song', and no more
arrogance than is contained in the anthem of any
other nation. As to “O.K.’s” outburst on a language
which is “almost a bestial offence to every foreign
ear;” all I can say is that the language has inspired
some of the greatest geniuses of all times, whose
works have been translated into every European
tongue, including English. Every language has its
faults and its beauties, which, however, are only
revealed to the full by years of study. English, for
instance, rejoices in the distinction of being the most
barbarously spelled tongue in Christendom. Spelling
was given to the Englishman to conceal the pronuncia-
tion. You can find English words which don’t con-
tain one sound mentioned in the English alphabet.

“Trousers for Ever”: I have just dis-
covered a new humorous paper, The Scot,
at Hame and Abroad. Part of it is
written in broken English by a kilted jour-
nalist who signs himself “Lauchie Lang
Lugs.” Lauchie is evidently the “Answers
to Correspondents” demon of The Scot ;
but he-is a kindly functionary with a weak-
ness for breaking into verse. Hear him: —
D. M. M. Milburn (Maoriland) writes: “The Scot
comes tae haun’ a’ richt, an’, man, I enjoy it fine.”
Then D.M. breks intae rhyme thuslv: —-
I read each one from end to end,
And then to friends I did them lend,
An’ a’e Scots lassie said to me: '
“Tae us Scots folk hoo guid they be.”
That’s "no bad for a start, D.M., but Lauchie wad
like tae add a .line: —
Aye! aye! D.M., ye may depend
Ye’ll ayg hae frien’s while ye can lend;
But, ginsy’er frien’s enjoy The Scot
When they hae a free readin’ o’t,
Their pleasure wad be mair enhanced
Gin tliroe-an’-saxpence they advanced ;
Then we. an’ they wad shairly gain,
Gin ev’ry reader bocht his ain.
Pinna be blate f
Note the strange aboriginal curse with
which he begins this: —
My fogs'! -the Northcote Scots ken hoo tae hau’d on
tae a guid thing. They ha’e re-electit Blither Angus
for president. Aye! an’ they presentit him wi’ a
framed group o’ the officers an’ commytee, an’ whit
dae ye think? They hae mair nor seeventy pounds in
the bank—something tae crow aboot efter wan year
of existence. Aye! they’re a gran’ lot o’ lads oot
there, bit the lasses? Aw! hau’d yer tongue! They’re
.fist perfection. Oh ! Lauchie’s a guid judge, an’ ye
can talc’ his word for it.
Never again will I scoff when the musical
comedy triflers, garbed as Parisians, jabber
amongst themselves in pidgin English.
(from London) : Should Metho-
dists be blackleg journalists? The question
has just been debated by the English
Wesleyan Methodist Conference at Leeds.
A formal complaint had been submitted
by the National Union of Journalists that
a number of Methodist ’clergymen were
habitually doing reporting and other jour-
nalistic work for daily papers at reduced
rates. The matter was referred to a com-
mittee, which, after investigation, came to
the conclusion “that, while the Conference
had _po power to prevent ministers from
contributing to the press, it was undesir-
able, in| view of serious issues involved, that
they should personally compete with pro-
fessional journalists.” The committee added
the opinion that the remedy lay in the
hands of editors of newspapers. No doubt
it does, but this abuse is generally the result
of a working alliance between blackleg
editors and blackleg preachers. The con-
ference adopted the report of the committee,
but the pious expression of opinion that it
is advisable to cease taking the bread out of
.the mouths of working journalists will pro-
bably be ignored or conveniently forgotten
by all the reverend non-unionists concerned
in this most unchristian and uncharitable
competition. But cheap-labor journalism is
by no means the only secular arena in which
the Methodist minister “supplements his
income.” Rev. Dr. Workman, President
•of the Westminster Training College, who
took part in the debate, is a man likely to
know things, and he savs his brother parsons
also make a bit by organising excur-
sions and holiday tours, acting as commis-
sion agents and undertaking various other
assorted’ activities. _He appealed to the
conference to “strike decisively at all
secular occupations which interfered with
the proper work of the minister.” The
conference evidently thought that would he
a pretty big contract, and gave it the go-by.
No wonder that for eight years in succes-
sion the English Methodists have been de-
clining in numbers, and that the sect has
been described in one of the London Metho-
dist weeklies as “on the road to ruin.”
* * * *
London Daily News, being the property
of the cocoa-millionaire Quaker Cadbury,
a leading opponent e.f militarism and
scrap of every description, had an awk-
ward proposition to tackle in the cham-
pionship fight between Carpentier and
Gunboat Smith, at Olvmpia. The diffi-
culty was overcome by displaying, on page
1, a vigorous and whole-hearted denunciation
of the affair by Editor Gardner, as the “ugli-
est scene I have witnessed in my life, a foul
scene, a hateful scene, a scene'that I shall
never forget, and that I shall never remember
without a sense of shame.” And then, at the
end of the denunciation of the brute 1 and
degrading spectacle, came the announcement
in parentheses : “Full details of the fight will
be > found on page S'.” Thus the Quaker nro-
prietor and his co-religionists were propiti-
ated on one page, while the ungodly herd of
bruiser worshippers got all they wanted over
the leaf.

“8.J.H.0.”: The Jewish Evening News
Iwas born in London just before the war. The
author of its being was a Roumanian immi-
grant named Morris Myers, who a few
months ago started the Jewish Times. The
Fog has also the Jewish Journal, which
has been trying to interest and amuse Sliem,
Ham and Japheth for eight or nine years,
and tlie Daily Jewish Express, which be-
gan talking through its nose about 15 years
ago. All these papers are set by linotype
and printed, in Yiddish. A couple of them
make a good show of ads., and the Journal
claims, a daily circulation of 12.000. All
have their own standard of news values. The
Ulster business, for instance, was given
about the same space that the S. M. Herald
would devote to a moderately big strike at
Broken Hill; but news, say. of a pogrom in
Russia is leaded and rushed in with double
headlines.
* * * *
4 Tto the present boom in ring-fighting, the
gossipy journalist. G. R. Sims, recalls a
yarn of the interest taken in stoush a cen-
tury back. The story is that Thurtell. the
“gentlemanly” murderer of Weare in 1823,
having taken the sacrament, and having a
few minutes to wait for the scaffold, got the
obliging governor of Hertford Gaol to go out
and ascertain whether Tom Spring had
beaten Langan at Worcester the previous
day. He had. and Thurtell, on hearing it,
proceeded to die happily.

The Double-bed Dialogues.
HOW TO ACQUIRE A DOUBLE-BED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
TRANGE how one gets used to things
—when one is married! Even when
one is married to Babette!
That night Babette did not hear my
knock on her bedroom door, and I entered
her room to discover my wife at that inter-
esting, if somewhat draughty, stage of a
woman’s disrobing when she is chiefly corset.
Unwittingly I viewed a panorama of Bab-
ette’s back. Now, the landscape of Babette’s
back-view is a subject for sonnets, an excuse
for lyrical rhapsodies. By a complicated
of mirrors Babette even man-
ages to admire it herself. But to me, her
husband, that night it did not contribute
.even a thrill. All I noted was that my wife,
;since her marriage, had begun to put on
flesh. The map had been drawn on a larger
. scale.
Of course I admired Babette’s. figure.
Even an archbishop would admire it. At
any rate Babette did, in addition to three
.dentists, two doctors and nine shop-walkers.
It is a harassing experience for a woman to
have so beautiful a figure as Babette.
That was why Babette was always chang-
ing her dentists and doctors. They would
: admire lier figure., And she could never
.shop in the same department of a shop
.twice. Babette was quite frank about it
.to me. She always repeated exactly what
the dentist or the curate had said —or,
rather, what he hadn’t said, but what he
had looked. Sometimes I have thought that
perhaps Babette was the teeniest bit to
'.blame. Unconsciously, I gently put it to
[her, she might have given them some en-
. couragement. But she indignantly replied
.that she couldn’t alter her figure; no en-
> couragement would suppress the least of her
.curves. No; she had just to suffer the adora-
tion she invariably inspired. She bore up
vunder it heroically, uncomplainingly. But
ithe stopping of one tooth of hers brought me
iin three dentists’ bills. “You see,” she
’.pathetically explained, “when you’re lying
iback in a dentist’s chair, you’re so help-
less, aren’t you?”
That explained to me why dentistry is
-such a popular profession. I have often
wished since that I had been a dentist. Or
a shop-walker. And the number of curates
who called to save Babette’s soul —of course,
sshe hadn’t a soul —proved to me that there
was a great deal of earnestness hidden
within the collar that buttons at the back.
Several curates, not finding Babette’s soul on
the surface, had tried to reach it by squeez-
ing Babette’s hand, or looking for it in
the depths of Babette’s soulful eyes. Ba-
bette found that it was almost as harassing
not to have a soul as to have a luscious
figure.
But this night I found myself admiring
Babette’s back in a purely artistic sense. I
didn’t rush to her and rapturously kiss her
corset laces. It was a very cold night, so
I merely slipped into bed. That is to say,
I merely wound the clock, removed the pil-
low-shams, placed Babette’s new hat under
the bed, gathered up her scattered hairpins,
turned back the coverlet without creasing
it, arranged the blinds, placed the matches
handy, tidied up the floor, shook her pillow
up, and slipped into bed.
Babette was certainly plumper. Not
fatter—that word is never mentioned in
married life. When I first met Babette
she was of that slight, ethereal, willowy,
.bendable, convenient build that allows you
vto clasp most of her with one arm, leaving
the other hand free to adjust her chin. Now
:'it required two arms —-not that it mattered
?at all, since now there was no need for ad-
justing her chin. It adjusted itself auto-
matically. Marriage had hurled Babette,
as it hurls most wothen, from one horn of
woman’s dilemma to the other. Every
slight woman wants to be fat: every plump
woman wails to be scraggy. .None, of them
\will ever admit that' she is just right.
When I fervently told Babette that I
Hiked her at her married weight—instead of
ibeing in the featherweight class she could
'qualify as a middleweight —she pathetically
(sighed. The more of her there, was the
vmore of her I had to love. Cubically she
was 18. per cent, more lovable. She said
that she hated statistics. ,
So now, observing Babette’s geographical
extent, I thrilled merely, aesthetically. I
contemplated contours with a mere town-
planner’s enthusiasm. And Babette did not
seem to mind. She simply didn’t care. She
did not even miss the usual kiss. Strange .
“Do you know, Babette,” I said, when she
had joined me in bed and snuggled herself
comfortable, “I was just thinking how
strange it is that I’ve grown used to you.
“Well, if marriage won’t make us used to
. each other, wliat’s it ' for anyway ?” she
laughed.
“Yes, but before we were married you
•were utterly mysterious to me, wonderful
rand strange —all women were. And you
were so different from all other women.”
“And now,” she almost sighed, “I’m just
like all the others.” 4
“No. But through you I seem to. have
discovered all the re?i of you. A wife is
like a dictionary to an unknown language—
the language of • womenv And before our
marriage all of you Was so hard to under-
stand...-and -you were untranslatable.”
“And now you know me off by heart?
“Yes, that’s the finest thing about it. It’s
like learning a new language—a great, won-
derful language.”
But Babette, thinking in her own lan-
guage, dropped from abstractions to our-
selves. “Those first few months!” she
sighed. “Why can’t we: have them all over
again? You were so wonderful, so differ-
ent. I used to be actually shy before you.
I used to flush when I. even thought of you.
I used even to be afraid “of you !”
“And now?”
Babette laughed—the confident laugh of
achievement. “You’re fonly a•. child, after
all —a little, clumsy child*. tb#t has got to be
humored and petted arid put up with. . And
once you were a hero! Dear old thing!”
And she slipped a happy kiss on to my
cheek. It was almost a contemptuous kiss —
most married kisses are.
“But those first few months!” she went
on. “How excited I was that evening we
were introduced! Fancy me getting ex-
cited over you now!”
“What I think, when I look back,” I said,
“is the sheer luck of meeting you at all. I
‘evenings.’ It was the merest chance
went that night. I didn’t know Kitty
very'%edl. I didn’t like her much. And yet
she me to her evening. Why? And
it happened that you wbye there that night
—though you weren’t often there. And then
we were introduced; Kitty introduced us just
as she introduced any ordinary persons.”
“Oh, no,”. said Babette, from the depths
of her woman’s knowledge. “No girl ever
introduces a man to a girl without a hope
that something will come of it.”
“But our meeting, casually like that, w r as
wonderful, wasn’t it?”
“It was wonderful,” Babette agreed. But
not so wonderful in the way I meant. “You
and I were bound to meet somehow, some-
where.”
“That’s true. Nothing could have kept us
apart. But it wasn’t mere chance. There
must be a purpose in the world, after all.”
And yet, as I uttered this platitude, I
felt mean. For, looking back on my court-
ship of Babette, I saw, in all its hideousness,
the long procession of meannesses and deceits
that I had practised upon innocent Babette.
The course of true love never runs smooth ;
but ours had —because I had dug, unsuspected
by her, my own channel for that stream. I
had deliberately imposed upon Babette’s
maiden ignorance. I had taken unfair ad-
vantage of her beautiful trustfulness. And
the fact that I loved her was no excuse; it
merely made my perfidy the more horrible.
I could love her and yet mislead her. I
could make her my wife under false pre-
tences.
That first evening, when I had met her at
Kitty’s “evening,” my long course of devi-
ousness began. When by the merest chance
we found ourselves temporarily isolated in

that cosy corner of the verandah —and how
beautiful my Babette bad looked against
the cushions that miraculously matched her
dress!—I had lied to her. I had told her
I hated music, and though I knew she was
yearning to go back with me to the singing
in the drawing-room, she had politely stayed
out there with me. I don’t know what
Kitty said to her afterwards : it was Ivitty s
“evening,” and Babette was her show guest,
and the dear angel allowed me to monopolise
her in that selfish way, though I could see
she was thinking of that other chap, John.
And, as I soon found out, John was in love
with her all the time.
Later on, too, though Babette tried not
to let me see, I discovered that she was see-
ing quite a lot of John. Decent fellow,
too; but he did not understand Babette as
I did. Women Why, the ordin-
ary man can see right through a woman, if
he only, looks into her eyes. Poor, innocent
child, she . was deeply troubled about John.
He loved her as much as I did ; but he did
not understand her crystal innocence as I
did. ‘ And" she was wavering, yes, she had
confessed it before we were married ; and if
I had not proposed at the very time I did I
believe I would have lost her. That is
where the man who understands woman
beats the fellow'who doesn’t mix his adora-
tion with masculine guile. Poor Babette,
I reflected as I felt her soft arm beneath
my neck, I had won' her, and she was
happy ; but one doesn’t set into matrimony,
any more than one gets into politics, with
clean hands. Well. I would make it up to
her. The end had justified the means.
Yes, my love for her had been rather
mixed. Like a cocktail, all sorts of things
were in it, giving it a flavor, without which
Babette would never have drunk the main
ingredient of love. That night when, all
unsuspecting, she had allowed me to lead
her out into the garden at that dance, and,
against her better instincts, .she had been
persuaded to sit out that dance in that sum-
mer-house. I could see that she had never
been there before —it was such a lonely and
unlit summer-house that I would never have
discovered it if a partner earlier in the even-
ing had not taken me there. Of course, I
knew why that other partner had taken me
there —she’s married now —and I had kissed
her according to the conventions of sitting-
out in lonely summer-houses ; but that girl
would have been surprised if she had known
that in the act of kissing her —in her strug-
gles, of course, she slid on to my knee —-I
was merely thinking what a lovely place it
was for me to kiss Babette in. It was a
kind of dress rehearsal —and I needed one,
because I hadn’t then kissed Babette at all.
That girl had been well rehearsed.
And then I led the unsuspecting Babette
to the slaughter-house. Poor child, she must
have had a vague suspicion of me, for at
first she refused to come there, but I over-
persuaded her; and she entered its dark-
ness supremely trusting in my honor. And
then I kissed her —not in the least as suc-
cessfully as I had kissed that other girl.
But then Babette was innocent: that other
girl was different. Babette struggled: the
other girl had struggled, too ; but it was
easy to see that that girl’s struggle was only
pretence. It was her method of getting on
to my knee —so that I could kiss her more
comfortably. Yet my rehearsal was of use,
even in Babette’s case, for, when she strug-
gled I managed to draw her on to my knee,
and kissed her more comfortably. I daresay
that to this day she had no suspicion how
she came to be sitting in that position with
her lips so easily accessible. Yet, lying
there in bed, conscious of Babettfe’s delici-
ous nearness, I felt mean. It was by suoh
guile that I had got her to share with me
that double-bed.
Even my proposal I had arranged with a
diabolical cunning. I had picked out the
very spot a week before, and I had arranged
that she and I would be there on a certain
evening. I will admit that chance favored
me. If Babette’s shoe lace hadn’t hap-
pened to come unloosed just as we were pass-
ing that shady spot it would have been some-
what difficult to suggest that we should sit
down beneath that tree without raising her
suspicions. But I would have made her
stop, anyway. And then I deliberately
kissed her; and, as I had foreseen, she was
shocked —for she had told me I must never,
never again, etc., and I had faithfully sworn
that I never would. That was the one con-
dition she had insisted upon when I asked
her to come to that concert. (We never got
to that concert.)
Then I humbly apologised. But, instead
of Babette forgiving me, as she regularly
used to —really, though she did not analyse
her sensations, she liked me to kiss her, but
wouldn’t admit the shameful fact to herself,
and, naturally, I never let her see that I
knew —this night she actually got angry and
said she’d go straight home if I couldn’t
behave as I had faithfully promised. But I
grovelled before her, and at last she forgave
me and even kissed me in token of her for-
giveness—a light touch of a butterfly’s wing
—not a real kiss at all. And we sat down
on a bank, and I pointed out to her in a
dispassionate way that the grass might be
wet —though, goodness knows (though I
didn’t, then) that she had more thicknesses
of clothing on just there than I had. So I
got her quite unsuspiciously to sit on my
knee, and then I worked up another quarrel,
I forget about what. I wanted the delici-
ous happiness of making it up again. When
a woman is magnanimously forgiving you
she is so much more easy to manage. This
evening she felt so magnanimous that her
sisterly kiss tremulously melted into the
other kind, and before I had really thought
out what I was going to say I had said it.
The moment I had done it I knew I had
made a mistake. I shouldn’t have rushed
things like that. I should have gently led
up to the subject, instead of blurting out
my proposal and shocking Babette. She
was awfully surprised. Of course, with my
knowledge of woman, I should have expected
that; but I was in love with Babette, I
knew she liked me, and I suppose I forgot
how sudden my declaration must have
seemed to her innocent soul. I had made
no allowances for her appalling ignorance of
her own heart. I had gathered that, in a
shy, frightened way, she might have looked
mistily into the future and vaguely consid-
ered the possibility that some time I might
like her enough to marry her; but, being a
woman, she was content just to go on being
friends, happy in the present. I overlooked
that in my state of mind, and risked more
than I knew in proposing so soon. Three
months later she mieht have been more pre-
pared ; but I couldn’t have wasted three
months. Luckily, it turned out all right. It
is true that she did not give me my answer
that night. She was too much affected by
the shock to make any definite promise. But
she admitted that she liked me, oh, very
much.... but, as a husband that, of
course was a bigger thing. We compro-
mised by the arrangement that I was to con-
sider myself engaged to her, and she was to
give the matter of her engagement to me
very earnest consideration. And I loved
her' the more for taking it so seriously.
My thoughts had kept me silent. I looked
to see whether Babette had fallen asleep.
But she was wide awake, evidently think-
ing, too.
“No,” I said, taking up the discussion
where I had left it, “it wasn’t chance that
brought us together. There must be a pur-
pose in the world.”
“I’ve beep thinking, too,” my wife gravely
said. “There was a purpose that brought
us together.” She hesitated a moment.
Then I felt her body stiffen itself, as if she
had taken a great resolution. “There was
a purpose— my purpose.”
“Your purpose?” I asked in surprise.
, “Yes, I had made up my mind to meet
you.” • __ ~

“I had heard about you.”

“So it was all a put-up job?” I blankly
said.
“That sort of thing always is.

“For me? I like that!”
“No, for herself. But she thought it
would be a pity to waste you—you were just
the sort that would be snapped up by a
silly, pretty face. And, though I wasn t

adored deep blue eyes and large
hands, so she thought it would be worth

And I get so hot at tennis, and the powder
comes off. So I stuck out for evening

And I was feeling very lonely. I’d just re-

I’d got so to depend on him taking me about

paying my tram-fares that I felt quite

I can’t carry on conversation with a crowd
