thing was that you let me see you liked me.”
“Do you know, Babette,” I wondered. “I
can’t remember what we talked about that
first wonderful evening. And we must have
talked quite a lot.”
“Oh, just conversation. Silly questions
and answers. We had to do something
to keep our eyes from blurting out the truth.
All the time I was wondering how it would
feel when you kissed me.”
“But I didn’t kiss you that evening.”
“Oh, yes, you did. I invented that kiss
while we were chatting.”
I was surprised. I had invented that kiss,
too, while we were chatting. I told Babette,
and she sighed happily.
“If I’d only known, then,” she added, “it
would have saved me so much worry after-
wards. There were times when I even
thought that you didn’t love me.”
“You couldn’t have!” I said.
“Oh, I knew you loved me, but what I
wasn’t quite sure of was whether you loved
me enough—enough to marry me.”
“I see. But, all the same, it was luck
that we were left alone in the corner of that
verandah while the others were singing,
wasn’t .it?”
“Not luck,” smiled Babette; “good man-
agement. Kitty is a splendid manager. Of
course, I had to pay her. She whispered to
me as I was leaving that she was going to
be my chief bridesmaid.”
“And she was.”
“Yes; but it wasn’t all plain sailing for
Kitty or me. Often we miserably wondered
whether there was going to be a wedding at
all. You were so slow, weren’t you?”
“Was I?” I asked in surprise.
“Jim Hastings beat you by three weeks,
and even John came up to scratch ten days
before you—and regularly every fortnight
after that till I switched him off.”
“But when I did speak, you said” —
“ 'This is so sudden !’ ” Babette laughed.
“No.” I protested.
“Well, something that meant tdie same
thing. All girls do. What I really said to
myself was, ‘Thank goodness, that’s over.’
All girls do. We have to pretend. All love-
making is pretence, isn’t it? That’s the fun
of it. Suppose I had known from the
that you meant to marry me, and suppose you
had known from the first that I meant to
marry you, where would the excitement have
come in?”
“I see,” I slowly said. “It would' have
been just as dull as being married.”
“Exactly.” Babette smilingly agreed.
“Love is only a game, and has to be played
by the rules. The lucky thing is that every
girl knows the rules off by heart. Do you
remember that night we sat out a dance,, and
I ruined a new pair of satin slippers walking
with you in the wet grass? I didn’t mind
that. I knew that you’d pay me back when
we were married. A girl has to make sacri-
fices, hasn’t she? Well, when you sug-
gested that -we might as well sit the dance
out in the garden, I knew you wanted to
kiss me—but I didn’t let you see I wanted
you to kiss me, If I had you wouldn’t have.
So I wouldn’t go to that shady summer-house.
I’d been there the dance before. I’d man-
oeuvred my partner to take me there, just to
see if it was a safe place for you to kiss me
iu and the poor man mistook himself for
you. Perhaps I was to blame —a little. It
was a kind of dress rehearsal, but. it was a
bit too realistic for me. I had to powder up
again before I came to you.”
“But you did go there,” I reminded her.
“Of course, you stupid. After having the
dress rehearsal. But didn’t my reluctance
to go make you all the more eager to go?
And, goodness me, when you did kiss me,
how shocked I was !”
“I felt a brute the minute I had done it,”
I admitted. “I wanted to grovel to you.”
“I was shocked, really. Shocked at the
bungle you had made of that kiss. But I
daren’t have helped you. I had to dodge.
Silly, wasn’t it? And I had to pretend to
be tremendously angry with you. But at
last I pardoned you, and just to show you
that I trusted you. I made you sit down by
me. And then, of course, you tried again.
And I struggled.”
“Pretence!” I said bitterly. “But how I
loved to feel you struggling in my arms!”
“And how I loved you holding me!” Bab-
ette snuggled nearer. “That’s why I strug-
gled so determinedly. And then, somehow—-
to this day I can’t tell how I managed it so
well—in my efforts to escape I found myself
on your knee. And neither of us admitted
even to ourselves that I was sitting on your
knee.”
“Pretence!” I sighed.
“Glorious pretence !” Babette cried. “And
the more I struggled the more comfy I was
making myself on your knees. And, quite by
accident, my lips found yours—you were so
clumsy I had to help—and I quite forgot to
take them away.’’
“And nobody mentioned it,” I mournfully
smiled.
“That wasn’t exactly the right time for
conversation, was it? That sort of silence
is golden.”
“And then, at last, I asked you to kiss
me back,” I reminded her.
“And I sternly refused, didn’t I ? As if
there was any beginning or end of that sort
of kiss, or as if I wasn’t doing iny full share
all the time. A kiss isn’t a kiss unless both
help. Nobody could possibly tell exactly
where your kiss ended or mine began.”
“I didn’t want to,” I reminded Babette;
My wife sighed happily. “Oh, isn’t it
mvely t 9 be able to confess like this. It’s
ike taking off a new corset. There’s such
sense of relief in just letting vourself
mow over.”
“Well,” I spurred her, “while you’re con-
fessing, you might tell me the truth about
John. I was frightfully jealous of John.”
“Oh, John!” Babette paused. I had a
dim suspicion that she was sorting otit the
things that she might tell me about John
and the things that it would only worry me
to know. “Oh, I only used John to shake
you up. You were so slow. You don’t know
how self-sacrificing a girl has to be if she
wants to get married. I wasted three-weeks
over John —and, except when he’s proposing,
we haven’t a single subject in common.
Luckily, he was always proposing, so I
wasn’t very bored.”
“If it hadn’t been for John,” I mused, “I
wonder. . . . ?”
“I often wonder, too,” said Babette sim-
ply. “But I couldn’t have been so happy.”
Having given her the usual kiss, and got
from her a quite unusual kiss, I went on.
“But everything wasn’t pretence. That
night I proposed!”
“Did you?” Babette sedately asked.
“Of course—otherwise it’s hardly moral
for you to be here.”
“But, tell me, dear, frankly. You didn’t mean
to propose that particular night, did you?”
“Of course, I arranged it all.”
“Really!” Babette seemed surprised.
“You see, I had made up my mind that
that sort of thing couldn’t go on for ever.”
“What sort of thing?”
“Cuddling you for hours every second
evening.”
“I often wished it would,” Babette sighed.
“But both Kitty and I agreed that it was
just a phase, pleasant to linger over, but a
waste of time unless it led to business. Kitty
and I often talked it over. You see, there
are limits to the area of a nice girl a young
man is allowed to kiss, and sometimes it
seemed to me that you had reached those
limits.”
I dared not hint that sometimes it had
seemed to me —afterwards —that different
interpretations might have been put by other
people upon the legal limits.
Babette, perhaps, had the same thought.
She sighed reminiscently. “I used to powder
inches lowey those evenings. But it wasn’t
wrong, surely?”
“Nice, 7 but not naughty,” I agreed.
“I told Kitty that it was air right, quite
conventional, in fact, if you were going to
marry me* Kitty said that that was just
the point. Were you, or weren’t you? She
said that- you had ' seen enough of me to
decide. You know you did see a good deal
of me, didn’t you? And I liked you to.
Shameless of ine, wasn’t it? But I’m
married' now, and can tell the truth. So
Kitty and I arranged that you’d propose to
me that week. We fixed upon the night you
were going to take me to the concert.”
“That night?” I gasped.
“Well, it came off all right,” Babette
laughed. “Everything went swimmingly,
didn’t: it?”
“But,” I sternly asked, “let us be quite
clear. You mean to say that you arranged
for me to propose that night?”
“Of course —but Kitty helped with sug-
gestions. That was why I wanted to.know
whether you had fixed on that night, inde-
pendently, too? Or whether any old night
would have suited you as we 11.... or
whether you hadn’t decided to propose at all ?”
“I meant to propose that night,” I sternly
asserted. “I arranged everything.”
“What a coincidence!” Babette cried.
“Fancy the whole three of us fixing on the
same night ! You’re right, there is a pur-
pose in this world. But what a lot of worry
it would have saved Kitty and me if we’d
only known! And how it would have re-
lieved you, if you’d only known! So much
pretence in love, isn’t there? And fancy
you picking out the same tree to pro-
pose under that Kitty and I had selected ! It
makes you almost believe in the God of Love.”
“Yes,” I said savagely. “But how would
you have got me to propose if I hadn’t sug-
gested our going under that tree? I might
not have stopped. I might just have taken
you straight to the concert.’
“Oh, Kitty and I arranged all that. Don’t
you remember my shoelace had come undone,
and me asking you to tie it up—though I
certainly didn’t ask you to smooth my stock-
ing while you were about it. I had carefully
untied that lace before we started. And
then....and then I pretended that I was
shocked, and made you apologise. A girl
can always manage a man so much more
easily when he’s humble. And I got angry,
and said I’d go straight home if a man
wasn’t fit to be trusted with a nice girl on
a moonlight night in such a lonely spot as
that, with no possible chance of anybody
interrupting them. And I pointed out
—that shows how angry I was —that even
if you prevented me from going to the con-
cert at all, nobody would know we hadn’t
been, that practically I was in your power
all the evening—which was all the more
reason for your behaving yourself and tak-
ing no advantage of a trusting girl. And,
then, just to show how much I could trust
you—just to shame you—l kissed you back
in a sisterly way. And then I sat down on
the bank, and when you pointed out that
the grass might be wet I had to sit on your
knee, hadn’t I? I had tried that on with
another man once before, so that I knew it
would work.”
“Oh, it worked all right,” I said miserably.
“And then, as things were getting rather
slow—and we only had two and a half hours
to ourselves—l managed to quarrel with you
again.”
“What was the quarrel about?” I wanted
to know.
“I quite forget. All I know was that
every time you made it up you got nicer and
more manageable. And then, suddenly, I
said to myself, ‘Time’s up !’and found myself
in your aiyns. And you blurted It out. I
won a box . of chocolates from Kitty that
night. She had bet me it wouldn’t happen
for another week.”
“But you really were surprised,” I insisted.
“I was astonished. It was so easy. I
coukL have managed it a month earlier. I
had wasted, a whole month.”
“But why didn’t you accept me definitely
that night?” I asked.
“And get done with all the excitement in
one evening? I wanted it all over again the
next night.” *
“Well, you got it, Babette.” My innocent
Babette! “Well, as you have been so frank,
I don’t mind being frank, too. There’s a
thing I often wanted to tell you. Immedi-
ately after I had proposed—l mean, just
after you had proposed—for one moment I
was afraid. I had done it! And I felt a
sickening trickle of fear right down my
spine. I had taken the great jump—and I
hadn’t the least idea where I was going to
land, or how far down the bottom was. Any-
how. I couldn’t get back. And I comforted
myself by that thought—that, anyhow, I
couldn’t get back.”
“Did you really feel like that?” Babette
curiously asked. “So did I.”
“And here we are,” she added happily
and sleepily. “It wasn’t a bad sort of place
we landed on, either this double-bed.”
HENRY JAMES JAMES.
Randolph Bedford: “Henri Singemoine”
takes me to task for citing W. H. Kelly as
“a terrible example of a little man who
didn’t have to work for a living.” Knowing
my own meaning, which “11.5.” will not see,
I take back not a word of it. W.K.’s de-
meanor in Parliament, his insulting affecta-
tion of superiority over the more or less
working men of the other side, prove his
“terrible example”-ness. But everything is
comparative, and I did not say that W.HJv.
is not better than a dipsomaniacal remit-
tance man, or better than the rich and rene-
gade Australians who live in London and
run down Australia as a mistaken means
of currying favor with their British
acquaintances, or the trust makers, or the
rough house specialists, or the police bribers.
He is a much better man than all these;
but that doesn’t affect his tremendous airs
of superiority to other politicians.
“Y.Z.” writes from Melbourne: —
The war has produced a few cases of curious funk
in our vicinity. Opposite me lived two spinsters of
the middle-ages, sisters, of Irish extraction, heiresses
to a fortune their hairy Kerry parent accumulated
from Cow. When the scare first hit this State, and
many people were expecting hunches of primitive
Prussians armed with siege guns to invade their
bedrooms at night, these afflicted twin sisters did
their most important valuables up in bales and took
to the hills out from Morwell. But before going, the
wise virgins instructed a dull mechanic to put up a
sign in the front garden before the deserted home.
The faithful henchman complied, and the sign' is
there now. The notice is in a foreign tongue. The
other day I took a transcript of it, and invited my
friend Pfaffstein to impart its significance. It trans-
lates : “Trespassers Will Be Prosecuted. Beware of
the Dog!” and is done in German!
Another interesting item is a small, sallow subject'
who haunts the St. Kilda foreshore, or perches out on
the Red Bluff, with a pair of opera glasses, greedily
inspecting the bay. He is rather a shabby unit. His"
boots look as if they had -been shelled by a destroyer
and had foundered with all on board. Apparently he'
has nothing to do but sit on an eminence out in the
open and scan the horizon minutely from early morn
till dewy eve. His opera-glasses are small, and of
no particular use in research work, but the im-
material object has great faith in them. I thought
he was studying the European situation, and made
first-hand inquiries. “What'in I doin’?” he said.
“Why, I’m a-lookiri fer the Germans. They don’t
catch me nappin’, When. I see ’em with these I
signals to the missus to run and draw me money.”
He showed me his signalling apparatus. It was a
Chinese cracker 1

“Ilamer” : When the war became such an
engrossing topic that even divorce cases went
unreported, the legion of lines-men on the
various daily papers went clean out. Very
few of them are men of providence (most
of them have very little to be provident
upon), and the rejection of their screeds
hit them immediately. They had the satis-
faction, however, of reading in the papers
for which they usually wrote urgent appeals
to the patriotism of employers to refrain
from placing men out of work. The incon-
sistency of the daily newspaper owners
didn’t stop merely at the neglect of these
men. One of the Sydney dailies put off,
while its appeal to employers was in full
blast, 25 persons employed in vari-
ous departments. lint it was the advertising
men who suffered most. Advertising fell
down heavily, and for about the first time
on record the journalist felt no cause to envy
the man on the business side of newspaper
work.
* * * #
“Ivaniva” : The weekly paper which was to
wake things up in Melbourne, the Victorian,
has fizzled out. At the start it had very little
to recommend it, and its progress did not
fulfil expectations. Whether the biggest
trouble was want of capital, or want of
brains, or want of push, or just plain, bad
luck, makes no difference to the tombstone.
When a paper is dead it matters very little
what it died of.
“Plattypussy”: Among the first to feel
the effects of the war were the Maoriland
newspaper companies. These concerns, sud-
denly found themselves confronted with a
shortage of the material with which the
meat is enveloped. If the trade routes to
Canada are blocked, the American stories
printed on Canadian paper, which are the
“Christmas Numbers” of the patriotic
printeries of Maoriland, will be off this
year. It is amusing to see an illustration
in one recent weekly representing a Maori-
land paper mill. It was printed on the
imported material which the aforesaid mills
are capable of producing themselves. What’s
the matter with the proprietors of M.L.
papers supporting local industry in the
paper-making business. Also they could, at
a pinch, patronise the local literature-pro-
ducing business.
* * * *
“Map of Europe” : Where are all the news-
paper military experts comine from? Two
or three years ago, Australia took only the
most .perfunctory interest in defence, and
military or naval news of any kind was a
sort of curiosity. A smart man might have
searched for a week with bloodhounds and
ferrets without scaring out half a
dozen pressmen with special knowledge
of war. Now every rag is full of
chatter—-not to say gabble—about fleets,
frontiers, gunnery, army corps, tactics
and the rest of it. Every newspaper
man feels a bit of a humbug now and again—-
it is one of the penalties of pretending to
know everything; but these office Moltkes
must feel uneasy in their consciences about
six hours a day.
* * * *
“Pick”: At Braidwood (N.S.W.) the
other week there passed out at the age of
82, John Musgrave, who had something of
a record on the Inky Way. Far back in
the ’so’s he set up the Braidwood
Despatch, when the palmy goldfield days
gave to that town less of a decayed appear-
ance than it wears nowadays, and through
varying fortunes he stood at its editorial
wheel until the old pirate with the scythe
cut him down. An early training on some
of the Sydney dailies was, perhaps, respon-
sible for the musty traditions of Toryism
that usually pervaded his office.
* * * *
“Albion” : Some of Australia’s struggling
country newspaper proprietors take firewood,
horsefeed, goats, groceries, etc., in payment
for ads. or papers. Once I happened to be
piloting a sheet in Western Queensland, and
received a note from one of our esteemed sup-
porters, who informed me that he had “ten
bob” coming to him at the Shire office for
some dingo scalps, and authorising me to
collect the amount for his annual subscrip-
tion. I reckon that’s the limit.
* * * *
“8.J.H.0.” : Buying and selling of news-
papers has been proceeding briskly in the
Cold Country. Millionaire Astor (William
Waldorf of that ilk) has lately sold both the
Pall Mall Gazette and the Observer to
Gardiner Sinclair, the Edinburgh printing
magnate. Under the editorship of the ener-
getic J. L. Garvin, the P.M.G., which has
always skited that it is run by gents, for
gents., is the most powerful Tory evening
sheet in London. The Observer, the oldest
Sawbath journal in England, was bought by
Astor from Northcliffe only about three
years ago. The ex-American (Wm. Waldorf
is a naturalised Britisher these times) has
disposed of the Pall Mall Magazine,
which has been swallowed by Nash’s. Also
that somewhat sad weekly, the World,
which Will Dyson did his best to brighten
with colored cartoons, has been transferred
to the shoulders of Fenton, of the Pink ’TJn,
who should at least make a more sprightly
Atlas than his predecessor.
LARGER TYPE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
As I the old familiar columns view,
In which the mummer critics weekly revel,
I gasp! Upon my sock and buskin it is true—-
Not an invention of the printer's devil!
A wondrous change I note—the time was ripe—
“ Poverty Point” appears in larger type!
The microscopic text no more I'll scan
That chronicles my every little habit —
My movements e'en from Beersheba to Dan,
And when I take a car, and when I cab it,
And how some plays were “great” and others
, “tripe”—■
“Poverty Point” appears in larger type!
Small print no more will make my optics smart.
What though pert paragraphists scorn and rend me,
More space devoted to me and my Art
Should —looked on as the usual ad. —befriend me!
So no regretful tear away l wipe—
You can’t insult a mummer with large type!
VICTORIA.
JEUNE PREMIER.

The Corpse that Won’t Lie Still.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A YE, call it murder if ye will!
’Tis not the crime I fear.
If his cold corse would but lie still
And silent in its bier,
Then would I be indeed content,
And count it folly to repent.
With these two hands I’ve slain the knave;
I’ve watched the red blood drop ;
I’ve rammed him tight into his grave,
And piled the clods atop,
And tramped them down exultingly....
Now back he comes to grin at me.
Once have I slain him in his bed,
Twice by the midnight blaze;
Thrice have I looked upon him dead
All in these seven days.
Yet here, this night, I’ve seen him stand
And pluck the pen from out my hand.
Nay, never spook nor sprite is he,
But solid flesh and blood,
Who schemes with deep malignity
To stint my livelihood.
And he has vowed a vow my name
Shall never grace the scroll of fame.
My name he bears, my garb he wears,
My pipes he idly smokes ;
And, friend-like, he but rarely cares
To praise my sorry jokes.
He spends my money lavishly
With ne’er a thrifty thought for me.
And when my ready'cash is gone
He runs me into debt.
Stern duty he will harp upon
When I would fain forget.
But when, through toil, I would be free
He soothes me with rank sophistry.
Whene’er with resolutions stern
I sit me down to work.
And mighty thoughts within me burn,
Then forth comes he to lurk
Here at my elbow, where he clings
And whispers of forbidden things.
So when I woo some lofty theme
Of deep religious tone,
He lures me on to idly dream,
As we sit there alone,
Of girls I have and have not kissed,
Of favors won and chances missed.
He whispers of that tempting book
I have no time to read ;
“One peep,” he pleads ; “one hasty look!
Where is the harm, indeed?”
And when I speak of work, and sigh,
“’Twill do to-morrow!” is his cry.
And oft —too well I know how oft—
Beneath his subtle spell
I fall, and dream of living soft
Who know —aye, none so well —
That living soft is but for him
Who earns his ease with labor grim.
Dreams, dreams, and ever idle dreams!
His glozing art I hate ! _
Yet pleasant for the hour it seems,
His soothing opiate.
And, though I slay .him, this I dread :
He’s oftener alive than dead.
Oh, I had been so very sure,
No later than last night,
That I had pinned the knave secure,
And I was free to write
Those mighty masterpieces which
To pen my fingers ever itch.
But, with his slouch and lazy leer,
Lo, back came he to-day :
With wheedling lips against mine ear
He tempted me to play
At tennis all the afternoon.
Work and resolve forgot so soon !
Yet, spite his faults, he is, I swear,
A merry knave withal;
And when I have the time to spare—
That’s seldom, if at all —
I’d roam with him ’mid fields and flow’rs
If he’d be still in business hours.
Each morn I bash him on the head
And hide him out of sight.
Full sure, indeed, that he is dead ;
But back he comes each night,
And on the lotus buds we feed
When bread and butter is my need.
Though many ways his death I’ve planned
And slain him. as I’ve said.
He takes a lot of killing and
He’ll never stay long dead.
And. though each day I cause his death,
I know he’ll live while I have breath.
But let me vow the vow again—
The vow I know by heart —
And. here and now, with hasty pen.
Stab to some vital part.
And. mocked by his departing laugh,
Rewrite his oft-writ epitaph.
“Here lies the man I should not be
By all stern rules of life.
The man who’s plagued and hampered me
All through this mundane strife.
A lazy, loaflne knave was he. . . .
But, sooth, he was fine company.”
VICTORIA.
DEN.
How to End the War.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
"TVT VPOLEON TOSSPOT sat at the
I small round table at the end of the
bar. Before him was a half-dis-
charged “schooner,” and Napoleon gazed at
it thoughtfully over the bowl of his briar.
I sat down beside him and commenced to
sip my bovril, and between sips remarked :
“How’s the war going?”
“War —there is no war yet!” he snorted.
“Mere affairs of outposts. The Germans
are doing rottenly. Three weeks on the job
and only open towns captured. Not a soli-
tary fortress taken. Hardly a German
soldier across the French frontier. And
Heaven knows how many Roosians *warin-
ing over the eastern frontier.
“I’ve been thinking about this war. The
problem the German has to solve is the
tucker supply. Now, always go for the
enemy’s bread-basket if you can. What
Britain should do now is engage three million
Chows. Arm ’em with waddies or any old
thing and land ’em by the hundred thousand
on any part of the German coast, where
they would, of course, surrender without
firing a shot.”
“What good would that do?” I inquired
wonderingly. “It would give the Kaiser
more food for his victorious journalism.”
“You’re no military genius,” said Napo-
leon Tosspot scornfully. “Can’t you see
that the Germans would have to feed ’em.
Also, they’d have to detach a lot of troops
to watch ’em. But it’s the tucker that
counts. Three million more to feed would
be the very devil. Then send along a fourth
million, and if necessary a fifth and sixth.”
“I’m afraid these brutal Germans would
shoot the lot!” I said, shaking my head
sadly. . .
“Well, it would make a hole in their
ammunition, for you bet some of those
Chinks would put up a good sprint. And
the burning or burying of millions of Pongs
would make the Germans beastly tired —•
break ’em up, in fact. I’m going to write to
Millen about it, anyway !”
“It might appeal to him, after all. Have
another ‘schooner’?”
“One more won’t hurt. Of course, there’s
another way it might be done. This war is
going to cost the Allies about five millions
per day. Well, offer the war expenses of
a week, £35,000,000, as a reward for the
heads of the entire German General Staff,
from the Kaiser downwards. They’d be
delivered in a week. That is, all that the
troops could catch. There’d be such a
stampede of officers to the French and Bel-
gian armies for protection that the unani-
mous movement might be mistaken for a
general advance. But the speed of the
stampede would make the Allies wise.”
“It’s a horrible idea—but it might work.
Are you going to put it to Millen, too?”
“My oath. He’ll get a peerage if he adopts
it and they take it on. Nothing surer! The
war will end the week the German army
gets the news.”
“That ought to make him jump at the
idea!” I said thoughtfully. “But it seems
to me that the difficulty would be to get
the news circulated in the German regi-
ments.”# , -
“The French and British have nearly 30U
aeroplanes. Bet ’em all loose distributing
the circulars right along the frontier. See
that they were dropped in every quarter
where the Social Purity Society has a
branch, and the old tabbies will do the
rest.”
Napoleon looked into his “schooner,”
which was empty. He seemed surprised.
Having obtained a fresh cargo for that ves-
sel he went on :
“There’s a bloke down in Woolloomooloo
who has invented a new and wonnerful
motive power for aeroplanes: You see,
there is no limit to aerial flight as long
as the engine can be kept going. Of course
you know that heat is a manifestation _ of
solar energy, and light is a manifestation
of heat. Do you get me? Well, his appli-
ance abstracts from the daylight sufficient
power to drive the aeroplane. It is not
quite perfect, but he hopes to improve it
so that with an auxiliary appliance lie can
collect a reserve stock which will drive the
aeroplane at night as well. At present the
machine must come down about three hours
after sunset. It works with the moonlight,
but it cannot accumulate enough to be
effective. He is pretty certain to perfect it
in the course of a week or so. And when
he does the British Government will be able
to fit out a squadron of huge aeroplanes,
each capable of carrying 50 or 60 men.
These sky-vachts can keep up as long as
the tucker lasts. Their business will be to
seek out the Kaiser and his staff, and kill
’em. After that all the general officers com-
manding. And as fresh ones are appointed
they must be promptly killed off. It’s the
old idea of the Assassins brought up to
d&tG ”
“Are you going to send it to Millen, too?”
“Nope! I’m going to send that to Kit-
chener direct. Millen’s going to. have his
chance with two good ideas, and if he can’t
cotton to one of them I’ve no more time for
him.”
“You’ll give him a very fair opportunity.
I think !” I said admiringly.
“I think so!” he replied thoughtfully.
He blew the froth from the bulwarks of
the “schooner” and looked at me .with gleam-
ing eyes, wiped his mouth, relit his pipe,
and went on :
“There’s another idea which I rather
shrink from. The enemy is so utterly help-
less against it that I hate to think of it.
But if the Allies should be in real danger I
shall have to throw compunction to the
winds. You know these people that are
called “disease carriers”- —individuals who,
while immune themselves, carry germs to
others? Yes, of course. Well, in some
parts of China there are coves who
can manufacture these “carriers,” and they
do it. for a price. And when you want to
get rid of an enemy you hire someone, gener-
ally a nice young girl, make her a carrier,
load her up with the particular disease you
want to hit your friend with, and then
entice one of his housemaids to leave. Have
the carrier on hand so that she will be
engaged for the vacant position—and the
germ does the rest.”
“What particular germ did you propose to
use —cholera ?”
“Cholera be Mowed. Too easily cured
with common cooking salt these days. .1
propose to send ’em out with a good, reli-
able disease —anthrax. No one knows how
to cure that. These carriers would be
located along the frontier as waitresses,
cooks, etc- Of course all neutrals would be
warned off. and all the people in any estab-
lishment to bo used for the purpose would
be carriers. What puts me off it is that,
although anyone can be made a carrier, a
carrier can never be unmade. He or she
goes on for ever.”
“Don’t do it —don’t do it!” I begged of
him.
“I won’t —unless the Empire is in danger.
If that happens T’ll see red. and Heaven
only knows what I’ll do. Don’t make me
think of it-—Mease don’t!” he expostulated,
ducting the froth from his waistcoat and
drifting out in a profound reverie.
C. A. JEFFRIES.

IN Melbourne, excellently well situated
between a lugubrious blue church and
a tombstone yard full of granite
angels, is all that piece or parcel of land
containing 2 roods 3 perches, and bearing
the gloomy double front, the long, rambling
dining-room and the asphalt yard of the
Cafe Elf. The scenery in the yard is of
asphalt and bricks, with a gate R.U.E. and
a large, old weepful willow tree left centre,
and a hit up stage. This willow tree has an
intermission of ten minutes in its trunk,
and the frugal Italian holders have wiped
out the invading pest by filling the rift with
cement as they keep those 500-year-old
olive trees alive with cement and bands of
iron on the road to Monte Amiata, first
turning after Archidosso, between the ceme-
tery and Santa Fiora walls.
There is a pianola there and a piano, a
mandolin with a black moustached Spanish
attachment, and a very plump, very black-
eyed. ever-laughing woman who uses the
mandolin’s “tink-a-tank” as accompaniment.
Minnie and Winnie, eight Italians, four
Australians, a Servian, a librarian and an
oil merchant made up the party the other
night. The librarian was interested in war
literature. One of the Australians —by name
Oscar was benevolently drunk, so that he
ordered wine for all men, friends, strangers
i\ u< l u 5 -1 £ inis , carefully ignoring the fact
that his funds were limited to one and four-
pence and a rupee.
lie was a hard-faced man with a per-
petual and good-humored grin which did not
leave Ins face even when lie passed deadly
insults to tlie fish-oil merchant who, in the
East End of London, had been a great flag-
flapping patriot, and had killed Kruger sev-
eral times by tearing the English language
to pieces with liis loose mouth. This patent
sneak, slant-eyed as a Pong, found his mar-
vellous dignity stricken to death early in the
meal. In a new accession of patriotism he
had requested the company to sing the Mar-
seillaise, and in the event Oscar had ruled
him out of order for that he had sung the
immortal music of liouget de Lisle with the
well-known English translation of the words
which are:-
Rn ! Ra ! R a ! Ra ! Ra ! Ra ! Ra ! Ra !

First the muzzle-loading guns; then the single-
shot breech-loaders; then the repeating arms, of
which there are a number of makes and styles.
In repeating arms several important fea-
tures must be considered, such as rapidity
of firing without loss of aim, ejection of
empty cartridges, safety, beauty and
durability. It is because of these
essential requirements that we
ask you to examine a Rem-

IT HAS
Medicinal
Virtues
The
Importance
of*
Efficient
Insulation!
The importance of efficient insulation, in many cases, is not realised. Great
care is shown in the selection of a steam plant to ensure the greatest eccuomy,
yet the insulation which is of equal importance is in many cases partly neg-

ALL BRITISH MANUFACTURE.
“MIRIS " STEEL is Manufactured at Perseverance Works, SHEFFIELD.
SOriE ADVANTAGES OF “MIRIS” STEEL—
1— By reason of the ratio of its elastic limit and elongation to its ultimate breaking strain, “MIRIS” STEEL will satisfy specifications where Nickel
or Chrome Steels, involving a great increase in cost, have hitherto been used.
2 “MIRIS” STEEL can he annealed dead soft WITHOUT LOSS OF ULTIMATE HARDNESS.
3 “MIRIS” STEEL welds to itself in a smith’s fire as readily as iron to iron.
4 “MIRIS” STEEL is a pure carbon steel, and is invariably homogeneous and uniform in structure.
Suitable for Lathe Centres, Axles, Railway Work, Tools, etc., and can be supplied in Ingots, Billets, Slabs,
Rods, Bars, Plates, Strips or Wire of any Carbon from 0.10 to 1.10.
